| :      | capture                 |
| !      | good move               |
| !!     | excellent move          |
| ?      | bad move or false trail |

## FROM THE AUTHOR

I am often asked how I started composing chess problems? Naturally, first of all, I learned to play chess. Then I became the champion of the school, the aeroclub, the battalion, the college, etc. At the same time, I engaged in solving problems and studies. I composed my first problem after reading V. N. Panov's book "Chess for Beginners". I sent it to the contest of the newspaper "Young Leninist" and received a response that my "work" had several side solutions (of which I was unaware), but the idea was interesting. "Keep working," encouraged the editorial office.

The beauty of chess combinations, the depth of problem ideas became apparent after careful study of the collection "Chess Problem". Alongside this, I became convinced that a composer's skill would improve faster the better one solves problems and studies: their analysis and review would immediately reflect on the acceleration of technical improvement of a novice chess composer.

To learn how to compose chess problems and studies, one must:

1. Be a qualified practical chess player. This will help a novice composer to identify defects in composition and quickly correct them.
2. Be able to solve and analyze chess problems and studies.
3. Know the mandatory and artistic requirements of chess composition, as well as the aesthetic and ethical norms of creating a chess work.
4. Study the thematic content of chess composition problems and studies.
5. Possess sufficient technical skills in composition, as it is well known that technical mastery allows one to realize the most complex ideas, and high technical art is the ability to find the optimal scheme for the economical implementation of an idea.

A turning point in the life of a chess composer comes when, having been nurtured by the creativity of others, he begins to educate chess enthusiasts with his own creativity.

I must admit, the suggestion to write a book on composing chess problems and studies made me think. After all, if the questions of solving chess compositions are already largely covered in the literature (for example, in the books "How to Solve Chess Problems" by A. P. Gulyaev, "How to Solve Chess Studies" by Yu. L. Averbakh), then there is little material on their composition.

On the topic of composing studies, I was able to find only the article "On Study Composition" in the book by A. O. Herbstman "Selected Chess Studies" and the article by Grandmaster V. A. Korolkov "Technology of the Chess Study", published in the official journal of the Permanent Commission on Chess Composition under FIDE "Problems" in February 1968. Already during the preparation of the manuscript for publication, I acquired the excellent book by International Grandmaster G. M. Kasparyan "Secrets of the Study Composer".

For composing problems, I only had the article by International Grandmaster L. I. Loshinsky "Tournament-Contest of Three-Move Problems" from the collection "Chess Problem" (1951). And only in the process of working on this book did I have the good fortune to encounter the work of another well-known Grandmaster V. F. Rudenko — "Pursuit of the Theme" (1983), in which the material of interest to me is placed in the first part "Chess Composition".

The above-mentioned and other works helped me in writing this book, and the comments from them, which are of great interest to a novice chess composer, are almost completely or partially preserved by us in the original with appropriate references to the authorship.

The book, which incorporates information from the history of the development of chess composition, formal and artistic requirements for it, its themes and techniques, is essentially the first manual for those who want to learn how to compose chess problems and studies.

The book presents the main and most relevant themes. Only typical examples are given to illustrate them, understandable to those taking their first steps in chess composition. The author aimed for a popular presentation of the material, accessible to all chess enthusiasts.

The book does not claim to be exhaustive. Even by continuously diving into the depths of Oksana, it is impossible to extract all the pearls. It is assumed that some readers may have the opinion that certain problems or studies should be placed in a different thematic section. However, many compositions are so multi-idea in content that they can be considered from different creative aspects with equal justification.

If my book in some way helps a novice chess composer to pave a path into the poetry of chess, I will consider that I have accomplished my task.

## COMPOSITION — THE POETRY OF CHESS

Chess composition, that is, the creation of chess problems and studies, is based on the means and rules of practical play and represents a peculiar area of creativity. It reveals the beauty of chess combinations, constantly evolving and attracting new enthusiasts of the wise game.

Composition is figuratively called the poetry of chess. It reflects the practice of chess play in the same way that art reflects real life. A true chess composer makes the pieces do the maximum possible on the chessboard, reveals their yet undiscovered power, and comprehensively uses the laws and rules of this ancient game. These creative traits are fully inherent in Soviet chess composers. By mastering and developing the best traditions of all schools and directions, they simultaneously strive to effectively combine innovation with aesthetic principles and simple methods of expressing ideas.

The emergence of chess composition is traced back to the turn of the 8th-9th centuries, when the first attempts were made to compose mansub ("mansuba" — literally from Arabic means "that which was erected, established, arranged", that is, in our understanding — composed). The characteristic feature of mansub was, first of all, its closeness to practical play. From a modern point of view, they appear naive in content, easy to solve, and primitive in construction, with forced play, aimed solely at external effect.

**№ 1. Dilaram Problem, Istanbul Manuscript, 1140, Mate in 5 moves**  
![№ 1. Dilaram Problem, Istanbul Manuscript, 1140, Mate in 5 moves](./diagrams/diagram0001.png)

Perhaps one of the earliest mansubs, and without doubt the most famous, was the Arabic problem (diagram № 1) with the poetic title "Dilaram's Mate." The following legend explains this name.

A certain nobleman, a passionate chess player and extraordinarily confident in his talent, lost all his wealth and, driven by gambling fever, made a final wager that he would still defeat his opponent. As his last stake, he pledged his beloved wife, the beautiful Dilaram.

But luck seemed to abandon him completely, and the game reached a position where White's king was about to be mated. In despair, the nobleman searched for a way to save himself and was already ready to resign. At the very last moment, Dilaram happened to glance at the board and whispered to her unfortunate husband: "Sacrifice both rooks and save me!"

He had nothing to lose, and White obediently played **1. Rh8+! Kxh8 2. Bf5+** (under the rules of that time, bishops - alfil pieces - could move only by leaping two squares diagonally, but they could jump over other pieces) **2... Kg8**.

The position seemed to return to its starting point, but then came **3. Rh8+ Kxh8 4. g7+ Kg8 5. Nh6#**. Everything was saved...

Of course, no one can confirm the authenticity of this legend. Otherwise, chess history would have full grounds to recognize the beautiful Dilaram as a pioneer of women's chess composition. But in another sense, this story is quite credible: it suggests that mansubs were not endings of actually played games, but specially composed positions.

**№ 2. Abu-Naim, 9th Century, Mate in 3 Moves**  
![№ 2. Abu-Naim, 9th Century, Mate in 3 Moves](./diagrams/diagram0002.png)

This mansub (№ 2) was composed almost twelve hundred years ago and found in the old Arabic manuscripts of Al-Adli (9th century): **1. Nh5+! Rxh5 2. Rxg6+ Kxg6 3. Re6#**.

Chess composition is divided into orthodox, non-orthodox, fairy, and special types of composition. Orthodox composition is based entirely on chess goals (mate against the king), material (board and pieces), and means (the rules of play).

Looking ahead, we should note that the Soviet school of chess composition developed as an integral part of the broader chess movement, and its principal direction is orthodox composition. Therefore, in what follows, we will consider mainly the orthodox field of chess composition.

By stipulation, two kinds of composition are distinguished: problems and studies.

A chess problem is an artificial board position in which one side (as a rule, White) mates the other side (Black) in a specified number of moves. Therefore, in problems, neither the number of pieces nor the relative strength of the sides is the decisive factor. Black may have only a king, while White may have a full army, yet this alone does not predetermine White's success. The main task is to find the concealed way to mate the opposing king in the required number of moves.

A chess study is also an artificial position, but unlike a problem, it is closer to a practical game ending. White moves first in a study (unless the stipulation states that Black moves first), but the task is not mate in an exact number of moves; rather, it is to win (gain a decisive advantage) or to draw. The number of moves, unlike in problems, is not fixed. Higher demands are placed on the initial setup of a study: it should resemble as closely as possible a position from a real played game.

Solving a study is much harder than solving a problem. A good study is usually highly individual, and finding its solution requires both theoretical knowledge and creative intuition. Compared with analyzing a position from a game, solving a study is easier in one respect: in practical play, the chances of both sides are not always clear, whereas in a study White has a mathematically precise and unavoidable path, against all defenses (though often the only one), to a win or a draw.

**№ 3. "Civis Bononiae," 13th Century, Mate in 2 Moves**  
![№ 3. "Civis Bononiae," 13th Century, Mate in 2 Moves](./diagrams/diagram0003.png)

The specific nature of the study becomes clearer from the history of its development, traceable back to the Middle Ages. Its chess heritage has reached us through two Italian manuscripts, "Bonus Socius" and "Civis Bononiae." Almost all compositions in these collections had solutions ending in mate, which means that by our classification they should be treated as problems. Diagram № 3 shows one of them: **1. Rf7+ Kxf7 2. Ng6#**.

**№ 4. "Civis Bononiae," 14th Century, Win**  
![№ 4. "Civis Bononiae," 14th Century, Win](./diagrams/diagram0004.png)

It is therefore not surprising that, while leafing through these two encyclopedic collections, one finds only a single study (№ 4), though it became a classic: **1. Rh5!! Rxh5 2. Ra6+ Kc5 3. Ra5+ and 4. Rxh5**, winning.

After the appearance of "Bonus Socius" and "Civis Bononiae," another two centuries passed with almost no changes in composition. European problems were essentially the same mansubs under a different name. So it may be said that mansubs survived almost until the middle of the 19th century, when they were clearly and formally divided into problems and studies. True, already in the treatise of H. R. Lucena (15th century), one can find positions that are genuinely study-like even by modern standards, both in construction and in solution.

**№ 5. H. R. Lucena, 15th Century, Win**  
![№ 5. H. R. Lucena, 15th Century, Win](./diagrams/diagram0005.png)

№ 5. White win thanks to the precise choice of the opening move: **1. Rd4!** (Only this!) **1...Ra2 2. Kc7 Rc2+ 3. Kb6 Rb2+ 4. Kc6 Rc2+ 5. Kb5 Rh2 6. Rh4**, and win.

**№ 6. Damiano, 16th Century, Win**  
![№ 6. Damiano, 16th Century, Win](./diagrams/diagram0006.png)

The beginning of the 16th century was marked by the appearance of the second printed work setting out the new rules of play. The treatise was titled "This Book Teaches How to Play Chess and Contains Problems." Its author was the Portuguese apothecary Damiano. Here is one composition (№ 6) from that treatise: **1. c5 h4 2. c6 h3 3. c7 h2 4. c8Q h1Q 5. Qg8+ Kh6 6. Qh8+**, winning.

The beginning of the next century, the 17th, is associated with the name of G. Polerio, considered the founder of the Italian chess school, and with his followers P. Carrera and A. Salvio.

**№ 7. A. Salvio, 1634, Mate in 4 Moves**  
![№ 7. A. Salvio, 1634, Mate in 4 Moves](./diagrams/diagram0007.png)

Diagram № 7 shows a problem with an unusual stipulation: White to move and mate in 4; Black to move and also get mated, but in 8 moves. A: **1. Nf6 Kh1 2. Ng4 h2! 3. Kf1 g5 4. Nf2#** and B: **1...Kh1 2. Nf6 Kh2 3. Ng4+ Kh1 4. Kf1 g5 5. Kf2 h2 6. Ne3 g4 7. Nf1 g3+ 8. Nxg3#**.

Problems with endgame-like material existed even before Salvio, but he was the one who clearly emphasized their closeness to practical endgames.

In the first half of the 18th century, F. Stamma became widely known. Despite mixed evaluations of his work, Stamma's importance for the development of composition is enormous: the eastern mansuba was revived and found a second life.

**№ 8. F. Stamma, 1737, Win**  
![№ 8. F. Stamma, 1737, Win](./diagrams/diagram0008.png)

№ 8. A combinational sacrifice is carried out: **1. Rc1! Rxc1 2. a7 Ka3 3. Kc3! Ka2 4. a8Q+ Kb1 5. Qa3**, with inevitable **6. Qb2#**.

The founder of French comic opera, Francois-Andre Danican Philidor, became even more famous as a chess player. In 1749 he published *Analysis of the Game of Chess*. No chess book had yet enjoyed such a fate: over the following 200 years it was published more than one hundred times.

**№ 9. F. Philidor, 1749, Win**  
![№ 9. F. Philidor, 1749, Win](./diagrams/diagram0009.png)

In position № 9, which had been considered drawn, Philidor found a line leading White to victory: **1. Rf8+ Re8 2. Rf7 Re2 3. Rh7 Re3 4. Rd7+ Ke8 5. Ra7 Kf8 6. Rf7+ Ke8 7. Rf4! Kd8 8. Be4! Ke8 9. Bg6 Kd8 10. Rf8+**, and White wins.

**№ 10. D. Lolli, 1763, Win**  
![№ 10. D. Lolli, 1763, Win](./diagrams/diagram0010.png)

Diagram № 10 presents a quartet study (that is, a four-piece study), included in the third part of D. Lolli's book *Observations on the Theory and Practice of Chess*. Its solution is simple but memorable: **1. Qb3 Kd2 2. Qb2 Kd1 3. Kf3! Kd2 4. Kf2 Kd1 5. Qd4+**, and White wins.

The next four-mover - White: **Kd8, Qa7, Rf5, Rh7, Bb1, pawns a3, b2, e5** (8); Black: **Kg8, Qb5, Rg4, Rg5, Ba4, pawns c5, d5, f6, g7** (9) - by an unknown author, appears in one of the Turkish manuscripts: **1. Qf7+! Kxf7 2. Rxf6+ Kg8 3. Rh8+ Kxh8 4. Rf8#**.

The period up to the middle of the 19th century was a synthetic one for both problems and studies, but later they developed along autonomous paths. Here it is necessary to mention the German player and composer Julius Mendheim, author of several books. The most significant is *Problems for Chess Players*, containing eighty-two works.

**№ 11. Julius Mendheim, 1832, Draw**  
![№ 11. Mendheim, Julius, 1832, Draw](./diagrams/diagram0011.png)

№ 11. This idea attracted the attention of contemporaries: **1. Nd7 a2 2. Ndf6 a1Q+ 3. Ke6** - and draw.

Julius Mendheim, who was among the first to recognize the beauty of correct mates, actively developed the battery idea, which remains relevant today. A battery is a position of two pieces of one color on the same line, where moving the front piece (the one that gives the battery its name) activates the long-range piece behind it: White **Kg1, Qe3, Ra3, Be5, Bg2, Nd4, pawns a5, f2** (8); Black **Ka7, Qg6, Rf8, Rg8, Bb8, Bc8, Nb4, pawns a6, b7, d5, e6** (11). Mate in nine: **1. Nb5++ Ka8 2. Qa7+! Bxa7 3. Nc7+ Kb8 4. Nxa6++ Ka8 5. Nc7+ Kb8 6. Nd5+ Ka8 7. Nb6+ Bxb6 8. ab+ Ka6 9. Ra6#**.

By 1836, the first chess magazine in the world, *Palamedes*, had been founded; its publisher was Louis-Charles Labourdonnais, one of the strongest players of his time.

**№ 12. L. Ch. Labourdonnais "Palamedes", 1837, Mate in 6 Moves**  
![№ 12. L. Ch. Labourdonnais "Palamedes", 1837, Mate in 6 moves](./diagrams/diagram0012.png)

№ 12. White's position requires urgent measures: **1. c8N+! Ke8 2. Qg6+ Kf8 3. Qf6+ Kg8 4. Ne7+-**, with mate in two moves.

Also noteworthy is the symbolic patriotic problem by the first Russian chess master, Alexander Dmitrievich Petrov (1794-1867), titled *Napoleon's Escape from Moscow to Paris*, published in *Palamedes* in 1838 (for the first time this problem, in a slightly different form, had been printed in A. D. Petrov's book *The Chess Game Brought into Systematic Order*, published as early as 1824): White **Kh2, Qh1, Bg6, Ne2, Nf1, pawns c2, c5, d4** (8); Black **Kb1, Rf4, Rf6, Be3, Na5, Nd8, pawns a4, b2, c4, c7, e6, f2, g4, g7** (14) - mate in fourteen moves.

This was probably the world's first original problem in the category of symbolic scachographic works in chess composition. (The word "scachography" is formed from two Greek roots: *scacho* - chess, and *grapho* - I write.) "Scachography," wrote Russian master I. S. Shumov, "is the art of depicting various objects or abstract ideas on a chessboard." Like peculiar chess "echoes," symbolic problems responded to events in Russia's political and military life in the 19th century.

In this problem, Napoleon's escape from Moscow to Paris is depicted. Square a1 represents Moscow, h8 represents Paris, the white knights represent the Russian cavalry, and the black king represents Napoleon. The fate of the black king on b1 closely resembles the inglorious end of the French emperor, who lost his multinational army in the vast expanses of Russia.

The pursuit of the king by white knights resembles the relentless chase of the emperor by Platov's Russian cavalry. As if to intensify the irony, the lone black king on his long path repeatedly meets remnants of his own army (knights a5 and d8, pawns a4, c4, c7), who can no longer save their ruler. The problem is not solved in the usual way, but demonstrated.

**1. Nd2++ Ka2 2. Nc3+ Ka3 3. Ndb1+ Kb4 4. Na2+ Kb5 5. Nbc3+ Ka6.**

At this moment, the move *6. Qa8#* would mate. However, this move cannot be played. Mating now, on move six, is too early, because by stipulation the problem must be solved in fourteen moves.

Trying to stay as close as possible to historical truth, Petrov brilliantly used this "prohibition" to realize another idea: he subtly hinted at the missed chance to capture Napoleon at the Berezina crossing (as is known, this happened through the fault of the inept commanders Chichagov and Wittgenstein, whom Napoleon misled with a feint).

The diagonal a8-h1 depicts the Berezina River. When, under white knight attacks, the black king reached a6, it was possible to mate at once with *6. Qa8#*, but instead the knights drove the king farther to h8. In his note to White's sixth move, A. D. Petrov wrote: "The queen should have blocked Napoleon's path; then he would not have escaped to Paris, and it would have been check and mate for him."

So, instead of *6. Qa8#*, one should play **6. Nb4+**.

Then the "pursuit" continues: **6...Ka7 7. Nb5+ Kb8 8. Na6+ Kc8** (the French troops are driven beyond the Berezina) **9. Na7+ Kd7 10. Nb8+ Ke7 11. Nc8+ Kf8 12. Nd7+ Kg8 13. Ne7+ Kh8**, and the Russian army victoriously ends the war: **14. Kg2#**.

This witty work by A. D. Petrov became one of the best examples of a symbolic military-themed chess problem. It quickly gained worldwide fame. Many generations of leading Russian and foreign composition masters learned from it how to depict military maneuvers on the chessboard. True, to some this kind of problem may seem a light amusement. But we should remember that in its time it spread through chess journals worldwide and remains popular even now.

Thus, A. D. Petrov was not only the first Russian chess maestro, but also the first chess composer.

**№ 13. A. D. Petrov, 1846, Mate in 6 Moves**  
![Chess Diagram 13](./diagrams/diagram0013.png)

№ 13. **1. Qxf4+! Kxf4 2. Rf1+ Kg4 3. Rf4+!** (Two striking sacrifices in Stamma's style.) **3...Kxf4 4. O-O+!** (Short castling!) **4...Kg4 5. Ne3+ Kh4 6. Nf3#**.

Besides A. D. Petrov, in 19th-century Russia K. A. Janisch, I. S. Shumov, and especially A. V. Galitsky were highly regarded as authors of notable compositions.

The foremost Russian master and composer of the previous century, K. A. Janisch, titled one of his problems "Tamerlane's Iron Cage" - White: **Kd1, Qd6, Rd5, Rg6, Bh3, Ne8, Nf7, pawns a7, e2, f2** (10); Black: **Ke4, Qa2, Ba3, Bd3, pawns c4, c5, d7, c3, e6, f4, g4, g7** (12) - smothered mate in ten. Here is how the black king is driven into confinement: **1. f3+ gxf3 2. exd3+ cxd3 3. Bf5+ exf5 4. Re6+ dxe6 5. Rd4+ cxd4 6. a8B+ Qd5 7. Bxd5+ exd5 8. Nf6+ gxf6 9. Qe5+ fxe5 10. Ng5#** - the king in a cage.

Thus, with the help of an old smothered-mate combination, K. A. Janisch symbolically captured the cruel treatment of prisoners by the Central Asian conqueror Tamerlane (Timur).

**№ 14. K. Janisch, 1837, Mate in 15 Moves**  
![Chess Diagram 14](./diagrams/diagram0014.png)

№ 14. **1. Kg3 f5 2. Kf2 f4 3. Kf1 h5 4. Kf2 h4 5. Kf1 h3 6. Ne5!** (Just in time! Otherwise Black gets tangled.) **6...Kh2 7. Kf2 Kh1 8. Ng4 f3 9. Kf1 f2 10. Nxf2+ Kh2 11. Ne4 Kh1 12. Kf2 Kh2 13. Nd2 Kh1 14. Nf1 h2 15. Ng3#**.

The Russian master I. S. Shumov gained particular renown through his scachographic compositions. Whereas the symbolic works of A. D. Petrov and K. A. Janisch were episodic in their wide-ranging creative output, I. S. Shumov spent many years working persistently and purposefully on symbolic problems as a conscious artistic goal. This is attested by his book, published at the beginning of 1867 under the intriguing title "A Collection of Scachographic and Other Chess Problems, Including a Complete Chess Primer — Political, Humorous, and Fantastic Mates..."

For example, the following problem — White: **Kh3, Rf1, Bc4, Nd3, pawns d5, e4, f3, g2 (8)** and Black: **Ka2, Bg1, Nb3, pawns a3, b4, c5, d6, e5, f2, f4, g3 (11)** — mate in eight moves. It was published in 1878 in the journal *Vsemirnaya Illyustratsiya* and in it the author strives to depict one of the most important episodes of the Russo-Turkish War — the heroic crossing of Russian soldiers over the Balkan mountain range.

The picturesquely arranged pieces and pawns represent the unbroken chain of obstacles of the barely passable ridge. The file of black pawns alone is striking. The problem's author composed the following verses to accompany this composition:

```text
The heroes crossed the Balkans!
Their terrible path was not swift:
The frost, the Turks, the blizzards —
All the hostile forces of the fearsome peaks,
Uniting into cruel obstacles,
Strained in vain for eight days
To break the valiant forces,
To halt the mighty warriors.
Nature itself has been conquered!
And onward — the blade shall take all!
But here, by the eighth move,
We shall arrive with ease — and Black is mated.
```

The solution unfolds as follows. From the first to the seventh move, the white king marches along the "mountain chain": **1. Kg4—f5—e6—d7—c6—b5—a4**. And Black? During this time they can only helplessly shuttle their bishop back and forth between g1 and h2. Finally, on the eighth move comes the decisive blow — **8. Bxb3#**.

The Russian army, whose "role" on the chessboard was "played" by the white king, delivered "mate" to the Turkish pasha.

**№ 15. I. Shumov, 1878, Mate in 4 moves by a knight only**  
![chess diagram](./diagrams/diagram0015.png)

Here is another, but orthodox, composition by I. S. Shumov (№ 15): **1. Rf6 b5 2. cxb5 exd5 3. b7+ and 4. b8N#**. The point of the problem is that the mate on the fourth move is delivered not by the knight already on the board, but by the newly promoted one.

The outstanding problemist A. V. Galitsky stood at the origins of the modern chess problem in Russia. He was characterized by a striving for artistic form — while observing all constructive requirements — featuring beautiful, non-obvious solutions and creating vivid, interesting combinations.

**№ 16. A. Galitsky, "Shakhmatny Listok", 1892, 1st prize, Mate in 2 moves**  
![chess diagram](./diagrams/diagram0016.png)

For example (№ 16), with a difficult and striking main variation: **1. Ba4!** (zugzwang) — **1... Bxe4 2. Bd1#**, containing an ambush by the white bishop.

**№ 17. A. Galitsky, "Stratégie", 1911, Mate in 3 moves**  
![chess diagram](./diagrams/diagram0017.png)

The problem (№ 17) is characterized by a paradoxical knight sacrifice. The straightforward *1. Ne3(5)?* or *1. h3?* leads to stalemate. With the maneuver **1. Nb6** White prepares, against **1... Kg4**, the reply **2. Nbd5 Kh5 3. Nf6#** or **2...h5 3. h3#**, and after **1... axb6** exploits the un-stalemate with **2. h3 b5 3. g4#**.

Changing in content and form over different periods of development, chess composition remained constant in one respect: it poetically and artistically distilled the principles and laws governing the development of the chess game. That is why each historical epoch in the development of composition — its conceptual content and technical execution — has its own style, handwriting, and creative level.

All forms of chess art - practical play and composition - are shaped by the same principles, common rules, and laws tested by time. Yet despite the common ground and the connection between practical play and composition, each defines its own means of creativity and speaks in its own artistic language.

As an example, let us compare a game played in the corridors of the London international tournament in 1851 with problem (№ 18).

**A. Anderssen - L. Kieseritzky**
**King's Gambit**

Hundreds of pages in chess journals and books have been devoted to this game. It is characteristic of Anderssen's style. Kieseritzky kept setting small traps, whereas Anderssen sought at any cost to gain an advantage in development and thereby create the preconditions for a combination.

**1. e4 e5 2. f4 exf4 3. Bc4 Qh4+ 4. Kf1 b5.**

The Polish master Lionel Kieseritzky was an outstanding player of his time, famed for unusual tactical ideas. This is one of them: by returning the gambit pawn, Black allows White's bishop to attack f7, but opens lines for his own queenside pieces.

**5. Bxb5 Nf6 6. Nf3 Qh6 7. d3 Nh5.**

Black is already threatening to seize the initiative. White cannot play *8. Kg1* (avoiding *8...Ng3+*) because of *8...Qb6+*.

**8. Nh4 Qg5?**

This maneuver, prompted by corresponding tactical considerations, is refuted by White through an unexpected piece sacrifice. Correct was *8...g6!*, not allowing the knight to f5 and threatening *9...Be7*; and after *9. g4 Nf6! 10. Kg2 Qh3 11. Bxf4 Nxg4* Black obtains counterplay (as indicated by master Ya. Neishtadt).

**9. Nf5 c6.**

This is Black's idea: if the bishop retreats (and what else can White do?), then *10...d5!* follows with active counterplay.

**10. g4! Nf6 11. Rg1!**

A powerful method: by sacrificing a piece, White thwarts the opponent's intentions and forces retreat along the entire front.

**11...cxb5 12. h4 Qg6 13. h5 Qg5 14. Qf3 Ng8 15. Bxf4 Qf6 16. Nc3.**

This is Anderssen's idea: for a piece he has obtained a huge lead in development, which in itself opens a variety of attacking possibilities. Black, meanwhile, has insufficient defense.

**16...Bc5.**

Later this move was condemned by critics, since White could simply and convincingly neutralize the pressure by *17. d4!* So what can one advise Black? Other continuations are no better. At least this poses some practical problems for the opponent. Such are the drawbacks of Black's delayed development. The fact that Black reached such a position reflects very poorly on his play. Anderssen, true to his principle - always attack - does not reckon with further material losses.

**17. Nd5?!**

The beginning of a beautiful conception that envisions sacrificing both rooks. But *17. d4* was simpler, better, and gave Black counterchances.

**17...Qxb2 18. Bd6!**

A beautiful move. White offers both rooks as a sacrifice in order to drive the black king into a mating net. *18...Bxd6* is impossible because of mate in four moves. (The next two moves are mistakenly given in reverse order in many manuals.)

**18...Bxg1?**

Black also remains true to his principle to the very end - take what can be taken, since a sacrifice may in fact prove incorrect. However, the loss of time connected with this move is fatal for Black. Black could have obtained counterchances by *18...Qxa1+ 19. Ke2 Qb2!* Now, however, White's attack is irresistible.

**19. e5!!**

Cutting off the black queen from defending g7.

**19...Qxa1+ 20. Ke2 Na6?**

Black defends c7, preventing the obvious mating threat (*21. Nxg7+ and 22. Bc7#*), but fails to notice that White has simultaneously created a second, concealed one. Both threats could be parried by the better defense *20...Ba6*. Yet even in that case, as proven by the founder of the Russian chess school Mikhail Ivanovich Chigorin, it is useless, since White still achieved an advantage after *21. Nc7+ Kd8 22. Nxa6 Bb6 23. Qxa8* etc., with irresistible threats.

**21. Nxg7+ Kd8 22. Qf6+ Nxf6**.

The queen sacrifice deflects the knight from defending e7.

**23. Be7#**.

The concluding combination is deep and striking. Seventy years later, the outstanding Czechoslovak grandmaster Richard Reti would write: "An immortal game, despite its many mistakes. For its mistakes were the mistakes characteristic of that time, while its immortal beauty lies in Anderssen's immortal ideas."

**№ 18. K. Bayer, "Era", 1856, 1st prize, Mate in 9 moves**  
![№ 18. K. Bayer](./diagrams/diagram0018.png)

№ 18. **1. Rb7! Qxb7 2. Bxg6+ Kxg6 3. Qg8+ Kxf5 4. Qg4+ Ke5 5. Qh5+ Rf5 6. f4+ Bxf4 7. Qxe2+ Bxe2 8. Re4+ dxe4**.

Thus, all of White's pieces are sacrificed in sequence, and in the finale the white king is supported by a single surviving pawn, which delivers the decisive blow - **9. d4#**. As we can see, both forms of chess creativity belong to one era - the flowering of the combinational period. In the practical game, the full richness of ideas, plans, and tactical elements is used. In the problem, the final goal is likewise mate, and the criteria for evaluating its artistic qualities are economy, uniqueness of solution, and a complete, exhaustive presentation of the combination.

"The inner content of a problem," wrote the German problemist F. Klett in his collection *The Chess Problem* as early as 1878, "consists in a combination, that is, a chain of ideas and conclusions which, with inevitable consistency, developing one from another according to the norms of chess laws, lead to the final result - mate. The value of a combination is determined, on the one hand, by its difficulty and, on the other, by its beauty."

All this is true. But what, then, is the difference between a work of practical play and composition?

In a practical game, two opponents struggle, whereas the creative process in composition consists in constructing positions with a specific task for one side, in which one idea or another is realized in artistic form. In it, the ideas of practical play are, as it were, concentrated to the maximum - ideas that in pure form would probably never occur in an actual game at the chessboard. This was vividly expressed by World Chess Champion A. A. Alekhine in the preface to F. Lazard's collection of problems and studies (1928):

"... I am deeply sympathetic to the very idea of composition. I would be happy to create entirely alone, without the need, as happens in a game, to adjust my plan to the opponent's plan in order to achieve something of value.

Ah, that opponent, that collaborator imposed on you. Every time, his notions of beauty diverge from yours, while his means (strength, imagination, technique) so often prove insufficient to assist your intentions actively. How many disappointments he brings to the true artist in chess, who strives not merely for victory, but above all to create a work of enduring value.

What suffering (unknown in any other field of art or science) it is to feel that your thought, your imagination, are inevitably constrained, by the nature of things, by the thought and imagination of another - too often mediocre, and always profoundly different from your own...

The breath of true art, freed from everything connected with our subjective personality, is expressed in joyful fullness in the work of the composer: in his problem (created by him alone), in his study."

And yet the foundation for the existence of composition is real chess play.

"For the chess problem as a work of art," wrote A. V. Galitsky, the founder of the chess problem in Russia, "nature, real life, is grounded in the chess game. Just as nature - the world of colors and sounds - provides elements and material for painting and music, so from chess play we take the elements for the problem, the material for its construction."

The inseparable connection between composition and game lies not only in the common discipline of play, in mutual interpenetration and enrichment by ideas and combinations, but also, as in every genuine art, in emotional and aesthetic impact on human feelings and thought. This specific feature of chess art belongs to both of its forms: practical play and composing; the educational power of chess art lies in cultural, meaningful creative communication.

The full diversity of the overall chess movement, in which a person participates and which in turn influences that person, is expressed through a creative approach to chess art: a person is revealed in the full breadth of his interests and ties with the chess world.

Thus, for example, the result of a played game expresses a person's feelings and attitude toward chess.

As for chess composition, by virtue of its specific features it has its own laws of logical and historical order. In the course of its development it has forged a whole arsenal of artistic principles, ideas, themes, and so on.

As already noted, chess composition finally took shape as an independent creative field in the middle of the last century. This period is considered the initial stage in the formation of a number of schools, trends, and styles. A little later, three independent directions of problem composition were clearly and distinctly formed: the *Czech*, *German*, and *English* schools.

Works of the Czech school are characterized by the presence of at least three variations ending in correct mates; that is, the primary element of the problem was taken to be a combination of beautiful mating positions that are simultaneously clean and economical. A *clean mate* is a mate in a problem or study in which every square is unavailable to the king for a single reason only: either occupied by its own pieces or attacked exactly once by an opponent's piece. An *economical mate* is a mate in a problem or study in which all pieces take part, except the white king and pawns because of their limited mobility.

In problems of the German (or Old German) school, the principal element was considered to be a striking, clearly executed combination in one main line, accompanied by sacrifices of White's pieces and ending, as a rule, in a correct mate.

Unlike the German school, the English school regarded the two-mover as its main genre. The fundamental requirements of this school are diversity and breadth of play with the greatest possible constructive clarity. And these are most simply and vividly realized in two-move form.

Since then, however, there has been a critical revision of views on the content of Old German and English school problems. This revision proved extremely fruitful and contributed to the emergence of new directions in problem composition, now the most widespread, known as the logical and strategic schools.

If we turn to the immediate process of creating a composition, we will notice that it consists of choosing an idea or theme and searching for means of its constructive realization. First of all, let us examine three problems: a two-mover, a three-mover, and a many-mover.

**№ 19. C. Mansfield, "Ajedrez Argentino", 1927, Mate in 2 moves**  
![Chess Diagram 1](./diagrams/diagram0019.png)

Diagram № 19. The problem was composed by the English International Grandmaster of Composition Comins Mansfield (1896-1984). The splendid first move **1. Qe7!** - the queen seems to move away from the scene of action - with the threat **2. Rxe6#**. A number of subtle variations arise: **1... Nxg7 2. Rf6#, 1... Nxf4 2. Rc3#, 1... Nxd4 2. Rc4#, 1... Nec5 2. Rxc5#, 1... Ndc5 2. Nf6#, 1... Nc7 2. Rxc7#, 1... Kd5+ 2. Rc2#** and **1... Rd5 2. Bxg5#**.

Note the role of the white queen - her position seems to cement the entire play. The problem realizes the idea of a half-pin: after one of the black pieces standing between the black king and the white queen moves, another piece becomes pinned.

**№ 20. L. Loshinsky, 1st USSR Championship, 1947, 1st place, Mate in 3 moves**  
![№ 20. Л. Лошинский, I чемпионат СССР, 1947, 1-е место, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0020.png)

№ 20. After the concealed key **1. Qb!!** (an ambush), White threatens **2. Nh5+ Kxe4 3. Re3#**. Black defends by freeing d4: **1... Rd5 2. Rd4!!** and **3. Nh5#** or **3. exd#**; **1... Rd6 2. Rd5!!** - again on the heels of the black rook - **3. Nh5#** or **3. Bxe5#**; **1... Rd7 2. Rd6!!** - the white rook seems to be drawn as if by a magnet.

However, its content is not exhausted by the given lines: against the black rook's horizontal moves **1... Rc4, 1... Rb4**, and **1... Ra4**, the white rook again comes alongside - **2. Rc3, 2. Rb3**, and **2. Ra3**, followed by **3. Nh5#**.

**№ 21. L. Loshinsky, "Shakhmatnaya Moskva", 1964, 1st prize, Mate in 6 moves**  
![№ 21. Л. Лошинский, «Шахматная Москва», 1964, 1-й приз, Мат в 6 ходов](./diagrams/diagram0021.png)

№ 21. After analyzing the solution to this six-mover, one wants to say: "A fairy tale has become reality!" On the chessboard there are four black queens - an incredibly formidable force - yet even that cannot prevent mate against the black king: **1. Kd7 g1Q 2. Bc7 a1Q 3. Bf3 d1Q** and **4. Rd4**, crossing the lines of action of the black queens. Now, if **4...Qd4**, then **5. Bxd5+ Qxd5 6. Nf4#**; after **4...Qa1xd4** follows **5. Nc5+ Qxc5 6. Re5#**; in the case of **4...Qdxd4** the solution is **5. Nf4+ Qxf4 6. Bxd5#**; and finally, **4...Qgxd4 5. Re5+ Qxe5 6. Nc5#**.

**№ 22. A. Kazantsev, "Problem", 1967, 1st prize, Win**  
![№ 22. А. Казанцев, «Проблем», 1967, 1-й приз, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0022.png)

As if taken straight from a game, № 22 runs: **1. e7! Na3+ 2. Kb6 Nc4+ 3. Kc5 Qa4 4. Rxb4 Qa7+ 5. Kxc4 Qxe7** - Black seems to have achieved his goal: White's hope, the future queen, has been destroyed. But... **6. Ng6+! fxg6 7. Bf6+! Qxf6 8. Kd5+ Kg5 9. h4+ Kf5 10. g4+ hxg4 11. Rf4+ Bxf4 12. e4#** - all the pieces are sacrificed, and mate is delivered by one of the two remaining pawns.

**№ 23. G. Zakhodyakin, "Shakhmatny Listok", 1930, 1st prize, Draw**  
![№ 23. Г. Заходякин, «Шахматный листок», 1930, 1-й приз, Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0023.png)

№ 23. White has no way to prevent the black foot soldier from promotion. So be it... **1. g7+ Kxg7 2. Nf7+ Kg8 3. Bc5! f1Q 4. Nh6+ Kh8 5. Bd6!!** - and it is a draw. Fantastic coordination of the white pieces: the bishop controls the black knight, the pawn protects the knight, and the knight protects both bishop and pawn. For example, **5...N- 6. Be5+ Ng7 7. Bd6**.

As we can see, ideas that in practical play appear only sporadically, sometimes indistinctly, and at times do not stand out from the background at all, are realized in composition fully and vividly.

Therefore, for a problem or study to become a work of art, the author must possess creative imagination, a clear conceptual design, and great mastery in its realization. Only then will the work move and inspire the chess lover, because in a genuine artwork the artistic form is inseparable from the thematic content.

As shown by the experience of the classics of chess composition - the Platov brothers, A. A. Troitsky, L. I. Kubbel, G. Ripka, and others - the possibilities of enriching problem and study technique with new devices are truly inexhaustible. The technical methods used by problemists and study composers contributed to the artistic execution of the concept underlying the work.

To understand this, it helps to analyze two positions that entered the golden fund of chess art. One is a remarkable study by the Platov brothers, composed before the Great October Socialist Revolution; the other is a four-mover by USSR grandmaster Ya. G. Vladimirov, which took second place in the 8th Championship of the Soviet Union in chess composition.

**№ 24. V. and M. Platov, "Rigaer Tageblatt", 1909, 1st prize, Win**  
![№ 24. В. и М. Платовы, «Ригаер тагеблатт», 1909, 1-й приз, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0024.png)

№ 24. This study is known for having attracted Vladimir Ilyich Lenin's attention and high praise.

We give the solution with commentary by A. S. Gurvich, Master of Sports in chess composition, from his book *Studies* (1961):

"The study is only 4-5 moves long, yet one must think so deeply and sharply to find them. Imagine that this position arose in a game: then one does not know in advance that an original winning possibility exists here.

A chess player who thinks conventionally, after the introductory move *1. Bf6* and the obvious *1... d4*, will immediately see that his material advantage is illusory, since the black pawn inevitably queens. But experience will at once suggest a combination that wins the newly promoted queen. He will certainly play *2. Nf3* and after *2...a1Q* triumphantly reply *3. Bxd4+ Qxd4 4. Nxd4 Kxd4*.

Having relatively easily neutralized the main danger, our player will quickly realize that the opponent still has to lose a tempo by capturing the pawn on d3; this can be used to win the black pawn on h7 and then queen his own pawn. But after *5. Kg4 Kxd3 6. Kg5 Ke4 7. Kh6 Kf5 8. Kxh7 Kf6* it turns out there is no win, because after *9. h6* (otherwise the pawn is lost) comes *9...Kf7*, and the white king is boxed in. Thus, in this position the habitual, stereotyped combination proves powerless.

But fresh, sharp, stubborn, and deep thought - thought that can seize the invisible - does not move this way.

After **1. Bf6 d4**, White's next move **2. Ne2!!** will seem to our conventional player mere whimsy. Here the knight performs the same role as on f3 - making a second attack on d4 - and the fact that it comes under king attack has no practical significance, since the knight cannot be taken because of 3. Bxd4. But when after **2...a1Q** White, "against reason," plays **3. Nc1!!**, refusing the saving 3. Bxd4+ and, moreover, offering the knight yet again, the worldview of the stereotyped thinker suffers a severe blow.

What, however, has happened on the board? If the knight is taken, the queen is won - no longer on the long diagonal, but on another diagonal, and with the bishop preserved. Not take it? But with the knight on c1, the bishop move to g5 is not check, but... mate. A completely unexpected, astonishingly beautiful mate in the center of the board.

Yet it is Black to move; he can move either queen or king. Alas, by using the only way for the king to escape the mating net (to d2), Black runs into a knight fork (*4. Nb3+*) and loses the queen. If instead Black defends against mate with **3...Qa5** - there is no other way to bring g5 under fire - only then follows **4. Bxd4+**, and whether the king takes the bishop or retreats to d2, the knight fork on b3 again brings White victory.

What a rich complex of beautiful ideas lies in the four-move play of this simple, light position: a mate that seems unreal, so ghostlike is its appearance - and four different ways of winning the queen, each of them unlike the one that first catches the eye in the initial position.

How much harder it is to find the two miraculous moves 2. Ne2 and 3. Nc1 than to calculate to the end the entire ten-move drawing combination. And if anyone in practical battle had been fortunate enough to penetrate the secrets of this position and finish it in his favor, what evergreen game could stand beside it?!

And this is how the then World Chess Champion Dr. Em. Lasker commented on the Platov brothers' masterpiece:

"Every chess player should derive the greatest pleasure from this study. Why? Is it because the win is achieved by observing the strictest economy of means? Is it because Black's more mobile and resilient pieces, despite every effort, as if by magic, become victims of White's weaker pieces? Or because White strives at all costs to avoid a draw? Perhaps what truly delights us is that the banal and ordinary are overcome here by the power of thought."

The four-mover is the most difficult genre of the modern problem, combining the breadth of the three-mover with the depth of a many-move combination. The technical difficulties of composing such problems are very great. To overcome them, one must possess virtuoso technique (which not every composer can master).

**№ 25. Ya. Vladimirov, "Probleemblad", 1966, 1st prize, Mate in 4 moves**  
![№ 25. Я. Владимиров «Проблеемблад», 1966, 1-й приз Мат в 4 хода](./diagrams/diagram0025.png)

Here (№ 25) we have a mind-bending concept: a cyclic interchange of White's moves in the solution. The splendid key move **1. Kb1!!** is organically linked with the threat **2. Rb4+ Kc5 3. Rb5+ 4. Rc5#**. Main lines: **1...Nd7 2. Rc3+ dxc3 3. Ne3+ fxe3 4. d3#, 1...Ne6 2. d3+ exd3 3. Rc3+ dxc3 4. Ne3#**, and **1...Re5 2. Ne3+ fxe3 3. d3+ exd3 4. Rc3#**.

It should be noted that in the first line, White's second move is White's fourth move of the third line; White's third move is the second, and the fourth is the third. We observe the same analogy in the second line relative to the first, and in the third line relative to the second. This is precisely what characterizes the cyclic interchange of White's moves in the solution.

Indeed, the difficulty of solving is determined by the depth of conception, the unobtrusive play, and the quiet, non-forcing moves that should create the impression of White's position weakening. The combination is adorned by sacrifices of the strongest pieces, difficult key and continuation moves, and the purity and economy of the finale.

**№ 26. M. Lipton, 1960, Mate in 2 moves**  
![№ 26. М. Липтон 1960 Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0026.png)

Look at problem № 26: an attempt to solve it by short castling, *1. O-O?!*, with the threat *2. Re1#* (*1...Ke4+ 2. Nc5#* and *1...Ke2+ 2. Nd4#*) fails because of *1...bxc6*. But a rook somersault to the other side succeeds: **1. O-O-O!** with the same threat: **1...Ke4+ 2. Ng5#** and **1...Ke2+ 2. Nf4#**.

At first glance, this fantastic position seems possible only in composition. Could even an approximately similar position arise in practical play? Yet the game between the American master Ed. Lasker and the Englishman G. Thomas (№ 27) takes us into the realm of the incredible.

**№ 27. Ed. Lasker - G. Thomas, London, 1911, White to move**  
![Chess Diagram 27](./diagrams/diagram0027.png)

In this position White, by sacrificing the queen (!), "draws" the black "monarch" into hand-to-hand combat and drives him into the rear, where he capitulates: **11. Qxh7+!! Kxh7 12. Nxf6++ Kh6** (but not *12...Kh8* because of *13. Ng6#*) **13. Neg4+ Kg5 14. h4+ Kf4 15. g3+ Kf3 16. Be2+** (after *16. Kf1!* Black would be mated one move earlier, but this is not composition, where one must mate the enemy king in a stipulated number of moves) **16...Ng2 17. Rh2+ Kg1 18. O-O-O#!** or **18. Kd2#**.

But that is not all. It turns out mate could have been delivered even earlier and... also by castling, only short castling this time. On move fourteen White should have played *14. f4+ Kxf4 15. g3+ Kf3 16. O-O#!* (also *16. Rf1+ Kg2 17. Rf2+ Kg1 18. O-O#*).

Practical play and composition enrich one another. A question may arise: is it worthwhile for practical players to familiarize themselves with composers' works? Absolutely. It develops combinational vision, making it possible to apply problem and study ideas in practical play.

**№ 28. M. Liburkin, "Shakhmaty v SSSR", 1934, 1st honorable mention, Win**  
![Chess Diagram 28](./diagrams/diagram0028.png)

In this study (№ 28), White wins if he can exchange his rook for both black knights. But carrying out such a "favorable" exchange is not so simple: **1. Rh8 Nfd7 2. Kc7 Ka7** (a waiting move) **3. Re8!! Nf6! 4. Rxb8 Ne8+** (the knight cannot be taken because of stalemate!) **5. Kd7!!** (but Black does not give up) **5...Nc7!** (and here comes the striking finale) **6. Ra8+! Kxa8 7. Kc8 Nc7 8. Nxc7!** - at last White has exchanged the rook.

Note that this position arose after the fifth move in an offhand game between famous grandmasters in Berlin in 1914. The game ended as shown in the study. J.-R. Capablanca defeated Em. Lasker. The inspired chess artist M. S. Liburkin transformed it into an outstanding work.

**№ 29. Yu. Kaem-NN, Dnepropetrovsk, 1956, Black to move**  
![№ 29. Ю. Каем-NN](./diagrams/diagram0029.png)

In one game (№ 29), in which White was the Dnepropetrovsk Candidate Master Yu. Kaem, after **1...a2** White was saved by a study-like stalemate resource: **2. Ra3 Rxa3 3. Rd3+! Rxd3**, stalemate.

**№ 30. Yu. Kaem and V. Rudenko, "Shakhmaty v SSSR", 1957, Draw**  
![№ 30. Ю. Каем и В. Руденко](./diagrams/diagram0030.png)

The practical player showed this ending to the future International Grandmaster of composition V. F. Rudenko, and soon study № 30 appeared. After **1. O-O-O+! Kg2 2. Rg5+ Bg4! 3. Rxg4+ Kh3 4. Ra4 a2**, the familiar stalemate position (№ 29) arose.

**№ 31. A. Arulaid - B. Gugenidze, Voroshilovgrad, 1955, White to move**  
![№ 31. А. Арулайд — Б. Гугенидзе](./diagrams/diagram0031.png)

Quite often one hears from chess players about the usefulness of problems and studies in practical play.

Position № 31 arose in a game played at a tournament in Voroshilovgrad in 1955. "Frightened" by Black's dangerous pawns racing to queen, White resigned. Yet there was a path to salvation: **1. Kd6! Kc8 2. Rc1+ Kb7 3. Rb1+ Ka6 4. Kc6 Ka5 5. Kc5 Ka4 6. Kc4 Ka3 7. Kc3 Ka2 8. Rf1! h5 9. Kd3 h4 10. Ke3 h3 11. Kf3**, with a draw.

**№ 32. B. Horwitz and J. Kling, 1851, Draw**  
![№ 32. Б. Горвиц и И. Клинг](./diagrams/diagram0032.png)

But the idea of such a plan had been indicated more than a hundred years before that game, in study № 32: **1. Kf4 Kh3 2. Kf3 Kh2 3. Ke3!**, etc.

International Grandmaster L. A. Polugaevsky, as an "amusing curiosity," cites the ending of a game between two strong grandmasters, L. Ljubojevic (White) of Yugoslavia and the American U. Browne, played at the 1972 international tournament in Amsterdam - White: **Ka5, pawn b3 (2)** and Black: **Kc6, pawn f7 (2)** - Black to move.

After *39...f5? 40. Kb4*, the players agreed to a draw, which both considered the natural result. The curiosity, in Polugaevsky's view, was that... one spectator pointed out that by **39...Kd5!** Black could win: **40. b4 f5 41. b5 f4 42. b6 Kc6 43. Ka6 f3 44. b7 f2 45. b8Q f1Q+ 46. Ka5 Qa1+**, or **40. Kb4 Kd4**, and White has no defense.

**№ 33. N. Grigoriev, 1928, Win**  
![№ 33. Н. Григорьев 1928 Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0033.png)

There is no doubt that the grandmasters were unfamiliar with study № 33, while the amateur apparently knew it. This position is almost a mirror reflection of N. D. Grigoriev's study, only with colors reversed.

**1. Kd4! b5.**

*1...Kb5* does not help because of *2. Kd5 Ka6 3. f4! Kb7 4. f5 Kc7 5. Ke6! Kd8 6. Kf7 b5 7. f6 b4 8. Kg7 b3 9. f7*, etc.

**2. f4 b4 3. f5 b3 4. Kc3 Ka3 5. f6 b2 6. f7 b1Q 7. f8Q+.**

After *7...Ka4*, *8. Qa8+ Kb5 9. Qb8+* follows, with a win.

**7...Ka2 8. Qa8#**.

**№ 34. Opočenský - P. Keres, Buenos Aires, 1939, Black to move**  
![№ 34. Опоченский — П. Керес Буэнос-Айрес, 1939 Ход черных](./diagrams/diagram0034.png)

Diagram № 34. The ending of the game K. Opočenský - P. Keres, played at the Buenos Aires Olympiad in 1939: **1...Kb2 2. Rb8+ Ka2! 3. Rc8 Rh4+ 4. Ka5 Kb2 5. Rb8+ Ka3 6. Rc8 Rh5+ 7. Ka6 Kb4 8. Rb8+ Ka4 9. Rc8 Rh6+ 10. Ka7 Rxh7-+**.

Perhaps the then still-young Paul Keres would have had to rack his brains before steering the game to a winning finish, had he not known Emanuel Lasker's famous study - diagram № 35.

**№ 35. Em. Lasker, "Deutsches Wochenschach", 1890, Win**  
![№ 35. Эм. Ласкер, «Дойчес вохеншах», 1890, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0035.png)

This is a classical study in rook endings. The systematic maneuvering that arises here has great practical value. Without this study, rook endgame theory would not be so complete.
The task seems difficult to fulfill, because the black pawn is strong. Incidentally, if Black's king were on a7, all White attempts to win would be futile. A small difference in king position leads to fatal consequences.

**1. Kc8-b8!** There is no time to lose. After *1. Rf3 Ka7!* Black has nothing to fear.

**1...Rc2-b2+ 2. Kb8-a8 Rb2-c2 3. Rf7-f6+ Ka6-a5.** Here and further, the black king retreats along the "a" file in order to keep checking along the "b" file. Going to b5 is out of the question: **4. Kb7** follows at once and the struggle is over.

**4. Ka8-b8(b7) Rc2-b2+ 5. Kb8-a7 Rb2-c2 6. Rf6-f5+ Ka5-a4 7. Ka7-b7.** Of course, 7. Kb6 is also possible, but it would be a blemish on the pure face of the study.

**7...Rc2-b2+ 8. Kb7-a6 Rb2-c2 9. Rf5-f4+ Ka4-a3 10. Ka6-b6.** Threatening *11. Rxf2*.

**10...Rc2-b2+ 11. Kb6-a5.** Excellent work. The king manages to support the pawn and actively participate in the attack.

**11...Rb2-c2 12. Rf4-f3+ Ka3-a2 13. Rf3xf2! Rc2xf2 14. c7-c8Q**, and White wins. Victory.

So P. P. Keres was helped by Lasker's study recipe. Our outstanding grandmaster himself was also actively engaged in composition, publishing from 1929 onward about 180 problems and 30 studies. He repeatedly won highly prestigious competitions. And at the first Championship of the Soviet Union in chess composition, he took 3rd place in the study section.

P. P. Keres's thoughts on composition echo Alekhine's:

"Composition is a field that has always attracted great attention from chess lovers. Here one can realize one's ideas without interference from an opponent-partner and thus enjoy complete creative freedom. It is well known that every chess player, whether a beginner or highly experienced, feels special pleasure when, after long reflection, he manages to find the solution to some tricky position - a 'nut to crack'...".

To become a chess composer, a "creative streak" or an interest in chess is not enough. Nor, let us say, are the qualities needed to be an excellent mathematician or physicist enough. Composition is, in our view, first and foremost an art, because it is a creative process.

A chess composer, unlike, for example, a writer or painter, must "build" works economically: without "waste," using only the material - pawns and pieces - required for the chosen scheme of the problem or study, for eliminating defects, and, for the soul, for satisfaction. Even the choice of scheme should be made in the simplest possible form - and that is not easy to achieve.

Steadfastness, willpower, the ability to overcome difficulties, and the cultivation of perseverance and patience are indispensable qualities of a true composer. It hardly needs saying that a composer must also possess creative imagination. Without it, the goals of chess composition cannot be attained.

One recalls the naive fears of one's own early path, when it seemed that venerable composers had already done everything, invented everything. The genres had long been defined, the themes long discovered. And I still kept discovering what had already been discovered. Yet further practical work proved how profoundly mistaken I had been. It turned out that some ideas are advanced precisely to open new ones, even more complex.

When my first two-mover succeeded, I was seized by an irresistible urge to compose a second. Then came the desire to "construct" a three-mover, a many-mover, and finally a study.

To set oneself a goal, to seek, to find and to err, to experiment, to rejoice and to be disappointed, and finally to feel the triumph of victory - is this not great satisfaction? Everyone who searches experiences it, and especially those who discover something new, unexplored. And the harder the goal is to attain, the greater the satisfaction when it is reached.

From the first mansuba to modern problems and studies, a huge distance has been covered. Chess composition is quite broadly represented in print. In every region of our country, chess commissions and federations are at work; first-class club buildings are at our disposal, where millions of chess enthusiasts study, with help from experienced coaches.

## MANDATORY AND ARTISTIC REQUIREMENTS OF CHESS COMPOSITION

It has already been noted that in the course of composition's development, artistic and formal requirements for chess works were formed, and knowledge of these is necessary for both composer and solver.

A problem or study is considered correct if it has a solution, the solution is unique, and the arrangement of pieces could arise in play from their initial positions. Here are a few examples.

**№ 36. Yu. Sushkov, 1981, Mate in 2 moves**  
![№ 36. Ю. Сушков 1981 Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0036.png)

№ 36. At first, the threat *2. Ng6#* arising after *1. Nf-?* is refuted by *1...d4*, unpinning the bishop on e4 (while after *1...Rg8*, *2. Qf6#* follows). Therefore White tries the more precise *1. Nd6!?*, attempting to preserve both threat and variation (after *1...d4*, *2. Rxe4#* now works). However, *1...Nd4!* unpins the bishop again. Finally, the corrected move *1. Nxg3!?* is refuted by a third unpinning of the bishop: *1...Bd4!*

White then gives up the possibility of mating with 2. Ng6# and therefore unpins that same bishop himself: **1. Nd4!** with the threat **2. Ne2#**, interposing against the queen that mates in the **1...Rg8** line and giving the black king a flight square. But now Black, by playing **1...Ke5**, gives White the chance to play **2. Ng6#**. Against **1...Nxd4** and **1...Bxd4**, mates follow with **2. Bd6#** and **2. Qxg3#**. If **1...Bd3**, then **2. Ne6#**.

The paradoxical nature of the problem is further enhanced by the fact that the key move is made precisely to the square on which Black prevents White's intended plan.

**№ 37. V. Melnichenko and V. Rudenko, "Shakhmaty," 1980, 1st prize, Mate in 3 moves**  
![№ 37. В. Мельниченко и В. Руденко «Шахматы», 1980, 1-й приз Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0037.png)

№ 37. The authors created a classical work on the modern Vladimirov theme (or Azerbaijani theme) in three variations. Their merit also lies in having developed a well-known two-move theme in a specifically three-move form with a light construction, wittily motivating the paradoxical change in move order.

Attempts *1. Ra8?*, *1. Rb8?*, *1. Rc8?* are refuted by Black with *1...Ra7!*, *1...Rb7!*, and *1...Rc7!*, after which *2. e4?* fails because of en passant capture.

In the solution, after **1. e4!** there is zugzwang; the first moves from the tries now follow on the very moves that had previously refuted those tries: **1...Ra7 2. Ra8!**, **1...Rb7 2. Rb8!**, and **1...Rc7 2. Rc8!**. Black is again in zugzwang, having lost the right to capture en passant (if **1...dxe3**, then **2. Rh4!** with the irresistible **3. Rc4#**). A known rule of practical chess thus becomes the basis for a changed play in the problem.

**№ 38. I. Breyer, 1938, Mate in 5 moves**  
![№ 38. И. БреЙер 1938 Мат в 5 ходов](./diagrams/diagram0038.png)

Here (№ 38), the naturalness of the position is reinforced by the placement of all eight black pawns. The stipulation - mate in five - also seems almost impossible. In fact, straightforward 1. Rd1 or 1. Rc1 does not achieve the goal, because the attacked pawn is immediately protected by its fellow pawn.

The siege of the pawn bastions must be conducted from the other side of the board: **1. O-O-O!**. Castling is allowed in problems if there is no proof that king or rook has previously left its original square. **1...d5** is a natural defense, but now a simple typical method of breaking a pawn chain is possible: **2. Rf1! f5 3. Re1**, and a breach appears in the pawn fortress, since the e6 pawn is undefended and White wins by **4. Rxe6 (e5)** with the unavoidable **5. Re8#**.

**№ 39. B. Horwitz and J. Kling, 1851, Win**  
![№ 39. Б. Горвиц и И. Клинг 1851 Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0039.png)

As already noted, study position № 39 should resemble as closely as possible the ending of an actually played game.

Two black pawns are irresistibly racing toward the first rank and seem on the verge of becoming a formidable force by promoting to queens. But White takes emergency measures and wins:

**1. Qa6+! Ke3 2. Qe6+ Kf3 3. Qf5+ Ke3 4. Qf2+ Kd3 5. Qf3+**, followed by **6. Qxe2!**. If **2...Kd3**, then **3. Qf5+ Kd4 4. Qf4+ Kd3 5. Qf3+** and **6. Qxe2**, also winning.

The works reviewed from all the main genres of composition show that uniqueness, clarity, and purity of solution are the principal criteria of a full-fledged chess creation - whether problem or study.

The most important artistic requirements of composition are: expressiveness of conception, economy, and beauty of solution. They are determined by the principles of unity of form and content, and by the harmonious correspondence between the conceptual idea and the means used to realize it.

Expressiveness of conception consists in clearly highlighting thematic variations that embody the author's idea. A composition in which this idea is expressed in the maximum number of thematic variations is called a record problem or record study, i.e., a task-problem or task-study.

**G. Baev, 1930.** White: **Ka6, Qh6, Rb1, Rh4, Bg3, Bg8, Nc2, Nc5, pawns b4, c7, e7 (11)** and Black: **Kd5, Re1, Rg5, Bf6, Nf7, Ng4, pawns c4, c6, e6 (9)** - mate in two.

The example under consideration is a record problem (in other words, a task problem): six interferences of black pieces on one square. Because of the complexity of the idea, the problemist (composer) had to resort to promoting the pawn to a knight on the first move: **1. c8N!** with the threat **2. Nb6#** - **1...e5 2. Bxf7#, 1...Nge5 2. Rd4#, 1...Be5 2. Qxe6#, 1...N7e5 2. Bxe6#, 1...Rge5 2. Qd2#, and 1...Ree5 2. Rd1#**.

**L. Loshinsky, 1940.** White: **Ke8, Qc3, Ra1, Rd3, Be7, Bh1, Ne4, Nf5, pawns c5, d2, g4 (11)** and Black: **Kh2, Ra8, Ba2, pawns b7, c2, d6, e5, g5, h3 (9)** - mate in three.

This is also a task problem. In six variations (the maximum possible!), the valve theme is shown: the black bishop, opening a path for the rook to a1, shuts off its action on other lines.

**1. Rc1!** with the threat **2. Bf3** and **3. Rh1#**: **1...Bb3 2. Rxd6 and 3. Qxe5#, 1...Bc4 2. Nexd6, 1...Bd5 2. cxd5, 1...Be6 2. Bxd6, 1...Bf7 2. Nfxd6, 1...Bg8 2. Kf2 and 3. Rxh3#**.

It may seem that White's pieces are under-employed, but that is not so: all of them either block lines, control squares, or participate in at least two variations.

**V. Savchenko. "Shakhmatnaya Moskva," 1970, 1st prize.** White: **Ke1, Ra1, Ba4, Ba7, Nf6, Nh5, pawns a2, b2, c4, c6, d5, f2, h4 (13)** and Black: **Ke5, Qf8, Bh3, Nf1, Nf7, pawns a3, a6, d6, f4, f5, g6, h2 (12)** - mate in eight.

*1. Rd1?* does not work because of *1...Nd2 2. Rxd2 h1Q+!* At first glance, **1. O-O-O** also seems to change little, because a new check appears: **1...axb2+**, and one cannot play *2. Kxb2 Qb8+!* or *2. Kb1 Nd2+!*

But then comes the unexpected **2. Kc2! b1Q+ 3. Kc3 Qb2+ 4. Kd3!**, for the third time refusing to take the queen! The threats **5. Nd7#** and **5. Re1+** force yet another queen sacrifice, which White now accepts: **4...Qe2+ (4...Qa3+ 5. Bb3) 5. Kxe2 f3+ (5...Ng3+ 6. Kd3 Bf1+ 7. Rxf1) 6. Ke1.**

The king has returned to its initial square, but a "weakness" has appeared in Black's position: the square f3 is blocked, and after **6...Nd2** there follows **7. Nd7+ Ke4 8. Bc2#**. An exceptionally effective and clearly executed logical combination!

**V. Korolkov, 1929.** White: **Kh1, Qf8, Rc2, Bc5, Bd7, pawns a7, b2, b6, b7, d4, f7, h2 (12)** and Black: **Kd1, Be2, Na2, pawns b4, f2, h3 (6)** - win.

White has an enormous material advantage, but his monarch is threatened with mate, and a furious attack begins.

**1. Rd2+! Kc1.** If *1...Kxd2*, then *2. Qh6+* and *3. Qxh3*, immediately eliminating all threats.

**2. Rd1+! Kxd1 3. Ba4+ b3!** Prudently transferring the bishop from diagonal a4-e8 to diagonal a2-g8. Much weaker is *3...Ke1 4. Bxb4+ Nc3 5. Bxc3+ Kf1 6. Bb5 Bxb5*; then c6 must be defended by *7. Qc8!* and the familiar *8. Qh3+*.

**4. Bxb3+ Ke1 5. Bb4+ Nc3!** The subtle point of the sacrifice becomes clear after Black's eleventh move.

**6. Bxc3+ Kf1.** The first signs of stalemate appear - there is no *7. Bd5? Bf3+ 8. Bxf3*, stalemate.

**7. Bc4! Bxc4 8. Qc5!** The square d5 must be protected - that is why *3...b3!* was needed.

**8...Bd3 9. Qb5!** White's ranks are thinning.

**9...Bxb5 10. b8N!** - and they are immediately replenished with new fighters!

**10...Bd3! 11. a8B!!** But not *11. a8Q? Be4+ 12. Qxe4*, stalemate.

**11...Be2!** Threatening *12...Bf3+ 13. Bxf3*, stalemate. The additional meaning of *5...Nc3!* is now clear: it is not only a "material dump" to aim for stalemate, but also a lure, drawing White's bishop to c3, where it is hemmed in on both sides by White's own pawns b2 and d4 and therefore cannot "de-stalemate" the black king by leaving diagonal a5-e1.

**12. f8R!!**, and White wins (there is no *12...Bf3 13. Rxf3*, and e2 is free). Three white pawns in succession promoted into different minor pieces! And they froze on the board, reminding us of the riddles Black posed to White, and of the inventive struggle between the sides (see IL № 19).

It should be said that at present more than three hundred composition themes are known; however, all of them are based on only seven fundamental tactical ideas: blocking squares around the black king, bringing white pieces onto those squares, decoy, interference, pinning black pieces (by self-pin or half-pin), unpinning white pieces, and checks against the white king.

But since the construction of task compositions, in which an idea is embodied in the maximum number of variations, as a rule leads to bulky, weakly artistic works of primarily technical interest, this naturally contradicts the principle of expressiveness and economy of form. Economy of form compels the author to realize the conception with minimal means. In turn, the general principles of economy of form are further divided into three sub-principles: economy of the initial position, of play, and of the finale.

**№ 40. L. Kubbel, 1939, Mate in 2 moves**  
![№ 40. Л. Куббель, 1939, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0040.png)

Economy of the initial position, for example, is characterized by all pieces participating in the solution. Here (№ 40), the idea of long castling by White combined with a check to the king is realized in miniature form: **1. O-O-O! a1Q+ 2. Nb1#** and **1...Kc3 2. Ne4#**.

**№ 41. S. Loyd, 1857, Mate in 3 moves**  
![№ 41. С. Лойд, 1857, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0041.png)

Pieces intended to eliminate cooks, duals, unsoundness, and so on are technical pieces playing a supporting role. For example (№ 41), the solution of this miniature problem (total number of pieces no more than five): **1. Rf4!** — zugzwang: **1...Kxg3 2. O-O! Kh3 3. Rf1#** and **1...Kxh1 2. Kf2 Kh2 3. Rh4#** — is very interesting. But what purpose does the pawn on g3 serve? It turns out that without it the problem would have cooks: *1. Rfg1+ Kf3 2. Rh4 Ke3 3. Rg3#* and *1. Rhg1+ Kh2 2. Rg8 and 3. Rh1#*. It is therefore a technical piece that eliminates a flaw in the problem.

Accordingly, the composer must strive to reduce the number of technical pieces, to employ all white pieces in thematic variations, and thereby ensure the lightness of the construction.

**№ 42. R. Kofman, 1939, Mate in 3 moves**  
![№ 42. Р. Кофман, 1939, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0042.png)

In a simple form (№ 42) the author succeeded for the first time in presenting the complex-block theme in three variations within a three-mover with correct mates: **1. Bc2! Ra6 2. d4+ (threat) cxd3 3. f4#**, **1...c4 2. Nc6+ Kd5 3. Be4#**, and **1...cxb2 2. Nxb4 Rf5 3. Re4#**.

The principles of economy of form are strictly observed here: the economy of means is impressive, and the naturalness of the construction is particularly striking.

**№ 43. R. Réti, 1928, Draw**  
![№ 43. Р. Рети, 1928, Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0043.png)

The last requirement — that the initial position should approximate as closely as possible a position from a practical game — must always be observed in composing studies. As an example, nothing fits better than the famous study by Czech grandmaster R. Réti (№ 43), which resembles the ending of a game adjourned in a hopeless position. Yet, surprisingly, a single white pawn draws against the opponent's three connected passed pawns.

**1. Kg6 Kb6 2. Kxg7 h5 3. Kxf6 h4 4. Ke5 h3 5. Kd6 h2 6. c7**, and draw. **1...h5 2. Kxg7! h4 3. Kxf6 Kb6 4. Ke5! Kxc6 5. Kf4 h3 6. Kg3 h2 7. Kxh2** also does not change matters — the king catches the pawn on the verge of its promotion.

The main criterion characterizing economy of play in a problem is the correspondence between the number of solution moves and the conception of the composition. That is, if the complete expression of the idea requires only three moves, realizing the conception in the many-mover genre is entirely unjustified.

**A. Popandopulo. "Vechirny Kyiv", competition dedicated to the 1500th anniversary of Kyiv, 1982, 3rd honorable mention.** White: **Kh6, Rb8, Rd8, Ba8, pawn e6 (5)** and Black: **Ka6, Qd3, Ra4, Ra5, Bb1, Bc5, Nh5, pawns a7, b5, c7, d5, e7, e5, f5, g5 (15)** — mate in fifteen moves.

The two white batteries already set up in the initial position cannot deliver a decisive blow — they lack the necessary scope. But were the white pawn on e6 absent, a third mixed-piece battery could come into existence, and that would decide the outcome. All three batteries — two auxiliary and the main one — fire twice.

**1. Bb7+ Kb6 2. Bc8+ Kc6 3. Bd7+ Kd6.** (After 6...K—, 7. Rf8# follows.) **6. Bc8+ Kc6 7. Bb7+ Kb6 8. Bxd5+ Ka6** — the king has returned to its starting position. **9. Rd6+! cxd6 10. Be6 Qe4 11. Bc8+ Qb7 12. Rxb7 Be4 13. Rb8+ Bb7 14. Rxb7 and 15. Rb8#**.

So the main play (White's forced maneuver) ends with a striking rook sacrifice, but the concluding part of the solution is artificially prolonged and of little interest. One may well ask: why did a renowned Soviet many-move composer need to attach this "foreign body" to the main idea?

As for economy of play in a study, it is not violated by an artificial prolongation of the solution before returning after a few moves to the author's main line. Moreover, in a study all pieces must display their capabilities to the maximum (by which is meant their mobility, dynamism, etc.); in other words, their efficiency must be pushed to the highest level.

**№ 44. A. Troitsky, USSR Championship, 1929, 7th place, Win**  
![№ 44. А. Троицкий, Чемпионат СССР, 1929, 7-е место, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0044.png)

№ 44. Extremely rarely in world chess literature does one encounter a position where the assignment to win provokes as much bewilderment as in this study. With an even balance of modest forces, the opposing pieces are at maximum distance from one another, and no sign of their interdependence is visible. All forces on the board are free.

Can this apparently dead-drawn endgame really conceal any secret? Inconceivable — yet **1. Bh6!**, and the black king is immediately overshadowed by a mating threat. If he does not immediately leave his corner, and instead a bishop or pawn move is made, a mating net materializes at once.

For example: *1...c5* (or *1...Bb7 2. Ne4*) *2. Nb5*, and whatever Black replies, White, playing *3. Nd6!*, will keep the enemy king under "house arrest" on h8 and g8 until the triumphant white king arrives at the scene: *3...B— 4. Kc2 Kg8 5. Kd2 Bf3 6. Ke3 Bc6 (g2) 7. Kf4 c4 8. Ke5 c3 9. Kf6 Bd7* (threatening *10. Nf5* or *10. Nc8 and 11. Ne7—*) *10. Ne4 c2 11. Ke7 B— (11...c1Q 12. Nf6+ Kh8 13. Bxc1 B— 14. Kf8) 12. Nf6+ Kh8 13. Kf8 and 14. Bg7#*, or *9...Bh3! 10. Ne4 c2 11. Ke7 c1Q 12. Nf6+ Kh8 13. Bxc1 Kg7 14. Nh5+ Kg6 15. Nf4+* and White wins.

Black is therefore obliged, without loss of time, to play **1...Kg8** and, after **2. Ne4**, to break the king free before it is too late. But after **2...Kf7** White radically transforms the entire plan and shifts the attack from the h8 corner to a8!

We saw how, when the bishop or the c-pawn moved, White quickly paralyzed the enemy king and the h-pawn. Now that Black has used the first two tempos to flee with the king, White's **3. Nc5** immediately paralyzes the bishop on a8 and the c-pawn before they can stir. The knight fights energetically on two fronts, choosing in the critical moment the one where the opponent hesitates.

White's aim is now to win the bishop, for which three strategic tasks must be carried out simultaneously:

1. prevent the black king from coming to the aid of its bishop;
2. prevent the black king from driving the white knight from its key position;
3. neutralize Black's strong counterchance connected with advancing the h-pawn, which now plays the same "liberating" role that the c-pawn attempted to play in the mating lines.

**3...Kg6.**

There is no point for Black in playing *3...Ke8*, since after *4. Bg5 h5 5. Bh4* Black must return to the main line, having lost two tempos. Against *4...h6* White can even capture the pawn, let the black king reach c8, and place the bishop on f4, controlling b8 and c7.

In that case the black bishop will not be able to escape through b7: c8 is blocked by its own king, a6 is controlled by the knight, and just as in the mating lines the blocked king had to stamp on g8 and h8, the blocked bishop is now forced to shuttle between a8 and b7 until the white king comes to destroy it.
Thus resistance is hopeless in that corner too. The black king could help its bishop only if it managed to reach b6 by going all the way around the board, after which the bishop could escape via b7 and c8.

**4. Bf8 h5! 5. Kc2 Kf5 6. Bd6! Kg4 7. Kd2 Kf3 8. Ke1 Kg2.** The fierce struggle has moved to the third corner of the board!

**9. Be7 Kg3 10. Kf1 Kf3.** Hopes in the h-pawn have also come to nothing, and Black seeks one last path to salvation.

**11. Bd6 Ke3 12. Be5!** Not allowing the king through d4 to c4.

**12...Kd2 13. Kg2 Kc2 14. Kh3 Kb1.** This is where the king wanders in search of a way through to the "arrested" bishop!

**15. Kh4 Ka2 16. Kxh5 Ka3.**

The last hope — but...

**17. Bc3!**

Black is defeated in the fourth corner of the board. They are forced to retrace the same cheerless path with the king, not daring to approach their doomed bishop, because the white bishop at the right moment undoes with a single move the many-move maneuvers of the black king, while the white king approaches a8 without interference.

A magnificent study! With extremely limited forces, White establishes iron control over the entire board, gives battle to the opponent in every sector — both center and edges — and asserts supremacy everywhere.

In the scale of play that can be achieved by coordinating a king with only two minor pieces on an open board — an unsurpassed work! (Commentary — see IL № 13).

Now let's compare the following two studies.

**G. Kasparyan, 1936**. White: **Kf4, Re7, Nd8, pawn h4 (4)** and Black: **Kg6, pawn f2 (2)** — win.

**1. h5+! Kh6!**

There is no *1...Kxh5 2. Rh7+* and *3. Rh1*, nor *1...Kf6 2. Rf7#*.

**2. Nf7+ Kxh5.**

If *2...Kh7*, then *3. Ng5+ Kg8 4. Re8+ Kg7 5. h6+ Kg6 6. Kg4! f1Q 7. Re6+ Qf6 8. Rxf6+ Kxf6 9. Kh5*.

**3. Re5+ Kh4!**

After *3...Kg6* there follows *4. Rg5+ Kf7 5. Rf5+ and 6. Kg4*, or *4...Kh7 5. Rh5+ and 6. Rh1*.

**4. Ng5! f1Q+ 5. Nf3+ Kh3 6. Rh5+ Kg2 7. Rh2#.**

The elegant final mate is preceded by a good introduction with sacrifices of the white pawn; White's rook and knight take up positions for the concluding attack (the moves beginning with 3. Re5+), and the black king arrives on the fatal square g2. But the conflict situation is still the same: the rook cannot stop the pawn while standing on the adjacent e-file, Black has no counterplay, only aimless king maneuvering. There is introductory play, but no play of idea, no equality of the sides in the struggle.

**№ 45. Z. Birnov, 1947, Win**  
![№ 45. З. Бирнов, 1947, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0045.png)

№ 45. **1. Rg7+ Kb6 2. a8N++!!**

Only this way. Any other continuation deprives White of the initiative.

**2...Ka6.**

Curiously, Black has immediately fallen into a mating net. On both a5 and c6 they would be punished at once; moreover, in the mate on c6 a sad role would be played by their d6-pawn, which intends to join the struggle and will show itself again.

**3. Nc7+ Ka5!**

After *3...Kb6* there follows *4. Nd5+ Ka6 (c6) 5. Nb4+* and *6. Nxc2*. If instead *3...Ka7 (b7)*, then *4. Ne6+*, bringing g5 under fire, and then *5. Rg1* with a win, since the bishop has no move to g5.

**4. Rg1.**

It would seem that all is over, but the struggle is only beginning.

**4...Bg5!**

By threatening to queen the pawn, Black compels White to accept the sacrifice. But why? After all, the bishop is captured with check, and the rook returns to g1. Let us see.

**5. Rxg5+ d5+!**

A sharp rejoinder! Black answers a check with a check and once again compels White to accept a sacrifice.

**6. Rxd5+,**

after which the rook can no longer return to the first rank to stop the pawn (the Roman theme!).

**6...Ka4.**

Of course not *6...Kb5 7. Rb5+ Kxc7 8. Rc5+* and *9. Kb5 (d5)*. Now White's attack is over and, left with a single pawn, Black seems to have achieved the goal: its promotion to a queen cannot be prevented. But White too has a sharp rejoinder. After

**7. Nb5 c1Q+.**

White in turn answers a check with a check, but a more formidable one!

**8. Nc3+ Ka3 9. Ra5+ Kb2 10. Ra2#.**

Promotion of the pawn to a knight, sacrifices of Black's pieces, the Roman theme, mutual checks by both sides, and finally an effective mate: it is remarkable how harmoniously all this has merged in Z. Birnov's charming miniature (see IL № 13).

"Only one extra unit in the initial position, and yet how many bright new colors the study begins to display. Here there is an introduction pleasantly linked with the finale (the appearance of the white knight), and genuinely thematic play (creating the conflict that leads to the noted adjacency of the movement lines of the white rook and the black pawn), and the same economical correct mate resolving the dispute between White and Black" (commentary by international master Ap. Kuznetsov).

The following examples illustrate the possibilities of finding introductory play without adding extra material.

**R. Réti, 1925**. White: **Kf5, Rf1, Bc4 (3)** and Black: **Kh2, pawn g3 (2)** — win.

**1. Rf3!**

Lying in ambush for the g3-pawn along the third rank.

**1...g2 2. Bf1!** Lying in ambush for the g2-pawn along the diagonal f1-h3.

**2...g1Q 3. Rh3#.**

The solution is clear and effective. And is an introduction really needed?

**№ 46. N. Kralin, 1963, Win**
