![№ 46. Н. Кралин, 1963, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0046.png)

№ 46. The same characters as in Réti's study.

**1. Rf2!!**

It looks paradoxical, since it allows the black pawn to advance with tempo.

**1...g3 2. Rf3!**

Réti-style "ambush."

**2...Kh2!**

Clearly *2...Kg4? 3. Ke4 g2 4. Be6+ Kh4 5. Rh3+, 6. Rg3+ and 7. Rxg2* is weak; more intricate is the line *2...Kh4!? 3. Rf4+ Kg5 (3...Kh3 4. Bc4 g2 5. Bf1) 4. Ke4 g2 5. Rf8! g1Q 6. Rg8+ and 7. Rxg1*.

**3. Bc4! g2 4. Bf1!**

Réti-style "ambush."

**4...g1Q 5. Rh3#.**

Clearly better: the solution is harder (1. Rf2!!), the black king lands on the mating square (2...Kh2!), and the study's dynamics are richer. The black king moves, White's rook and bishop each make two moves, the black pawn advances not twice but three times, and the motivation for 1. Rf2! is beautiful: the square f1, to which the bishop is heading, must be protected (see IL № 19).

For the final position to be economical, as many of the pieces remaining on the board as possible must participate in it. Chess composers should use this circumstance when constructing compositions that end in correct mating positions. In them the requirements of purity of mate are observed, meaning each square around the black king is controlled for only one reason, either occupied by a black piece or attacked once by White, and of economy of mate, when all the white pieces remaining on the board take part in creating the mating picture, except the king and pawns because of their low mobility.

**№ 47. S. Kryuchkov, 1927, Mate in 3**  
![№ 47. С. Крючков, 1927, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0047.png)

Here in № 47 the mate in the line **1. Bc6 e1Q 2. Bg2+ Kg1 3. Nf3#** is pure and economical: all the squares around the black king are taken away exactly once. The square f2 is blocked by Black's own pawn, and the others are each attacked once by White, so the mate is pure; all the white pieces except the king participate in creating the mating picture, so the mate is economical.

In the line **1...e1N 2. Bb5+ Nd3 3. Qd1#** the mate is also correct, because the white bishop actively participates in the mating picture by pinning the knight, refuting 3...Ne1! Thus it is highly significant when, in creating a mate or stalemate, a white piece participates by pinning and blocking a square near the black king.

The convention of correct mate consists in the fact that, whenever possible, Black is obliged to make moves that lead to the creation of a correct mating position.

For example, here in the threat line Black captures the bishop: **1...bxc6 2. Nf3!** and **3. Qh3#**, or **2...e1Q 3. Nh2#**; that is, two pure and economical mates. So altogether there are three variations with four correct mates (see IL № 27).

When composing studies, the initial position should approximate a practical game position as closely as possible. We offer two studies for your attention.

**V. Mandinyan, 1915-1946**. White: **Kb1, Nh5, pawns b3, b6, c2, g7 (6)** and Black: **Kg5, Ra4, Ra5, Ba3, Ba6, pawns b2, b4, b5, b7 (9)** — win.

**1. Ng3 Kh6 2. g8R Kh7 3. Rg5 Kh6 4. Rg4 Kh7 5. Nf5 Kh8 6. Nh6 (e7) Kh7 7. Ng8 Kh8 8. Rg6 Kh7 9. Rg3 Kh8 10. c4 Kh7** (If Black captures the pawn, then 11. Nf6 with mate to follow on g8.) **11. c5 Kh8 12. c6 Kh7 13. c7**, and White wins.

**V. Korolkov, 1936**. White: **Kh3, Qd4, Rf1, Rg1, Bg3, pawns f2, f4, g5, h2, h6 (10)** and Black: **Kh5, Rd1, Bc2, Nh4, pawns e6, f3, g2, g6, h7 (9)** — win.

**1. Qc5 Bf5+ 2. Qxf5 exf5 3. Re1 Rc1 4. Rd1 Rb1 5. Rc1** (If 5. Rg1, then 5...g1N+ 6. Rxg1 Ng2, and Black escapes) **5...Ra1 6. Rb1 Rxb1 7. Rxb1 g1Q 8. Rxg1 Ng2 9. Rxg2 fxg2 10. Kxg2**, and White wins.

Now compare the studies just examined with the next two.

**№ 48. A. Kazantsev, 1947-1948, Draw**  
![№ 48. А. Казанцев, 1947—1948, Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0048.png)

№ 48. In this simple position we have before us classical piece material.

**1. d6.**

The only chance to neutralize the black bishop by forcing it to fight against two passed pawns at once.

**1...Kb5 2. dxe7 Ke5.**

Now both promotion squares of White's pawns are under Black's control: h8 by the bishop, and e8 by the threat of a fork on d6; meanwhile the a-pawn will advance unhindered.

**3. e8N!**

The first surprise. The knight not only parries the fork threat, but also attacks the bishop, which has only one square of retreat: h8.

**3...Bh8 4. h7 a3 5. Kg8 Kxc6 6. Kxh8 Kf7.**

White's position has become completely hopeless.

**7. Nd6+!**

A far-sighted idea.

**7...Kf8 8. Nxb5 a2 9. Nd4!**

And in this "hopeless" position the white knight takes up a post from which it reaches both a1 and f8. Now yet another possibility of "study-like" salvation opens up for White.

**9...a1R! 10. Ne6+ Kf7 11. Nd8+ Kg8! 12. Kg8 Ra8.**

And the third pawn too cannot promote to a queen. But at the end White once again employs the grand combination: promoting a pawn to a knight.

**13. h8N+! Kh5 14. Nhf7,** and the battle ends in a draw.

From a simple, light, and natural initial position there gradually unfolds a game exceptional for its richness of content and beauty (see IL № 21).

**№ 49. E. Somov-Nasimovich, "Magyar Sakkvilag", 1928, Draw**  
![№ 49. Е. Сомов-Насимович, «Мадьяр шаквилаг», 1928, Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0049.png)

№ 49. **1. Kd3 Bb2 2. Rg2 Ba1 3. Rg1 Bc2+**.

Otherwise the white rook would attack the black bishop forever.

**4. Kc4 Nb3**.

The dark-squared bishop is defended, but now the rook begins to pursue the light-squared one.

**5. Rg2 Na5+.**

If *5...Bd1 6. Rg1 Bc2 7. Rg2*. Black finds new possibilities to continue the fight.

**6. Kb5 Nc6!**

Here is Black's counterchance: both black pieces are invulnerable. *7. Kb4? Be4+* or *7. Rxc2? Nd4+*, and Black wins.

**7. Rg1! Bd4**.

Otherwise the knight perishes.

**8. Rc1! Be3**.

Black has nothing better, but with the bishop on e3 White manages to exchange the rook for Black's two pieces.

**9. Rxc2 Nd4+ 10. Kc4 Nxc2 11. Kd3 - draw**.

Both outstanding works of study art, not inferior to each other in the least in merit, are original in conception, full of surprises, double-edged and subtle play, extremely effective in their climactic moments, and at the same time maximally economical and natural in their initial positions.

Beauty of solution in a problem or study is achieved through hidden, hard-to-find moves and maneuvers by both sides. In a study, a solution is considered beautiful when it is achieved by dynamic, sacrificial, and subtle play, accompanied by strong false trails. It is desirable to achieve an organic unity of introductory play and finale.

Moscow international master of chess composition A. G. Kuznetsov writes about this point in greater detail and more accessibly in his book *The Colors of the Chess Spectrum* (1980). In his opinion, the core of a study is the main line of the solution, the author's, thematic, principal variation, which must stand out from the others by special beauty and effectiveness, depth and subtlety, and be, as it were, in relief.

Connected with it, supporting and conditioning it, are the side variations, when White tries to deviate from the main line. And despite the fact that side and proving variations have features that give rise to substantially different opinions, the conclusion is clear: the logic of the former is governed by the principle of solvability, that of the latter by the principle of uniqueness of solution. The absence of side variations in a study makes its solution dull; without them studies acquire a lifeless, schematic appearance.

**№ 50. D. Gurgenidze, 1970, Draw**  
![№ 50. Д. Гургенидзе, 1970, Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0050.png)

№ 50. **1. Ka3!!**

The knight is under siege, and losing even a single tempo is unreasonable: *1. Kb2? Kf7!* and the white king does not get into the square of the h-pawn. Why *1. Kb3?* also fails will become clear from the main line.

**1...Ke6!**

Now in the case of *1...Kf7 2. Kb4 Kg7 3. Kxb5 Kxh7 4. Kc4*, it is a draw, a side variation.

**2. Nf8+! Kf5! 3. Nd7 h5 4. Nc5 h4 5. Nb3!**

Here is the explanation for preferring 1. Ka3 to 1. Kb3!

**5...h3 6. Nd2 h2.**

A theoretical side variation: *6...Kf4 7. Nf1 Kf3 8. Kb4 Kf2 9. Nh2 Kg2 10. Ng4 Kg3 11. Ne3*, and the saving resource is the fork: *11...h2 12. Nf1+ and 13. Nxh2*.

**7. Nf1! h1Q 8. Ng3+ and 9. Nxh1,**

successfully completing the knight's round-the-world cruise across the whole board.

So, side variations in win or draw studies must at minimum satisfy the stipulation; in the latter case, a win for White is also acceptable.

Proving variations are another matter. Their difficulty, complexity, and depth would seem only to strengthen the impact of the main line and, therefore, of the main idea. However, it is not so simple.

**№ 51. G. Nadareishvili, 1950, Win**  
![№ 51. Г. Надарейшвили, 1950, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0051.png)

№ 51. **1. g6 Kf6! 2. g7 Bh7! 3. e4!!**

So far, two incomprehensible moves, one by Black and one by White...

**3...Nf3 4. e5+! Nxe5.**

The knight has transferred to the center.

**5. Kxh7 Nf3 (f7) 6. g8Q Ng5+! 7. Qxg5+! Kxg5 8. h6 c4.**

If *8...Kf6*, then *9. Kg8* and the queen will come through with check.

**9. Kg7 c3 10. h7 c2 11. h8Q c1Q 12. Qh6+ and 13. Qxc1.**

Only now does the depth of the paradoxical *3. e4!!* become clear: the diagonal h6-c1 has been opened.

At first this study was published without the pawn on c7, and its absence allowed a proving variation: *3. Kxh7 Nf3 4. g8Q Ng5+ 5. Qxg5+ Kxg5 6. h6 c4 7. Kg7 c3 8. h7 c2 9. h8Q c1Q 10. Qh6+ Kg4 11. Qxe6+ Kf3 12. Qf5+! Ke2 13. e4 c-* with fairly good winning chances.

The cited proving variation here is connected not thematically but only formally. The author acted correctly in adding this pawn, which leads to a simple draw in that line and thereby cuts off tiresome over-analysis.

**№ 52. G. Kasparyan, 1949, Draw**  
![№ 52. Г. Каспарян, 1949, Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0052.png)

№ 52. **1. Bh5+ Ke1 2. Bh4+ Kd2 3. Bg5 Bxc5 4. Kf2.**

This is the initial position of the forthcoming systematic maneuver.

**4...Kd3 5. Bg6+ Re4+ 6. Kf3 Bc6.**

*7. Bc1?* looks natural, but after *7...Kc2! 8. Bg5 Bxa3 9. Bxe4+ Bxe4+ 10. Kxe4 a5 11. Kd4 Kb3!* White loses.

**7. a4!**

The first step of the systematic maneuver is complete.

**7...Kd4 8. Bf6+ Re5+ 9. Kf4 Bd6 10. a5!**

The second step is complete. Again there is a loss after *10. Bc2? a5! 11. Bd1 Bd5 12. Bg7 Bc4 13. Bf3 Bb3 14. Bc6 Bd5!*

**10...Kd5 11. Bf7+ Re6+ 12. Kf5 Bd7 13. a6!**

Again onto the color of the bishop that is defending the rook! In the line *13. Bc3? Bc5 14. Be5 a6! 15. Bg8 Bb4 16. Bc7 Kc6* Black would again win.

But now the third step has been made, and the position has been repeated for the fourth and final time: a positional draw.

Here the proving variations are organically fused with the main line, governing the movement of the modest a-pawn, whose participation in the systematic maneuver lends the play a special charm.

Therefore, formal proving variations, which merely ensure the correctness of a study and the absence of cooks, should be avoided. But thematic proving variations, even if long and difficult, are necessary, for they lend subtlety and depth to the main solution and give the study as a whole breadth and even a sense of volume.

In a problem, as stated in the Chess Code, especially one with a small number of moves, a beautiful solution is one in which White's moves do not involve any significant strengthening of White's position or any striking weakening of Black's, nor a restriction of the freedom and mobility of Black's pieces. Therefore, quiet moves are preferable: moves without checks, captures, taking away squares from the black king, and so on. A beautiful key move is one that creates the appearance of weakening White and, conversely, strengthening Black. For that reason it is undesirable to give check to the black king, to leave unanswered a check against the white king in the initial position, or to promote a pawn to a queen.

The defects of a problem or study are considered to be: a cook, a dual, unsoundness, and an impossible position. Naturally, the solver must detect these defects as well. For the composer, however, besides all of the above, it is also important that the work he has created should not have a predecessor.

Predecessors are earlier published compositions whose conception, scheme, and presentation coincide to one degree or another with the conception, scheme, and presentation of the newly published composition.

The existence of a predecessor is established by comparing the actual publication dates of the printed sources in which both compositions were first published. It does not matter whether the author of the new composition knew the old one or not.

One distinguishes: a **complete predecessor**, where the idea, scheme, and construction of both compositions coincide completely, in which case the new composition loses its right to exist; and a **partial predecessor**, where the idea and scheme coincide, but the construction differs.

A composition having a partial predecessor may be recognized as a chess work and published on condition that the surname of the old author, "A," and of the new author, "B," are indicated. If the old composition has been improved only slightly, by reducing the number of pieces, removing a defect, and so on, the new composition is published under the name of "A," with the note "Revision by B" below it. If the improvement of the old composition is substantial, the new composition is published under the name of "B," with the note "After A" below it. Moreover, the new composition, as a correction or reworking of the old one, may by agreement with the author of the old composition be published as a joint work of "A+B."

An **ideological predecessor** is a composition that either partially coincides in conception or differs in scheme, that is, in the nature of the thematic pieces. A composition having an ideological predecessor may exist independently if, in comparison with the old one, its idea is expressed:

1. with different thematic pieces;
2. in a greater number of variations;
3. in combination with other ideas, correct mates, thematic false trails, or illusory play;
4. with substantial enrichment of the play, improvement of the construction, and so on.

And now a few examples.

**W. Shinkman, 1885.** White: **Kf5, Qb3, Nb6 (3)** and Black: **Kd4 (1)** — mate in three.

The problem is solved by an unusual, hard-to-find first move: **1. Na8! Kc5 2. Ke5 Kc6 3. Qd5#**.

Exactly the same problem was published by E. Sushkevich in the Belarusian newspaper *Zvyazda* in 1954.

**Z. Birnov, 1951.** White: **Kd1, Qg3, Ra8, Rf3, Bb8, Nb6, pawns b4, c5, e5 (9)** and Black: **Kb7, Bg5, Na5, Nd8, pawns c4, e6, e7 (7)** — mate in three.

**1. Qg2 Nac6 2. Rf3** and **3. R3a7#**, **1...Ndc6 2. Rf8** and **3. Ra7#**, and also **1...Be3 2. Rf7** and **1...Kc6 2. Rb3**. A fairly well-constructed problem, with only sixteen pieces, although the first move is not entirely successful. It turned out that White's strongest piece is unnecessary: the queen can quite well be replaced by a bishop, and nothing in the play changes, only the first move becomes worse.

**L. Loshinsky, after Z. Birnov, 1951.** White: **Kd1, Ra8, Rf3, Bd4, Bg2, Nb6, pawn c5 (7)** and Black: **Kb7, Bg8, Na5, Nd8, pawns c7, e6 (6)** — mate in three.

In this position the first move has improved: **1. Be5!**, a third quiet variation has appeared, **1...c6 2. Rd3!**, and three pieces have been saved.

**M. Liburkin, 1981.** White: **Kf2, Rc8, Nf8, pawn b2 (4)** and Black: **Kb5, Nc3, pawns a2, b4, h7 (5)** — win.

**1. Ra8 Nd1+ 2. Ke1 Nc3 3. Ne6 Kc4 4. Nc5 Kxc5 5. Kd2 Nb1+ 6. Kc2 Na3+ 7. bxa3! a1Q 8. axb4+ Kxb4 9. Rxa1**, and White wins.

However, Black can force a draw after *4...Nb5 5. Rxa2 Kxc5 6. Kd2 Kc4 7. Ra8 Kb3 8. Kc1 Nc7 9. Rh8 Ne6 10. Rxh7 Nc5!*

**M. Liburkin, reworked by V. Dolgov, 1983.** White: **Kf2, Rd8, Nf8, pawn b2 (4)** and Black: **Kb6, Ne3, pawns a2, b4 (4)** — win.

Here, without adding material, it proved possible to preserve the author's play: **1. Ra8 Nd1+ 2. Ke1 Nc3 3. Nd7+! Kb5 4. Nc1 Kxc5 5. Kd2 Nb1+ 6. Kc2 Na3+ 7. bxa3 a1Q 8. axb4+** and so on.

**E. Spirin, 1979.** White: **Kc3, Qe8, Re1, Rh5, Bd1, Nb7, Nf2, pawns c4, e5, g3, h6 (11)** and Black: **Ke6, Nd7, Nf7, pawns e7, f5 (5)** — mate in two.

**1. Ne4!** — zugzwang: **1...Nf- 2. Ng5#, 1...Nd- 2. Nec5#, 1...Nfxe5! 2. Nd8#** and **1...Ndxe5! 2. Nbc5#**.

**P. Pechenkin, 1962.** White: **Kb3, Qd8, Rd3, Rh5, Bc1, Na7, Ne2, pawns b4, d5, f3, g6 (11)** and Black: **Kd6, Bd7, Nc7, Ne7, pawn e5 (5)** — mate in two.

**1. Nd4!** with analogous play — this is a strong thematic predecessor.

Well then, now about defects. Here it is necessary to set up the pieces on the board and analyze the positions independently.

A cook is a solution to a problem or study by a method different from the author's and beginning from the very first move. This defect is the most frequent uninvited "guest" in problems and studies alike, both by beginners and by renowned authors. Let us consider a few examples.

**V. Lukyanov, 1982.** White: **Kd1, Rb4, Ba8, Bc7, Nd8, Ne3, pawn c3 (7)** and Black: **Kc5, Na5, Nd4, pawn c6 (4)** — mate in two.

The set play is *1...Na- 2. Nb7#* and *1...Nd- 2. Ne6#*; the solution is **1. Bxc6!** — zugzwang: **1...Na- 2. Rc4#**, **1...Nd- 2. Rb5#**, and **1...Nxc6 2. Nb7#**, **1...Nxc6 2. Ne6#**. The highly complex conception collapses after **1. Rb6!**, that is, because of a cook.

This defect occurs even more often in three-movers.

**I. Storozhenko, 1981.** White: **Kg5, Rg6, Ba4, Ne2, Ng2, pawns c3, e3, f3 (8)** and Black: **Ke5, Qa2, Rb1, Ba1, pawns b5, c7, d6, e6, e7, h4 (10)** — mate in three.

The tries are *1. Nd4 (f4)? Qxg2+!* and *1. Ngf4? Rg1+!*; the solution is **1. Kg4!** with the threat **2. Rg5+!**; **1...Qc4+ 2. Nd4!** (but not **2. Nef4? d4!**) **2...Qa6 3. Nd3#**, and **1...Rb4+ 2. Ngf4! d4 3. Nd3#**.

An excellent key move, and the play of White's knights is subtly differentiated, but **1. Ng3! hxg3 2. Nf4!** is also possible, which is a cook.

**V. Anisimov, 1981.** White: **Kb2, Qc6, Bh7, pawns b4, c2, c3, e4 (7)** and Black: **Ka4, Qa7, Bh5, pawns b5, e6, f3, f7, g7 (8)** — mate in five.

It is very difficult to judge the many-mover section, especially when the fighting power of the opposing sides is great. In such cases the arbiter's persistent attempts to discover defects are always justified, for they are seldom absent here.

According to the author: **1. Bg8!! f2 2. Qxe6! fxe6 3. Bxe6 Q(B)f7 4. Bb3+ Qxb3 5. Bb#**, or **1...Bg4 2. Bxf7 Qa5! 3. Qe5** with the threat of **4. bxa5**, **3...Qa8 4. Qg1! Qd5 5. Qa7#**.

This problem is assigned to the "popular genre of riddle-problems." Unfortunately, the riddle is transparent, since **1. Bf5!** is also possible, and so on.

**V. Novikov, 1980.** White: **Kb5, Rb1, Be8, Nh7 (4)** and Black: **Ka8, Rf2, pawns g3, h3 (4)** — win.

Because of the complexity of the study genre, both in composition and in analyzing the content of the works, finding defects in studies is difficult even for the most authoritative judge or highly experienced solver.

White, balancing on the brink of defeat, conducts the mating attack with precision. The author's solution is **1. Ng5 h2 2. Ne4! g2 3. Bc6+ Ka7 4. Ra1+ Kb8 5. Ra8+ Kc7 6. Ra7+ Kd8! 7. Nd6 Rb2+ 8. Kc4! Rc2+ 9. Kd5 Rd2+ 10. Ke5 Re2+ 11. Kf4! Rf2+ 12. Ke4 Re2+ 13. Kd3! Re3+ 14. Kd4!**, winning, since the diagonal g1-d4 is blocked.

None of the solvers indicated the cook, but it was found: **1. Ka6! Ra2+ 2. Kb6 Kb8 3. Kc6+ Ka7 4. Kc7 Rc2+ 5. Bc6 Rxc6+ 6. Kxc6 g2 7. Nf6!**, with mate not far away.

Such are the wonders that occur in study-solving competitions.

A **dual** is a partial solution, that is, the possibility of solving a problem or study in variations from the second or subsequent moves in a way different from the author's.

A dual in the thematic variations of a problem is inadmissible. In a study, admittedly, one distinguishes admissible and inadmissible duals, although again there is no single opinion on this question. In supplementary, non-thematic variations, duals are allowed, although they unquestionably diminish the aesthetic impression of the work.

The smallest percentage of duals is found in two-movers.

**V. Dubrovsky**, 1979. White: **Kd3, Rd5, Rd7, Bf7, Nf8, pawns b4, d4, e7 (8)** and Black: **Kc6, Ba8, Nd6, pawn b6 (4)** — mate in two.

**1. Ne6!** zugzwang: **1...Kxd5 2. Nd8#, 1...Kxd7 2. e8Q#**, and **1...Bb7 2. R7xd6#; 1...b5 2. R5xd6#**. Also **1...Nb5 2. Nd8#** and **1...Nb7 2. b5#**. But when the black knight retreats indifferently, duals appear, that is, after *1...N-* both *2. Nd8#* and *2. b5#* are possible.

**Yu. Marker**, 1979. White: **Kh7, Qa6, Rd3, Be2, Bg1, Nb6, Nf3, pawns b5, c2, c6, e5 (11)** and Black: **Kc5, Qc1, Ra2, Be8, Nd4, Ne3, pawns a4, b3, b4, d5, f7 (11)** — mate in three.

A strategically very rich problem: **1. Nc8!** with the threat **2. Qb6+!**; **1...Ndf5! 2. Rd4! Nc4! 3. Rxc4#** but there is a dual on *2. Qb6+*; and **1...Nef5! 2. Re3! Nxe2 3. Rc3#**, and again there is the dual *2. Rd2!*

**S. Pivovar**, 1980. White: **Kd1, Qb3, Bf1, pawns b4, b5, b7, c2, c6, e2, g2, h7 (11)** and Black: **Kb1, Rh8, Ba1, pawns b2, c3, c7, e3, f2, g3, g4 (10)** — mate in fourteen.

**1. Qg8! Rxg8 2. hxg8R! Ka2 3. Ra8+ Kb1 4. b6 cxb6 5. b8B! b5 6. Ba7! Ka2 7. Bxe3+ Kb1 8. Ba7! Ka2 9. Bxf2+ Kb1 10. c7 gxf2 11. c8N! g3 12. Na7! Ka2 13. Nxb5+ Kb1 14. Nxc3#** with three successive different promotions, but there are duals leading to mate in twelve: *8. Bd2 cxd2 9. c4 bxc4 10. c7 c3 11. c8Q c2+ 12. Qxc2#* or *10. c3 (e4) c3 11. Bd3 c2+ 12. Bxc2#*.

**Al. Kuznetsov and V. Shanshin**, 1981. White: **Kc2, Nf5, pawns d2, d3, h7 (5)** and Black: **Kb5, Bh8, pawns a3, b2 (4)** — draw.

Here the play is permeated by the idea of a positional draw, based on the motifs of zugzwang and stalemate.

**1. Kb1! Ka4 2. Ka2 b1Q+ 3. Kxb1 Kb3 4. d4,** closing the gaping diagonal.

But Black plays for zugzwang: **4...a2+! 5. Ka1 Ka3 6. d3 Bf6 7. h8Q Bxh8 8. Ng7!** and then falls into zugzwang himself.

**8...Kb3 9. Ne6! Bf6 10. Nc5+**.

The check is a consequence of zugzwang.

**10...Ka3 11. Ne6! Be7** (what else?) **12. Nf4 Bf8 13. Ne2! Bg7 14. Nc3! Bxd4**, and yet it is stalemate with a pin, though now the pinned unit is not a pawn but the knight.

Everything is fine, but there is a dual in the knight's march to c3: *12. Nc7 Kb3 13. Nb5 Bf6 14. Nc3!* and so on.

**A. Maksimovskikh**, 1981. White: **Kc1, Bh3, pawn c6 (3)** and Black: **Ke1, Bb5, pawn a4 (3)** — win.

The play of both sides is not complicated, but very pleasant and symmetrical.

**1. c7 Ba6 2. Kb2! Kf2! 3. Ka3! Kg3! 4. Be6! Kf4 5. Kxa4 Ke5 6. Ka5! Kd6! 7. Kb6! Kxe6 8. Kxa6 Kd7 9. Kb7,** winning.

However, the banal *4. Bf1 Bc8 5. Kxa4 Kf4 6. Kb5 Ke5 7. Kb6 Kd6 8. Be2* also wins, because the white king penetrates to b8, and that is already a dual.

**Unsoundness** is a very rarely encountered defect of chess problems and studies, but as a rule it is also hard to detect. No chess composer is insured against this defect: neither the novice problemist nor the experienced study composer.

**Yu. Sushkov**, 1982. White: **Kh6, Qa1, Rb5, Rf3, Bc4, Bh2, Nc6, Ne2, pawns f2, g2 (10)** and Black: **Ke4, Rd1, Rg4, Ba7, Nh5, pawns b4, c3, f5, f6, g3, g6 (11)** — mate in two.

The set play is *1...Bd4 2. Bd5#* and *1...Rd4 2. Re3#*; the try is *1. N2d4?* with the threat *2. Bd5#*, but not *2. Re3#?*: *1...Nf4 2. Re3#* and *1...Rxd4 2. Qe1#* — and it is refuted by *1...Bc5!*

The author's solution is **1. N6d4!** with the threat **2. Re3#**, but not *2. Bd5#?*: **1...gxf2 2. Bd5#** and **1...Bxd4 2. Qa8#**. Yet this fails because of ***1...f4!***, that is, the problem is unsound and incorrect.

**V. Gladkikh**, 1979. White: **Kh3, Qe5, Rf6, Ba4, Bd4, Nd3, Ng4, pawns c2, c7, g2 (10)** and Black: **Kc4, Qe1, Rf2, Rg7, Bc8, Bh2, Ne6, Nf5, pawns b3, d5, e3, f3, f4, g5, h6 (15)** — mate in three.

**1. Bb2!** with the threat **2. Qd4+** and **3. Nge5#**: **1...N- 2. Rxf5!**, **1...Ned4! 2. Qe6!** and **1...Nfd4! 2. Qf5!**, but after ***1...Rc7!*** the problem has no solution.

**L. Shilkov**, 1981. White: **Kf3, Qa3, Rh6, Be7, Nc8, Nf4, pawns c3, e4, f5, g7 (10)** and Black: **Ke5, Qh7, Rc6, Rd7, Bg6, Nb7, Nf2, pawns e3, h5 (9)** — mate in four.

The author realized a grand conception: **1. Qb4!** with the threat **2. Qb5+ Nc3 3. Qxc5+**: **1...Bf7 2. Rd6! Rxd6 3. Qd4+ Rxd4 4. Bf6#** and **2...Rxd6 3. Bf6+ Rxf6 4. Qd4#**; **1...Qg8 2. Bd6+ Rxd6 3. Qxd4+ Rxd4 4. Nxg6#** and **2...Rxd6 3. Nxg6+ Rxg6 4. Qd4#**; finally, **1...Nd3 2. Qd6! Rxd6 3. Nxd3+ Rxd3 4. Bf6#** and **2...Rxd6 3. Bf6+ Rxf6 4. Nxd3#**. But after ***1...Qxg7!*** the problem is unsound. Thus, what seems a harmless black defense destroys the problemist's splendid conception.

The same befell grandmaster V. F. Rudenko, 1982. White: **Kd7, Qc2, Bf5, Bg5, Nb4, Nd2, pawns d6, f2 (8)** and Black: **Ke5, Ra1, Ra3, Bf1, Bg7, Nc1, Ng3, pawns b7, c4, d4, f3, f7, h7 (13)** — mate in four.

**1. Be6!** with the threat **2. Qe4! Nxe4 3. Nd3!** — interference of Black's pieces with a knight sacrifice on the intersection square — and **4. Nxc4#** and **4. Nxf3#**; **1...Rd3! 2. Nxc4 Ke4 3. Nd2+ Ke5 4. Qc5#** and **1...Bd3 2. Nxf3+ Ke4 3. Nd2+ Ke5 4. f4#** — with interference of Black's pieces without sacrifice of a white piece on the intersection point of the black pieces, the so-called Grimshaw theme, and with the opening of the c- and f-files. Also **1...f5 2. Qd3!** — again interference with a piece sacrifice, see the Novotny theme — **2...Rxd3 3. Nxc4+ Ke4 4. Bd5#** and **2...Bxd3 3. Nxf3+ Ke4 4. Bd5#**.

However, this exceptionally complex logical problem is also unsound: ***1...Re3! 2. fxe3 Bd3!*** and so on.

The solver encounters great difficulty in proving unsoundness in analytical studies, that is, where unsoundness has to be demonstrated by wearisome chains of analysis. See for yourself.

**A. Bor**, 1981. White: **Kd3, Rb8, Bb4, Ne7, pawns d5, f6 (6)** and Black: **Kf7, Rd7, Bd6, pawns c5, h7 (5)** — win.

In the author's solution the combinational battle ends in an economical mate: **1. Rh8! Kxf6 2. Ng8+ Kg6! 3. Bc3 Be5! 4. Bxe5 Rxd5+ 5. Ke4 Rxe5! 6. Kxe5 Kg7** (the rook is trapped, but ...) **7. Nh6! Kxh8 8. Kf6 c4 9. Kf7 c3 10. Kf8 c2 11. Nf7#**.

However, no win is evident after ***3...Rd8***, for example: ***4. Be5 Bxe5 5. Ne7 Kf6 6. Rxd8 Kxe7 7. Rc8 Bd4***, or ***4. Kc4 Re8 5. Bf6 Re3 6. Kb5 Bf8 7. Kc6 Bg7 8. d6! Bxh8 9. d7 Rd3 10. Bxh8 Kf7! 11. Nf6 Ke7***, and so on. Thus the unsoundness of the study is eventually proved after all.

**Impossible Position**. A defect of chess compositions that is encountered only rarely, and one that is hard to detect. It has happened that this flaw in a problem or study was discovered many years later, after the defective work had already been reprinted several times. For example.

**Author unknown, 19th century.** White: **Kh3, Qe7, Ra8, Re2, Bc1, Bf3, Nc7, Nf8, pawn g2 (9)** and Black: **Kg8, pawns f4, f5, f6, f7, h4, h5, h6, h7 (9)** — see text.

This problem once traveled all around the chess world, delighting people with its unusual character. It is called "The Cunning Soldier"; in Russia this position is known under the title "How they used to drive men through the gauntlet under the tsars." The stipulation was to mate with the pawn on g2 in fourteen moves.

**1. Nd7+ Kg7 2. Rf8! Kg6 3. Ne6! fxe6 4. Qf7+ Kg5 5. Ne5! fxe5 6. Be4! fxe4 7. Bc3! fxe3.** One wall of pawns has been shifted.

**8. Qe7+! Kg6 9. Kh2! h3 10. g3 h4 11. g4 h5 12. g5 h6 13. Qf6+ Kh7 14. g6#** and at last the stipulation is fulfilled. The moves 14. Qf7# and 14. Rh8# do not violate the author's conception.

In time, however, a shorter solution to the problem was also found: ***1. Nd7+ Kg7 2. Qf8+ Kg6 3. Ra4 Kg5 4. Nd5 Kg6 5. Ne7+ Kg5 6. Kh2 h3 7. Qg7+ Kh4 8. g3#***.

Unfortunately, this famous composition with such a pawn arrangement is artificial and cannot arise from the initial position of the pieces in a chess game: all eight black pawns are on the board, but where is the pawn from a7? It cannot reach any of the eight squares occupied by its comrades.

Here are several more illegal, impossible, positions.

1. White: **Ke1, Bh1, pawns a2, a3, b2, g2 (6)** and Black: **Ke8, Ba8, pawns a6, a7, b7 (5)** — the positions of the bishops and of the pawns a2, a3, a6, a7 are impossible.
2. White: **Kd1, Qg1, Bf1, pawns e2, f2, g2, h2 (7)** and Black: **Ke8, Ra8, Rb8, Bc8, pawns a7, b7, c7, d7 (8)** — the positions of the white queen and the black rook on b8 are impossible.
3. White: **Kh1, Bf1, pawns e2, f2, g2, h2 (6)** and Black: **Kb8, Bc8, pawns a7, b7, c7, d7 (6)** — the positions of both kings are impossible. Clearly, the kings of the opposing sides could not possibly reach those squares with the pawns and bishop arranged in that way. Naturally, other kinds of incorrect positions are also possible, but they will be discussed later.

Now that you have already mastered and can clearly picture illegal positions, try, as homework, to look for positions that would illustrate impossibility arising from the presence of copies on the chessboard. Are there any such positions?

High demands are placed on White's first move, which usually creates a threat, that is, the possibility of mate requiring Black to defend. As a rule, the threat should be unique, except in cases where a multiplicity of threats is part of the author's conception; see the Novotny, Fleck, Rudenko, Odessa, Sambor, and other themes.

White's moves are crude if they are accompanied by a capture, especially if a black piece rather than a pawn is captured. Capturing a piece on the first move is altogether inadmissible. Checks on the first move of the solution do sometimes occur, but later in the variation there must be at least one quiet move.

It is aesthetically undesirable for the key move to take away a free square from the black king. And if that cannot be avoided, one must provide the enemy king with a new square. Better still, if in the problem new squares are opened for the black king while the previously free ones are not taken away. This requirement is illustrated with exceptional clarity in the following problem.

**L. Knotek. White Memorial Tourney, 1953, 1st prize.** White: **Kb1, Qa1, Bd6, Na8, pawns c4, c7 (6)** and Black: **Kb7, Nd7, pawn c5 (3)** — mate in three.

With the magnificent key **1. Qg7!** White grants the black king no fewer than three free squares, sacrifices the knight, and seemingly removes White's strongest piece, the queen, from the game. Moreover, in the main line White adds a new element of self-weakening, expressed by promoting the pawn to a bishop: **1...Ka7 2. c8B! Kxa8 3. Qa1#**.

To find such a solution, the solver sometimes must exert considerable mental effort, but what pleasure one feels on reaching the goal!

## THEMES OF CHESS PROBLEMS AND STUDIES

The conceptual richness of a modern chess problem is impressive. However, all the diversity of its themes is based mainly on several fundamental ideas, the various combinations of which allow for the most complex combinations.

Not all tactical ideas can immediately enter the content of a problem; sometimes they are barely noticeable and play an additional role, sometimes they fill the entire content of the work, that is, one particular tactical idea is the basis of the author's concept.

In this case, the active side is White, as they hold the initiative and attack. But each of these ideas has its anti-form (Black is active), that is, defensive. There are six of them.

**Blocking** means that a piece occupies a square needed by another friendly piece, or a square near its own king, with the result that the king cannot move to that square. One distinguishes blocking of a free square, simple and complex blocking, with partial or complete shutting off of a long-range piece acting on that square, and obstruction, in problems of more than two moves and in studies.

**Decoying away** means that an enemy piece is drawn away from the square it is guarding.

**Interference** means that the line of action of an enemy or friendly piece is cut off.

**Inclusion** means that in defense a piece leaves the line of action of another long-range piece; one distinguishes simple and complex inclusion, known as the Somov theme.

**Pinning** means that a certain piece cannot move because the opponent's piece would then capture a more valuable piece, that is, because of the action of another long-range piece, for example against the opponent's king, if the screening piece were to move away.

**Unpinning** means that a pinned piece gains freedom of action because, for example, another piece, friendly or enemy, comes onto the square between the pinned piece and the king, indirect unpinning, or because the pinning piece leaves the line of the pin, direct unpinning.

Finally, standing somewhat apart among the defensive ideas in a problem is the idea of a **check against the white king**, that is, a threat against the opponent's king.

These basic tactical ideas have their anti-forms respectively: **unblocking**, **decoying** or **luring**, **shutting off**, **unpinning** or **pinning**, **preventing check**, and so on.

Let us consider these basic tactical ideas by way of examples.

**№ 53. E. Westbury, "Gazette Times", 1911, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 53. Э. Вестбери, «Газетт таймс», 1911, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0053.png)

№ 53. The decoy theme is carried out in five variations. **1. Nd5!** with the threat **2. Re7#**: **1...g5 2. Qxh6#**, **1...Bg5 2. Nxg7#**, **1...Ng5 2. Nf4#**, **1...Rg5 2. Qxe2#**, and **1...Nc5 2. Nxd4#**. However, these mates would not be possible if Black's pieces did not interfere with their own bishop and queen.

**№ 54. H. Bartolovich, "Cik", 1968, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 54. Х. Бартолович, «Чик», 1968, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0054.png)

№ 54. Seven blockings of two squares are achieved here, a record task. **1. Rxc7!** with the threat **2. Rxe4#**; **1...Qxd4 2. Rb7#**, **1...Rbd4 2. Rxa7#**, **1...Red4 2. Rf5#**, **1...Nxd4 2. Nd3#**, **1...Rxf4 2. Nc6#**, **1...Ngxf4 2. h8Q#**, and **1...gxf4 2. Nef3#**.

In the beautifully constructed problem № 55 there are five variations with complex blocking. Complex blocking is a combination in which the weakening caused by a black piece blocking a square for its own king is exploited by shutting off from that square a long-range white piece.

**№ 55. A. Botacchi, "Alfiere di Re", 1921, Mate in 2**  
![№ 55. А. Ботакки, «Альфиере ди ре», 1921, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0055.png)

**1. Qh4!** with the threat **2. Qf6#**; **1...c5 2. Nf5#**, **1...Nc4 2. Nge2#**, **1...Rc5 2. Nd5#**, **1...Rd3 2. Nfe2#**, and **1...Re3 2. Ne6#**. The last three variations, together with the variation **1...Rc6+ 2. Ng6#**, where the black bishop on a8 is interfered with, are created by the play of the same pieces, the black rook and the white battery, which gives them special unity. The black bishop is interfered with twice more: **1...d5 2. Nd3#**, again by the same battery, and **1...c6 2. Re4#**; see IL № 20.

**№ 56. O. Stocchi, Italian Problemists' Union Tourney, 1937, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 56. О. Стокки, Конкурс Итальянского союза проблемистов, 1937, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0056.png)

№ 56. When the free square e6 is blocked, three mates become possible, but each time two of them are parried by the inclusion of a black piece: **1. Bg8!** with the threat **2. Rf5#**; **1...e6 2. Rd4#** but not *2. Nb4#?* or *2. Ra5#?*. **1...Be6 2. Ra5#** but not *2. Rd4#?* or *2. Nb4#?*; and **1...Re6 2. Nb4#** but not *2. Ra5#?* or *2. Rd4#?*.

**№ 57. L. Isaev, "Problemist", 1928, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 57. Л. Исаев, «Проблемист», 1928, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0057.png)

One of the basic tactical ideas, the inclusion of a white piece, is shown in № 57 in three main variations: **1. Nh3!** with the threat **2. Qe6#**: **1...Qd5 2. Nf6#**, **1...Qd6 2. f6#**, and **1...Qxd7 2. Re5#**, united by the geometrically precise mechanism of the black queen's play.

**Complex inclusion**, or the **Somov theme**, is a combination in which the weakening caused by the inclusion of a white piece on a square is exploited by shutting off another white piece from that square.

**№ 58. E. Somov-Nasimovich, "Chess", 1928, 3rd prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 58. Е. Сомов-Насимович, «Шахматы», 1928, 3-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0058.png)

Here in № 58 the Somov theme is expressed in three variations. After **1. f4!**, the defenses to the threat **2. Re5#** lead to the inclusion of white pieces: **1...d6**, the white queen is included on c6, and from that square it becomes possible to shut off the bishop on a4, so **2. Rb5#** follows; **1...Bd6**, because of the inclusion of the white queen on e6 it becomes possible to shut off the white rook on e2, hence **2. Ne3#**; **1...Re6**, because of the inclusion of the bishop on h8 on d4 it becomes possible to shut off the rook on b4, hence **2. c4#**.

As we have already said, pinning can be carried out either by a half-pin or by self-pinning of one's own pieces.

**A half-pin** is a position on the chessboard where two black pieces are situated on the same line between a white long-range piece and the black king, and the departure of one of them from that line results in the pinning of the other.

**№ 59. A. Ellerman, "Good Companion", 1921, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 59. А. Эллерман, «Гуд компеньон», 1921, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0059.png)

№ 59. The half-pin is carried out in five variations and in combination with other tactical ideas: **1. Nd7!** with the threat **2. Nb6#**: **1...Qxe6 2. Rxd6#**, **1...Qxf3 2. Nxf6#**, **1...Bd4+ 2. Nec5#**, **1...Bf4 2. Qd1#**, and **1...Bg3 2. f4#**.

Self-pinning may be direct, when a black piece moves onto the line of the pin, or indirect, when the black king itself moves onto that line.

**№ 60. Z. Birnov, Sverdlovsk Sports Committee Tourney, 1946, shared 1st-2nd prizes, Mate in 2**  
![№ 60. З. Бирнов, Конкурс Свердловского спорткомитета, 1946, 1—2-й призы (на равных), Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0060.png)

№ 60. After **1. Nxd6!** the threat is **2. Rb5#**. Defenses by the black queen lead to its direct self-pin: **1...Qxd6 2. Nc3#**, **1...Qxd5 2. Nc4#**, and **1...Qxe3 2. Nb5#**.

Indirect self-pinning of the same queen is achieved in the line **1...Kxd5 2. e4#**. And in the case of **1...Qe7 2. Ne4#** a disguised, or hidden, self-pin is achieved, because the black piece, having come onto the line of the pin, remained free, and its pinning occurred only after the white piece left that line.

The theme of interference between black pieces is the most widespread in composition and has many variations. For example, interference in combination with decoying was considered above in the two-mover № 53.

**№ 61. I. Shifman, "Brisbane Courier", 1929, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 61. И. Шифман, «Брисбан курир», 1929, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0061.png)

**Interference** of the **Grimshaw** type, without sacrifice on the intersection of rook and bishop, in synthesis with the Novotny theme, where a piece is now sacrificed on the intersection square, is illustrated by the following problem, № 61: **1. Rd5! Rxd5 2. Bc4#** as the threat, and **1...Bxd5 2. Qf3#** as the **Novotny combination**. The main variations, **1...Rd6 2. Nc4#**, **1...Bd6 2. Rd3#**, **1...Re5 2. Nc8#**, and **1...Be5 2. Rb5#**, end in non-standard mates using Grimshaw interference.

Finally, let us consider the special defensive idea of checks against the white king.

**№ 62. K. Mansfield, "Good Companion", 1917, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 62. К. Мэнсфилд, «Гуд компенсьн», 1917, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0062.png)

№ 62. A striking first move unpins the thematic piece and leads to a series of checks against the white king, complicated by various tactical moments: **1. Be4!** with the threat **2. Nxc4#**: **1...Nxd6+ 2. Bd3#**, **1...Ne5+ 2. Rd3#**, **1...Nxe3+ 2. Nb5#**, and **1...Nd2+ 2. Nc4#**.

In conclusion, let us analyze another position by the same author, taken from grandmaster V. F. Rudenko's book *Pursuit of the Theme*. It develops a combination of a whole complex of both attacking and defensive ideas.

**K. Mansfield. "Hampshire Telegraph and Post", 1919, 1st prize.** White: **Kh2, Qe6, Rc1, Rd5, Ba2, Ba5, Na1, Nb3, pawns c6, d2 (10)** and Black: **Kc4, Qc2, Ra4, Rh4, Bf1, Nc3, Ne4, pawns a3, h3 (9)** — mate in two.

The problem is opened by the splendid key **1. Qf5!**, creating the threat **2. Rd4#** thanks to the inclusion of the white queen on b5. The main variations, **1...Qd3 2. Nd4#** and **1...Nb5 2. Nc5#**, end in striking battery mates using at once two weakening ideas, blocking of d3 and pinning of the black queen. Close to these central variations are **1...Nxd5 2. Qxf1#** and **1...Ne2 2. d3#**, with blocking of d5 and pinning of the black queen, interference with the black bishop, and unpinning of the white pawn.

The other two variations are simpler: **1...Qxd2+ 2. Nxd2#**. This is a check against the white king and attraction, a special case of decoying a black piece from the defense of a square by means of its moving directly onto that square.

Now let us become acquainted with themes having special names, although the content of any chess composition is necessarily based on one or another tactical idea, either expressed in one composition in record form, that is, one idea embodied there to the maximum, a task composition, or else several tactical moments enter into the content of the work at once.

So, the themes.

The **Azerbaijani theme**, or the **Vladimirov theme**, is close to the Banny theme, but in it the defenses a-b are answered by mates A-B, the first moves of the tries. The theme is formulated thus: the key moves of the tries become in the solution mating moves in reply to the very moves that had refuted those same tries.

It should be noted that, thanks to a proposal by Soviet grandmaster Ya. G. Vladimirov, after whom the theme had previously been named, justice was restored: its mechanism is now called the Azerbaijani theme, after the place of residence of the three principal authors of the idea, who lived in Azerbaijan.

**R. Aliovsadzade, M. Vagidov, Ya. Vladimirov, and I. Lukimovich. "Chess", 1977, special prize.** White: **Kd7, Ra4, Rd1, Bd3, Nc2, Nd2, pawns e2, e7, f3, g7 (10)** and Black: **Kd5, Rb4, Rg4, Ba5, pawns c5, c7, e5 (7)** — mate in two.

The tries by white knight moves *1. Nc4?* (A) and *1. Ne4?* (B) are refuted by the black pawn moves *1...e4!* (a) and *1...c4!* (b) respectively. In the solution **1. Bb5!** with the threat **2. Bc6#**, those same black moves are answered by the thematic battery mates **1...e4 (a) 2. Nc4# (A)** and **1...c4 (b) 2. Ne4# (B)**, with alternation of tries and defenses.

**Albino** is the fourfold play of a white pawn standing on its initial square.

**№ 63. H. Bartolovich and N. Petrovich, Spanish Tourney, 1963, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 63. Х. Бартолович и Н. Петрович, Испанский конкурс, 1963, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0063.png)

In the two-mover № 63, two Albino mechanisms are used in the tries: *1. fxe? N4e5!*, *1. f3? exd!*, *1. f4? N6e5!*, *1. fxg? Bxg3+!*, *1. dxc? b3!*, *1. d3? Ra6+!*, *1. dxe? N4-!*, and finally the solution **1. d4!** with the threat **2. Qxg4#**: **1...N4- 2. Qxe3#**, **1...N6e5 (f4) 2. Nf4#**, **1...b3 2. Nxc3#**, and **1...Ra6+ 2. Bxa6#**.

**Anti-Hergart** is a theme discovered by the Soviet composer S. Levman in 1928. It is a defensive idea constituting the anti-form of Hergart interference.

**№ 64. A. Vainshtein, "Pionerskaya Pravda", 1931, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 64. А. Вайнштейн, «Пионерская правда», 1931, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0064.png)

In problem № 64 a synthesis of these two combinations is presented. After **1. Qe8!**, defending against the threat **2. Ndf6#**, Black opens the pinned bishop on g6, counting on the fact that White, in trying to carry out the threat, will unpin it: that is the essence of the anti-Hergart defense. However, the move **1...f5** leads to interference with that same bishop on another diagonal, and White exploits this by playing **2. Nef6#**, with the unpinning of the interfered bishop. This is the Hergart interference theme.

**White correction** is a mechanism synthesizing into an interconnected whole four elements of a problem: an indifferent key move by a white piece, the threat created by that indifferent move, the refutation of the threat, and an exact, correcting move by the same white piece preventing the refutation of its indifferent move.

**№ 65. T. H. Bwi, 1973, Mate in 2**  
![№ 65. Т. Х. Бви, 1973, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0065.png)

№ 65. The indifferent retreat of White's f-knight creates the threat *2. Rf5#*, which, however, is refuted by Black's defense *1...Be6!* White must therefore move the knight more precisely, intending to use the inclusion by the black bishop of the white queen along the fourth rank, for example *1. Ne7* with *1...Be6 2. Nc6#*. Yet again, because the knight has blocked e7 for its own queen, Black has the refutation *1...Rd5!* Nor is *1. Nd4? Bd5!* possible, since d4 is blocked; and *1. Nd6? Rxc7!* also fails, though not *1...Bc6 2. Qd4#*, because d6 is blocked. In the solution **1. Ne3!**, against the defense **1...Be6**, White prepares a new mating reply: **2. Qxf4#**.

**White combinations** are the most important and original discovery of the Soviet problemist M. Barulin, 1927. It is a two-move theme: the choice of White's key move from two or more seemingly equivalent continuations, where the mechanism separating the keys is based on weakening tactical moments for White contained in those moves.

**№ 66. V. Chepizhny, Jubilee Tourney, 1967, 4th prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 66. В. Чепижный, Юбилейный конкурс, 1967, 4-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0066.png)

The white combinations theme is carried out originally in № 66. Here White's threats and tries to use the weakening moment of the refutation are cyclically interconnected in three thematic tries: *1. Rxb5?* with the threat *2. Bf5#* and the refutation *1...Nd4!* there is no *2. Nc5#*, because owing to the anti-critical move of the white rook it is cut off from e5; *1. Bg1?* with the threat *2. Nc5#* and the refutation *1...Bf3!* there is no *2. Nf2#* because of e3; and *1. Rf7?* with the threat *2. Nf2#* and the refutation *1...cxb!* there is no *2. Bf5#* because of f3. The solution is **1. Bg4!** with the threat *2. Nf2#* and the variations **1...Nd4 2. Nc5#** and **1...cxb 2. Bf5#**.

The **Bristol theme**, or path-clearing theme, was discovered by the English problemist F. Healey in 1861. Its essence lies in clearing a line: a piece, moving along its line of action, clears the way for another piece moving in the same direction.

**F. Healey. Bristol Tourney, 1861, 1st prize.** White: **Kh2, Qg6, Rd1, Rf3, Ba1, Nb6, Nf7, pawns a3, c3, d2, d5, g2 (12)** and Black: **Kc5, Bb5, Nb7, pawns a4, c4, f4, g7 (7)** — mate in three. **1. Rh1!** zugzwang: **1...B- 2. Qb1 Bb5 3. Qg1#**.

In three variations of position № 67 the **anti-Bristol theme** is carried out: the black queen, moving toward the bishop, closes its way to the needed square. **1. Qe2!** with the threat **2. Qf3+**: **1...Qc3 2. Nxb7**, **1...Qd4 2. Kxc7**, and **1...Qe5 2. Nf7**, each followed by mates **3. Nb6#** or **3. Ne7#**, because Black has no move **2...Bf6**.

The **Bukovinian theme** is a theme of the non-orthodox genre of chess composition: in a helpmate, where by stipulation Black does not oppose White's intentions as in ordinary problems, but on the contrary helps White to mate the black king in the prescribed number of moves and usually starts the solution, some square near the black king is controlled by a white piece which Black eliminates, but then Black himself blocks the square that had been controlled. The author is the Ukrainian problemist and doctor of physical-mathematical sciences N. I. Nagnibida of Chernivtsi.

**N. Nagnibida. "Probleemblad", 1979, 1st honorable mention.** White: **Kf1, Rg2, Be6, Nd3, Nf7, pawn d6 (6)** and Black: **Kc6, Qh7, Re3, Rf8, Bg4, Ne8, Nf2, pawns b5, b6, d7, e4, e5 (12)** — helpmate in two, with two solutions. **1. Nxd3!** the piece controlling c5 is removed, **1...Rd2 2. Nc5!** now Black himself blocks that same square, **2...Bd5#**. The play is analogous in the second solution: **1. Bxe6! Rg6 2. Bd5! Nd8#**.

The **Hamburg theme** is a logical combination in which the refutation of the main plan in the try and the new defense against the main plan that appears as a result of a preparatory maneuver are both carried out by moves of one black piece, while the preparatory maneuver itself is carried out by a move of another black piece.

**1. Bremer. German Circle Tourney, 1948, 2nd prize.** White: **Ka5, Rb5, Rd5, Na3, Na6, pawns b4, e3, f2 (8)** and Black: **Kc6, Rg7, Bg4, Bh8, Nh2, pawns c3, e4, f7 (8)** — mate in three.

The tries *1. Nc4?* and *1. Rd4?* are refuted by the black rook, respectively with *1...Rg6!* and *1...Rg5!* After **1. f4!**, defending against the threat **2. Rb6+ Kxd5 3. Nc7#**, Black opens the seventh rank for his rook by moving the f7-pawn. However, on **1...f6** the sixth rank is blocked, and after **2. Nc4**, against **3. Rd6#**, the move **2...Rg6** no longer defends, but a new defense by the same rook has appeared, **2...Rd7**, to which White replies **3. Rdc5#**, using the blocking of d7. That is the Hamburg theme.

In the second thematic variation Black first blocks the fifth rank and then blocks b7: **1...f5 2. Rd4 Rb7 3. Rbc5#**.

**Additional defense** is a variety of combinations in tries and consists in the fact that as a result of several black moves the same weakening is created, and several mates become possible.

The selection of the single possible mate from among all these mates is determined by the presence in Black's moves of additional defensive moments making all the remaining mates impossible.

**№ 68. L. Loshinsky, Sverdlovsk Sports Committee Tourney, 1940, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 68. Л. Лошинский, Конкурс Свердловского спорткомитета, 1940, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0068.png)

Here in № 68 the theme is presented in a record, task-like, number of variations, five: **1. Qa4!** with the threat **2. Bd6#**, including the queen on e4: **1...fxe4 2. Qa5#**, **1...Rxe4 2. b8Q#**, **1...Bxe4 2. Qxa1#**, **1...Nxe4 2. Nc6#**, and **1...Qxe4 2. h8Q#**.

**Domination** is control over certain squares of the board which, as a result, become inaccessible to the attacked piece or pieces of the opponent. As a theme, domination is often used by study composers.

**№ 69. R. Réti, "Chess", 1928, 1st prize, Win**  
![№ 69. Р. Рети, «Шахматы», 1928, 1-й приз, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0069.png)

№ 69. **1. Kh6! Be5 2. Kg7! Bh2 3. c4! bxc4 4. e5! Bxe5! 5. bxc4 Bf6+ 6. gxf6 Rh8 7. Kxh8 Kd7 8. Kg8! Ke6 9. Kg7**, and White wins.

It is a beautiful study, whose value lies not only in the final position, where the king dominates the rook, but also in all the preceding subtle play aimed at creating zugzwang. The final position of the study naturally also has great practical significance.

The **Dresden theme** is a logical combination in which the refutation of the main plan in the try and the preparatory maneuver are carried out by moves of one black piece, while the new defense arising against the main plan is carried out by a move of another black piece.

**B. Ebner. "Die Schwalbe", 1973, 2nd prize.** White: **Kh8, Re7, Bh4, Nf1, Nh2, pawn f2 (6)** and Black: **Kf4, Ra3, Ba4, Nb6, pawns c3, f5, h5 (7)** — mate in three.

The carrying out of each main plan is hindered by the black bishop: *1. Ng3* with the threats *2. Ne2#* and *2. Nxh5* fails because of *1...Bd1!*, while *1. Ne3?* with the threat *2. Ng2#* fails because of *1...Bc6!* After **1. Re6!**, the black defenses to the threat **2. Bg3+ Kg5 3. f4#** force an interference with the bishop, for instance by **1...c2**. Now the defense **1...Bd1** no longer prevents the main plan, but gives rise to a new defense, **2...Rxg3**, after which White finishes with **3. fxg#**. This is the Dresden theme.

**Entombment** is a combination in which the line of action of a long-range black piece is restricted by another black piece moving onto that line. If as a result the long-range black piece loses all possibility of movement, that is complete entombment of the piece. In other cases the entombment is partial.

№ 70. **D. Grechkin, "Chess in the USSR", 1931, 2nd-3rd honorable mentions, Draw**  
![№ 70. Д. Гречкин «Шахматы в СССР», 1931, 2—3-й почетный отзывы Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0070.png)

№ 70. **1. Nd6+ Kb4! 2. Ba5+!** A hasty capture of the pawn leads to the loss of the piece: *2. Nxf7? Bb5+ 3. Kb6 Rg6+ 4. K- Rg7*. **2...Kc5 3. Nxf7 Bb5+ 4. Kb7 Rg7 5. b4+ Kc4**.

Against other king moves as well, White follows with **6. Kb6**, attacking the bishop.

**6. Kb6 Rxf7**, stalemate.

Lively introductory play leads to a beautiful stalemate with the white bishop entombed.

**Defense on the attraction square** is a complex of a threat and two or more variations interconnected in such a way that the black defensive moves against the threat become defenses to it in the thematic variations.

**L. Kubbel. "Chess in the USSR", 1939, 3rd honorable mention.** White: **Ke7, Qd3, Rf7, Ne2, pawns b3, b7, d2, d5, f2 (9)** and Black: **Ke5, Rb4, Rg4, Ba3, Bh3, pawns b6, d6, g6 (8)** — mate in three.

After **1. Nc3!**, White threatens to attract the black rooks onto e4: **2. Qe3+ Rge4 3. f4#** or **2...Rbe4 3. d4#**, with self-pinning. Those same black moves form the thematic variations: **1...Rge4 2. b8Q!** and **3. Qxd6#** or **3. Qh8#**, there is no *2...Rbf4*; and **1...Rbe4 2. b8N!** and **3. Nc6#** or **3. Nd7#**, there is no *2...Rgc4*, with mutual interference of the black rooks moving toward one another. The tries *1. b8Q? Rbd4!* and *1. b8N? Rgd4!* fit the content well. This is Rudenko's commentary.

**Defense on the threat square** is a complex of a threat and two or more variations connected in such a way that Black's defenses to the threat are made on the same square onto which the white piece arrives on the second move of the threat.

**№ 71. V. Granzhzhkovsky, "Shakhy", 1947, 1st prize, Mate in 3**  
![№ 71. В. Гранжжковский «Шахы», 1947, 1-й приз, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0071.png)

№ 71. The first three-variation three-mover on this theme. **1. Be8!** with the threat **2. Ng4!** and **3. Nxh6#**: **1...Rg4! 2. Nf7! R- 3. Nxh6#**, **1...Bg4! 2. Nc4! Bh3 3. Nxe3#**, and finally **1...g4! 2. Qd5! Kg5 3. Nxf3#**. The threat and the first two variations end in correct mates.

**Nitvelt defense** is a combination in which Black's defense against the threat is carried out by self-pinning, with the aim that if the threat is carried out White will directly unpin the self-pinned black piece.

In the Nitvelt theme this self-pinning is the weakening moment used by White. The Nitvelt defense also includes the combination in which the defense to the threat is performed by an already pinned black piece moving along the line of the pin, with the same aim: that White should directly unpin that pinned piece if the threat is carried out.

**P. Mussuri. "Problems and Studies", 1929, 2nd prize.** White: **Kc2, Qh3, Re8, Rh4, Bh2, Bh7, Nd3, Nd8, pawns b3, d6, e4 (11)** and Black: **Kd4, Rg6, Bf4, Nb8, Nh6, pawns b4, b5, e3, f2 (9)** — mate in two.

**1. Qd7!** with the threat **2. Qa7#**: **1...Rxd6 2. Ne6#** and **1...Bxd6 2. Be5#**.

**Shifman defense** is a combination differing from Nitvelt defense only in that here indirect unpinning is used instead of direct unpinning.

For the two-move form it is characteristic that the threat against which Black defends by the Nitvelt defense is always executed by the white queen, while the threat against which Black defends by the Shifman defense is carried out by a white direct battery.

**№ 72. L. Isaev, "Chess Leaflet", 1930, Mate in 2**  
![№ 72. Л. Исаев, «Шахматный листок», 1930, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0072.png)

In problem № 72, two black pieces act against the white battery. When the action of one of them is destroyed by Black himself, White mates by eliminating the action of the other: **1. Qc3!** with the threat **2. Ndc5#**; **1...Bxd4 2. Nef4#** and **1...Rxd4 2. Ng7#**. Two more battery plays occur with checks to the white king: **1...Ke4+ 2. Ng5#** and **1...Kxe2+ 2. Ndf4#**.

The principal backbone of the thematic complex in this problem is the play of the battery, and the first two variations are complicated by the original combination of the Shifman defense.

The **Indian theme** is a combination of interference between white pieces on a square over which one of them has first passed, followed by play of the battery formed by those pieces. The theme got its name from the famous position known as the Indian problem, sent from Delhi by the English chaplain G. Loveday and published in European chess magazines in 1845. That problem contained an extraordinarily difficult maneuver and aroused widespread interest among chess players.

**№ 73. J. Kohtz and C. Kockelkorn, "Indian Problem", 1903, Mate in 4**  
![№ 73. И. Котц и К. Коккелькорн, «Индийская задача», 1903, Мат в 4 хода](./diagrams/diagram0073.png)

№ 73. A very interesting four-mover. The Indian maneuver is carried out in two variations: **1. exd c6 2. Ba2 c5 3. Rc4 K... 4. Rxc5#** and **1...e5 2. Rxa4 c4 3. Bxc4 K... 4. Be6#**.

In each case, on the second move, to avoid stalemate, the white long-range piece moves in such a way that it can be interfered with by another piece. Such a move is called critical.

The **Kiev theme** is a synthesis of two themes, white combinations and repeated threat, see black correction.

Kiev chess composers B. Avsharov, E. Samotugov, A. Dzektser, and especially B. Kovalenko of the Kiev region, made a major contribution to the development of the Kiev theme, among others. Yet in fairness it must be noted that the idea of combining white combinations and black correction had been developed much earlier by the German problemist Z. Bremer.

**№ 74. Z. Bremer, "Shakh-Express", 1949, Mate in 2**  
![№ 74. З. Бремер, «Шах-экспресс», 1949, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0074.png)

№ 74. *1. Nc1?* with the threat *2. Nd5#* is met by **N4-!**; *1. Nf2?* is met by **N4- 2. Qc1#**, but then *1...Nd2!*; *1. Nf4?* with the same threat is met by **N6-!**; *1. Ne5?* by **N6- 2. Bg5#**, but then *1...Nf4!*. The correct move is **1. Nc5!**, and White then has no difficulty in carrying out the plan. The delicate construction, four tries, and excellent key move granting the black king a free square all eventually won their due, and the problem was rightly included in the FIDE Album for 1945-1955.

**Valve** is a mechanism formed by two black pieces, one of which, while opening the line of action of the second, simultaneously blocks it along another line, a simple valve; or a mechanism formed by three black pieces, one of which, while opening the line of action of the second, simultaneously blocks a third piece, a double valve.

**№ 75. L. Isaev and S. Levman, "Chess Amateur", 1928, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 75. Л. Исаев и С. Левман, «Чесс аматер», 1928, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0075.png)

№ 75. After **1. Ng5**, defending against the threat **2. Ne6#**, Black opens his queen with the knight: **1...Nb4 2. Nb5#**, **1...Nc3 2. Rd3#**, and **1...Ne3 2. Nf3#**. In these variations interference is carried out against that same queen along other lines, the theme of the simple valve. In the variations **1...Ne7 2. Bc5#**, **1...Nc7 2. Qc5#**, and **1...Nf4 2. Qe4#**, other pieces are interfered with, the theme of the double valve. Thus we have a synthesis of the basic form of the theme with its anti-form.

**Combination in tries** is the choice of the exact mating move based on a mechanism separating two or more possible mates that arise as a result of the weakening caused by Black's move. This separation is ensured either by a defensive tactical moment in Black's move, additional defense, or by a weakening of White directly connected with the attempt to carry out the mating move.

**№ 76. M. Barulin, "Bristol Times", 1930, Mate in 2**  
![№ 76. М. Барулин, «Бристоль таймс», 1930, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0076.png)

№ 76. After **1. Ba5 d3**, the black bishop on f4 is pinned and the frontal battery can mate. The bishop on c6 has two possibilities for opening the rook, but the move **2. Be4?** is only a try because it unpins the black bishop; only **2. Bf3#** solves. Analogously, after **1...Bc1**, only **2. e3#** mates, not **2. e4#?**, because the black pawn would be unpinned.

**Knight's wheel** is the mechanism of the eightfold play of a white or black knight situated in the central part of the board.

**№ 77. K. Mansfield, British Problemists' Association Circle Tourney, 1958, 2nd prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 77. К. Мэнсфилд, Круговой конкурс Британского союза проблемистов, 1958, 2-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0077.png)

№ 77. In this Meredith, a problem or study with a total of 8-12 pieces, seven of the eight possible retreats of the white knight find subtle refutations in the moves of the black queen: *1. Nf1? Qa2!*, *1. Nd1? Qc8!*, *1. Nc2? Qa1!*, *1. Nc4? Qa5!*, *1. Nd5? Qe8!*, here unfortunately *1...Ng3* also refutes, *1. Nf5? Qd8!*, and *1. Ng4? Qb8!* The last knight move, **1. Ng2!**, solves. In the tries the knight's wheel theme is embodied in an original way.

**Fortress** is a study theme that is a variety of positional draw and consists in the weaker side saving itself by creating an impregnable position. It was proposed by the Soviet study composer F. M. Simkhovich.

**№ 78. I. Khashsk, "Chess in the USSR", 1965, 4th prize, Draw**  
![№ 78. И. Хашск, «Шахматы в СССР», 1965, 4-й приз, Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0078.png)

№ 78. The fortress is formed by bishop and pawns against queen, with the black king shut out of the game. **1. Ne5 e1Q 2. Ng6+ hxg6 3. Bxd5**.

In the resulting position the bishop is equal in power to the queen. The bishop performs two functions: it protects against mate, **Qf1#**, and at the same time blocks the black king.

**3...Qc1 4. Kg2! Qd2+ 5. Kh3 Qd3! 6. Be4! Qd4 7. Bd5 Qf2 8. Bc4 Qg1 9. Bd5**, draw.

The white king is securely confined in a stalemate fortress, while the bishop maneuvers subtly on d5, c4, and e4, keeping the black king shut in all the time.

**Cross** is the mechanism of the fourfold play of a single piece, king, queen, or rook, over equal distances horizontally and vertically.

**№ 79. V. Rudenko, "64 - Chess Review", 1980, 2nd prize, Mate in 3**  
![№ 79. В. Руденко, «64 — Шахматное обозрение», 1980, 2-й приз, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0079.png)

№ 79. **1. Nd5!** with the threat **2. Nb6+ Ke8 3. Rf6#**: **1...Bd6 2. Rxc5+ Ke6 3. Nxg5#**, **1...Bd8+ 2. Rb6+ Kc8 3. Nd6#**, **1...Bf8 2. Rd6+ Kc8 3. Rd8#**, and **1...Bf6 2. Rc7+ Ke6 3. Nf4#**. In reply to the fourfold play of the bishop, the so-called **star**, the mechanism of the fourfold play of one piece over equal distances diagonally, the white rook moves four times, a **rook cross**.

The **Crimean theme** is a synthesis of the Kiev theme with additional defenses, that is, a combination of three themes: white combinations, black correction, and additional defenses.

**№ 80. A. Malyshev, All-Union Sports Committee Tourney, 1948, 2nd honorable mention, Mate in 2**  
![№ 80. А. Малышев, Конкурс Всесоюзного спорткомитета, 1948, 2-й почетный отзыв, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0080.png)

The Crimean theme is presented very clearly in № 80. Threefold blocking of c4 leads to three variations with combinations in tries: **1. Qg7!** with the threat **2. Qd7#**: **1...Qc4 2. f7#**, but not *2. Ne6#?* or *2. Rd5#*; **1...c4 2. Ne6#**, but not *2. Rd5#?* or *2. f7#*; and **1...Nc4 2. Rd5#**, but not *2. f7#?* or *2. Ne6#?*

White blocking combinations appear in the tries *1. Bf7? Qc4!*, *1. Be6? c4!*, and *1. Bd5? Nc4!*

The **Lviv theme** is a cyclic alternation of the types of pieces participating in the thematic variations, as well as of the squares onto which these pieces successively arrive.

**№ 81. V. Zuev, Fifth All-Russian Tourney, 1968, honorable mention, Mate in 2**  
![№ 81. В. Зуев, Пятый – Всероссийский конкурс, 1968, почетный отзыв, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0081.png)

№ 81. After **1. Kg8!** with the threat **2. Qf8#**, the following play arises: **1...Nd3 2. e4#**, **1...e4 2. g4#**, and **1...g4 2. Nd3#**.

It is easy to see that, first, each variation begins with the move by which the preceding one ends; and second, there is complete graphical unity of adjacent moves, regardless of whether White or Black is moving.

**Moscow theme** means that the threat of a double check is parried by a black move containing two defensive ideas.

**V. Shif. Tourney dedicated to the Third International Tournament in Moscow, 1936, 1st honorable mention.** White: **Kc7, Qg2, Ra6, Bb5, Bd8, Nc2, Nh5 (7)** and Black: **Kf5, Qe1, Re4, Re8, Bh7, pawns c3, d4, e7, f2, f4 (10)** — mate in two.

After **1. Ra5!**, White threatens **2. Bd7#** by means of a double check. Black defends by parrying one check through direct line-closing and the other by inclusion of a black piece: **1...e5 2. Ng7#** and **1...Re6 2. Bd3#**. Close to the main content are two additional plays: **1...e6 2. Qg5#** and **1...Re5 2. Nxd4#**, in both cases complicated by blocking.

**Mkhedruli**, which in Georgian means knight-like, is the systematic interaction of knights creating perpetual or partial attack on the king and an indirect threat of winning a piece or a tempo. It was developed by international grandmaster G. A. Nadareishvili of Tbilisi.

**№ 82. G. Nadareishvili, "Ceskoslovensky Sach", 1965, 3rd prize, Win**  
![№ 82. Г. Надарейшвили, «Чехословенски шах», 1965, 3-й приз, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0082.png)

№ 82. **1. Nc8+!**

*1. Nc6+ Kb7* gave White nothing. Now the black king is not allowed to retreat to b7, note the possibility *2. Nd6+ 3. Ne4 h2 4. Nf2*, which must be borne in mind later as well. *1...Ka8* loses quickly because of *2. Nxb6+ Ka7*, if *2...Kb7 3. Nc5+* and so on, *3. Nc8+ Ka8*, if *3...Ka6 4. Nc5+*, *4. Kd6! h2 5. Kc7 h1Q 6. Ndb6#*.

**1...Ka6 2. Nb8+ Ka5 3. Nc6+ Ka4.**

The continuations *3...Ka6 4. Nb4+* and *3...Kb5 4. Nd4+* would allow the knight to reach the dangerous corner too quickly.

**4. Nb6+ Ka3 5. Nc4+ Ka2.**

If *5...Ka4*, then *6. Nb2+ Kb3 7. Nd1 h2 8. Nf2* and so on.

**6. Nb4+ Ka1 7. Nd3**, and White wins.

This is the first example in which the author succeeded in achieving a win on the Mkhedruli theme.

The **Odessa theme** is the alternation in the tries and the solution of at least two threats and two mates in the variations. In other words, the threats here are not merely an auxiliary element, they must be perceived as a variation. It was first developed by Odessa problemists Yu. Gordian and V. Melnichenko in 1967.

**№ 83. Yu. Gordian and V. Melnichenko, "Stella Polaris", 1968, 1st honorable mention, Mate in 2**  
![№ 83. Ю. Гордиан и В. Мельниченко «Стелла поларис», 1968, 1-й почетный отзыв Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0083.png)

№ 83. By unpinning its own pawn with *1. Rg7?*, White threatens two of its promotions, *2. dxc8N#* and *2. d8Q#*. The defenses *1...Qb7* and *1...Qxh8* differentiate the replies of the unpinned white queen, *2. Qc5#* and *2. Qf4#*, but *1...Nb6!* refutes this try.

Therefore White unpins the queen itself with **1. Rg6!**, so that Black, while defending against both thematic threats, **2. Qc5#** and **2. Qf4#**, is forced to unpin the white pawn: **1...Be7 2. dxc8N#** and **1...Ne7 2. d8Q#**.

This problem was probably the first to give an impulse to the development of the idea, although in Novotny interference mechanisms such an alternation had been used earlier as well.

**Refusal of capture** is a theme in studies and many-movers: in the solution there arises a position in which one side can capture an opponent's piece, but the correct course in that position is to refuse the capture and make another move.

**№ 84. V. Savchenko, K. Betins Memorial Tourney, 1968, 1st honorable mention, Mate in 6**  
![№ 84. В. Савченко Конкурс памяти К. Бетиньша, 1968, 1-й почетный отзыв Мат в 6 ходов](./diagrams/diagram0084.png)

№ 84. **1. Kb2! a1Q+ 2. Kc2!! Qb1+ 3. Kd1!!** Black, defending against the threat *2. dxe#*, decoys the king away from c3, but White does not capture the intrusive queen, instead taking shelter on d1, after which dxe+ and Be5# are threatened. Black can delay mate by only one move: **3...Qxc1+ 4. Kxc1**, then **5. dxe+** and **6. Be5#**, or **4...exd+ 5. Kxd2** and **6. e3#**.

**Pickaninny** is the mechanism of the fourfold play of a black pawn standing on the seventh rank.

**№ 85. V. Jorgensen, "Bulletin of the Slovenian Problemists", 1961, Mate in 3**  
![№ 85. В. Иоргенсен «Бюллетень словенских проблемистов», 1961, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0085.png)

№ 85. Here the maximum activity of the black pawn, the Pickaninny theme, is combined with similar play by a white pawn, the **Albino** theme. **1. Qh7!** with the threat **2. Rxg5+! Kf5 3. Qxf5#**. **1...exf 2. Re5++ Kxe5 3. d4#**. **1...e6 2. Ra4+ Kxa4 3. d3#**. **1...exd 2. Nxd6+ Kd4 3. dxc#** and **1...e5 2. Rxf4++ Kxf4 3. dxe#**.

The **Roman theme** is a logical combination in which the refutation of the main plan in the try, the preparatory maneuver, and the new defense against the main plan are all carried out by moves of one and the same black piece.

**№ 86. J. Kohtz and C. Kockelkorn, "Deutsches Wochenschach", 1905, Mate in 4**  
![№ 86. И. Котц и К. Коккелькорн, «Дойчес вохеншах», 1905, Мат в 4 хода](./diagrams/diagram0086.png)

№ 86. The problem that opened a new area of chess-composition themes. The authors dedicated it to their friend living in Rome, which is why the theme received its Roman name.

White has the strong move *1. Qe2!?*, intending then to threaten *2. Bd3* and *3. Qc2#*. But Black can hinder this by **Be7-g5xe3**. To remove the obstacle, White sacrifices the knight and decoys the bishop onto a neighboring diagonal: **1. Nd6 Bxd6**, after which the analogous defense is no longer sufficient: **2. Qe2 Bf4 3. exf Kxd4 4. Qe5#**.

The essence of the Roman theme consists in such a decoying of the black piece that carrying out the analogous defense afterwards leads to decisive weakening. One of the problem's authors, J. Kohtz, regarded this theme as his most important discovery.

In studies the Roman theme was first achieved by the Latvian grandmaster G. K. Mattison, 1894-1932. As an example of the study embodiment of the Roman theme we give a work by the founder of the modern artistic study, Honored Artist of the RSFSR A. A. Troitsky.

**A. Troitsky. "Deutsche Schachzeitung", 1913.** White: **Kf7, Nc2, pawns g2, h2, h6 (5)** and Black: **Kf4, Rf1, pawns a2, h4 (4)** — win.

**1. h7 Kg5+ 2. Ke7!**

But not *2. Kg7? Rb1 3. h8Q Rb7+!*

**2...Re1+ 3. Kd7 Rd1+ 4. Kc7 a1Q 5. Nxa1 Rc1+ 6. Kb7 Rb1+**.

Perpetual check? No, the rook must be transferred to a parallel line.

**7. Nb3! Rxb3+ 8. Kc7 Rc3+ 9. Kd7 Rd3+ 10. Ke7 Re3+ 11. Kf7**, and White wins. See also diagram № 45.

The **Rovno theme** consists in the fact that in the course of the solution a white piece and a black piece exchange places. Obviously this theme requires the minimum number of moves for its realization, three.

In 1968 N. A. Leontyeva, a woman problemist from Rovno, first demonstrated this interesting novelty, which later received the name of the Rovno theme.

**№ 87. V. Lukyanov, "Zmina", 1977, 1st honorable mention, Mate in 3**  
![№ 87. В. Лукьянов, «Зміна», 1977, 1-й почетный отзыв, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0087.png)

№ 87. Here it is complicated by the Dresden theme. The tries *1. Nd6?* and *1. Ne3?* do not immediately lead to the goal because of the corresponding black rook defenses *1...Rg3!* and *1...Rxc6!* First one must force interference with the rook's line by the black knight, which is achieved by **1. c7!** with the threat **2. Na5! Rg3 3. Nc6#**. Now in the thematic variations the white knight exchanges places with its black opponent: **1...Ne3 2. Nd6! Nc4 3. Nf5#** and **1...Nd6 2. Ne3! Nc4 3. Nf5#**.

The **Sambor theme** is one in which a try immediately creates three or more threats which in the solution are transformed into mates against Black's defenses. An obligatory condition is that these mates must be absent in the initial position, that is, they must not already be prepared.

A group of chess composers from the Sambor district of Lviv region worked actively on this interesting and difficult theme under the leadership of the enthusiast of chess composition R. Zalokotsky. Among them were E. Gavrilov, A. Mityushin, I. Soroka, V. Seredinsky, and others. Later the theme was named after their place of residence.

**№ 88. A. Mityushin and R. Fedorovich, "Mat", 1979, Mate in 2**  
![№ 88. А. Митюшин и Р. Федорович «Мат», 1979, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0088.png)

№ 88. Here for the first time the Sambor theme is expressed in the play of a rook battery. After *1. Bf6?* the square d4 is taken under control and this allows White to create immediately three battery mating threats by retreating the rook downward along the e-file: *2. Re3#*, *2. Re2#*, and *2. Re1#*. However, because of *1...Ng5!* all this collapses.

The solution is the symmetrical move of the bishop to the left: **1. Bb6!** zugzwang: **1...Rc3 2. Re3#**, **1...Rc2 2. Re2#**, and **1...Rc1 2. Re1#**. Additional play: **1...N5- 2. Nc7#**, **1...N7- 2. Nf6#**, and **1...Rc4 2. bxc#**.

**Systematic ideas** are ideas in a study, more rarely in a problem, consisting in the fact that one or more pieces in their movement form a certain periodically repeated system.

**G. Kasparyan. M. I. Chigorin Memorial, 1949-1950, 2nd prize.** White: **Kg2, Bf6, Bf7, Nc5, pawn a3 (5)** and Black: **Ke2, Re3, Bb5, Bf8, pawn a7 (5)** — draw.

**1. Bh5+ Ke1 2. Bh4 Kd2 3. Bg5 Bxc5 4. Kf2 Kd3 5. Bg6+ Re4+ 6. Kf3 Bc6 7. a4! Kd4 8. Bf6 Re5+ 9. Kf4 Bd6 10. a5! Kd5 11. Bf7+ Re6+ 12. Kf5 Bd7 13. a6!** — draw.

An analogous position of eight moving pieces is repeated four times, after White's 4th, 7th, 10th, and 13th moves. See also diagram № 35.

The **Tagil theme** is the change of repeated threats with a change of mates against repeated defenses. In the late 1950s a group of Nizhny Tagil chess composers, B. Nazarov, V. Maliy, F. Rossomakho, and Ya. Rossomakho, worked actively on the theme.

**№ 89. Ya. Rossomakho, M. I. Chigorin Memorial Tourney, 1949-1950, commendation, Mate in 2**  
![№ 89. Я. Россомахо Конкурс памяти М. И. Чигорина, 1949–1950. Похвальный отзыв Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0089.png)

№ 89. The try *1. Rc6?* with the threat *2. Qc4#* and the defense *1...N3- 2. Qd3#* is met by the further black possibility *1...Nc5!*, after which *2. Rd6#*, while after *1...Nd4* there would follow *2. Be4#*; this try is refuted by *1...b5!* The correct move is **1. Re4!** with the same threat. Now against the same defenses the mates change: **1...N3- 2. Qb5#**, **1...Nc5 2. Ne3#**, and **1...Nd4 2. Re5#**.

The **Banny theme**, named after the Soviet composer D. Banny, is as follows: the first moves of the tries, A-B, are refuted by the defenses a-b, while in the solution against the defenses a-b there occur mates B-A, that is, moves that in the tries had served as key moves. The key move of one try is repeated in the solution, where it becomes the mating move in reply to the refutation of the second try, and conversely the key move of the second try in the solution mates against the refuting move of the first try.

**№ 90. D. Banny, "Chess in the USSR", 1972, Mate in 2**  
![№ 90. Д. Банный «Шахматы в СССР», 1972, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0090.png)

№ 90. *1. e3? Nc6!* *1. e4? Nxf3!* and **1. Qf1!** with the threat **2. Qc1#**: **1...Nc6 2. e4#** and **1...Nxf3 2. e3#**.

The **Bogdanov theme** is one in which the mating or intermediate moves of the set play become the first moves of the thematic try and of the solution, with obligatory play in them. The author is E. Bogdanov of Lviv.

**№ 91. E. Bogdanov, "Mat", 1981, 3rd prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 91. Е. Богданов, «Мат», 1981, 3-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0091.png)

№ 91. One of the earliest problems on the Bogdanov theme. In the initial position there are prepared mates against black queen moves: *1...Qg8 2. Nf7# (A)*, *1...Qc8 2. Nd7# (B)*, and *1...Qa6 2. Nc6# (C)*. To create the threat 2. Rxe6#, the knight on e5 must move away. But where? *1. Nf7? (A)*, the first mating move of the set play, gives *1...Bg3 2. Ng5#* and *1...Nd8 2. N7d6#*, but *1...Bxf6!* refutes. Then *1. Nd7? (B)*, the second mating move of the set play, gives *1...Bxf6 2. Nxf6#* and *1...Nd8 2. Nc5#*, but *1...Bg3!* refutes.

The solution is **1. Nc6! (C)**, the third mating move of the set play: **1...Bxf6 2. Ng3#** and **1...Nd8 2. Nfd6#**, and so on. Later work on the theme showed that it can be successfully combined with other themes.

**E. Bogdanov and A. Vasilenko, "Glas Lyudu", 1983.** White: **Kh7, Qh5, Rb6, Rd7, Bg8, pawns e6, c7 (7)** and Black: **Kf6, Ra8, Ba1, pawns d6, f7 (5)** — mate in two.

In Meredith form the idea of combining the present theme with alternation of the first move and the threat is expressed. At first everything is ready: *1...Kxe6 2. Rb6xd6# (A)* and *1...d5 2. exf# (B)*. Then comes the try *1. Rb6xd6? (A)* with the threat *2. exf# (B)*; *1...Ra6 2. c8N#* and *1...fxe 2. Rxe6*, but *1...Be5!* Finally the solution is **1. exf! (B)** with the threat **2. Rb6xd6# (A)**; **1...Ra6 2. f8Q#** and **1...Be5 2. Qg6#**.

The **Brabec theme** is one in which in the set play and in the solution, in two variations, the defensive motifs alternate cyclically. It was revealed in 1970 by the Czechoslovak problemist Juraj Brabec.

**J. Brabec. "Slovak Chess Union", 1970, 1st prize.** White: **Kh5, Qc1, Rc4, Rg8, Be8, Be1, Ne7, Nf7, pawns d5, h2 (10)** and Black: **Kf4, Re3, Rf2, Be4, Bg7, Nh3, pawns c7, f3, h7 (9)** — mate in two.

*1. d6?* with the threat *2. Nd5#*: *1...Rd2* (direct defense) *2. Bg3#*, *1...Bd4* (unpinning) *2. Rg4#*, but *1...c6!* Then *1. Bf5?* with the threat *2. Rxe4#*: *1...Rd2* (unpinning) *2. Bg3#*, *1...Bd4* (interference) *2. Rg4#*, but *1...Ng5!* The solution is **1. Nf5!** with the threat **2. Qxe3#**: **1...Rd2** (interference) **2. Bg3#** and **1...Bd4** (direct defense) **2. Rg4#**.

The **Visserman theme** is one in which a white piece captures a black one on a thematic square, which is then used either by occupation of that square or by crossing it with another white piece.

**E. Visserman. "NBBG", 1946.** White: **Kc7, Bf7, Bg5, Nd2, Ne2, pawns f2, h4 (7)** and Black: **Ke5, Nb7, pawns f4, f5, g6, g7 (6)** — mate in four.

**1. Bc4!** with the threat **2. Bxf4+ Kf6 3. Bg5+ Ke5 4. f4#**; the thematic square is f4. **1...f3 2. Nxf3+ Ke4 3. Nd2+ Ke5 4. f4#**; the thematic square is f3.

The **Hergart theme** is the interference with a pinned black piece by another black piece, as a result of which White obtains the possibility of indirectly unpinning the pinned piece.

**№ 92. H. Hergart, "Algemeen Handelsblad", 1917, 3rd honorable mention, Mate in 2**  
![№ 92. Г. Гетгарт, «Альгемайн хандельблад», 1917, 3-й почетный отзыв, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0092.png)
