№ 92. After **1. Ng5!** with the threat **2. Ne4#**, the black knight by **1...Nc5** interferes with its own bishop on b6, so White can unpin it: **2. Nde6#**. The same knight also effects a Hergart interference of the black rook on f2: **1...Nd2 2. Ndf3#**. Additional play: **1...Nxg5 2. Nxb3#** and **1...Qxg5 2. Rxb6#**.

The **Dombrovskis theme**, named after the Soviet composition master, is as follows: in the tries, threat A is refuted by defense a, and threat B by move b. In the solution, against defense a there follows the mate from threat A, and against defense b the mate from threat B. The mating moves from the threats of the tries occur in the solution precisely after the black moves that had previously refuted them.

**№ 93. N. Nadezhdin, Finland Tourney, 1972, 2nd prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 93. Н. Надеждин, Конкурс в Финляндии, 1972, 2-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0093.png)

№ 93. The most successful example of a triple realization of the Dombrovskis theme. First phase: *1. Nd4?* with the threat *2. Be6#* is refuted by *1...c6!* Second phase: *1. Qg4?* with the threat *2. Ne3#* and the refutation *1...e4!* Third phase: *1. Qe3?* with the threat *2. Ne7#* and the refutation *1...Bxd7!*

The solution is **1. Kf6!** with the threat **2. Qxe5#**. Thanks to its tactical richness, the king leaves the pin line and additionally controls e5 and e6, so the former refutations become the cause of the previously threatened mates: **1...c6 2. Be6#**, **1...e4 2. Ne3#**, and **1...Bxd7 2. Ne7#**.

The **Zakman theme** has two meanings: first, in a problem the doubling of pieces by a circuitous maneuver instead of a critical move; second, in a study a black pawn closes the retreat of its attacked bishop.

**№ 94. Z. Birnov, "Chess in the USSR", 1946, Win**  
![№ 94. З. Бирнов, «Шахматы в СССР», 1946, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0094.png)

№ 94. **1. Rg5+ Kf8 2. Rh5 Bc7 3. Kd7 Bb6 4. Rb5 Ba7 5. Ra5 Bb6 6. Ra8+ K- 7. Kc6**, and White wins.

The **Zappas theme** is one in which one square near the black king is controlled by three white pieces. In each of three tries one of the pieces eliminates this control by the key move, and another by the mating move, whether in the threat or in a variation. Black's defense consists in eliminating the control of the third white piece so that the thematic square becomes available to the black king.

**№ 95. B. Zappas, Circular Tourney, 1977, Mate in 2**  
![№ 95. Б. Заппас, Круговой конкурс, 1977, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0095.png)

№ 95. *1. Qh1?* with the threat *2. f4#* and refuted by *1...b6!*; *1. N3e5?* with the threat *2. Nb6#* and refuted by *1...Bf4!*; *1. N7c5?* with the threat *2. Be4#* and refuted by *1...e5!*; and finally **1. N3c5!** with the threat **2. Be4#**. The Zappas theme is realized in the defenses to the threats with the thematic square d4.

The **Caprice theme** was proposed by the Leningrad chess composer Yu. Sushkov and is based on a change in the function of one and the same tactical motif, which in one phase serves a defensive role for parrying White's threat, while in another phase it weakens White as a result of White's key move. For example, if in one phase Black parries the threat by unpinning its own piece, then in the second phase White must unpin that same piece by its own key move.

**№ 96. Yu. Sushkov, "Shakhmaty", 1980, 2nd prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 96. Ю. Сушков, «Шахматы», 1980, 2-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0096.png)

№ 96. White's tries *1. Nh4?* with the threat *2. Qxh2#* and *1. Nxh2?* with the threat *2. Qh4#* are met by the shutting off of White's pieces, the rook on a4 and bishop on a1, respectively by *1...Rc4!* and *1...Qb2!* The excellent key **1. Nd4!** with the threat **2. Ne2#** shuts off both pieces by White itself, which is the essence of the Caprice theme. Black's defenses to the threat are based on hidden shutting off of those same white pieces, accompanied by bringing other white pieces into action on one of the thematic squares: **1...Rc4 2. Qh4#** and **1...Qb2 2. Qxh2#**.

Considering the threats and refutations of the false phases in relation to the solution variations, it is not hard to see that this whole complex also represents the Hannelius theme. One should note the roughness in the presentation of the complex idea: the presence of cramped technical pieces in the lower-right corner of the board and, what is especially unpleasant, the possibility of defending against the threat in the solution not only by the subtle thematic shutting-off *1...Qb2* but also by the straightforward *1...Qb5* or *Qa6*. Comment by grandmaster V. Rudenko.

The **Levman theme** is one in which Black parries the threat by first interfering with the line on which a white piece is to act.

**№ 97. S. Levman, "Problemist", 1932, 2nd prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 97. С. Левман, «Проблемист», 1932, 2-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0097.png)

№ 97. Here the theme is shown in three variations. After **1. Qc5!**, Black parries the threat **2. Qxe7#** by first interfering with the e-file on e6 and thereby freeing e5: **1...e6 2. Rf4#**, **1...Ne6 2. Rxg6#**, and **1...Be6 2. Qxd4#**.

In the first two variations the Levman theme is complicated by unpinning a white piece, while the first and third form a Grimshaw interference. The problem is well constructed in the standard style of the strategic school.

The **Loshinsky theme** is a complex of two or more variations in which a white piece, in reply to a move by an equally moving black piece, moves toward it or pursues it along the same line. See also № 20.

**L. Loshinsky. K. Gavrilov Memorial Tourney, 1947-1948, 1st prize.** White: **Ke1, Qa3, Rg3, Bf3, Bf4, Nb2, Nh4, pawns b5, c4, d3, e7, h7 (12)** and Black: **Kd4, Qa8, Ra6, Rg5, Be4, Na2, pawns a5, f5 (8)** — mate in three.

Here the Loshinsky theme is carried out in a diagonal mechanism, with bishops. **1. Na4!** with the threat **2. Qb2+ Kxd3 3. Be2#**: **1...Bd5**, unblocking e4, **2. Be4**, but not *2. Bxd5? Re6-!*, then **2...fxe 3. Qc5#** or **2...Qc6 3. Nf3#**. **1...Bc6 2. Bd5!**, but not *2. Be4?* because of *2...fxe!*, or *2. Bxc6?* because of *2...Qxc6!*. And **1...Bb7 2. Bc6!**, but not *2. Be4?* because of *2...fxe!*, and not *2. Bd5?* or *2. Bxb7?* because of *2...Re6+!*

The **Mlynka theme** is one in which the defensive motifs of three variations of the set play or a try alternate cyclically in the solution.

**№ 98. K. Mlynka, "Ceskoslovensky Sach", 1977, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 98. К. Млынка, «Чехословенски шах», 1977, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0098.png)

Diagram № 98. *1. Re7?* with the threat *2. Re3#*: **1...Nd4** (inclusion of a black piece) **2. Nd2#**, **1...Ne6** (shutting off the threatened piece) **2. Qxc6#**, and **1...f4** (attack) **2. Qh3#**, is refuted by *1...Re6!* The solution is **1. Rxb4!** with the threat **2. Rf4#**: **1...Nd4** (shutting off the threatened piece) **2. Nd2#**, **1...Ne6** (attack) **2. Qxc6#**, and **1...f4** (inclusion of a black piece) **2. Qh3#**.

The **Montreal theme** is one in which in the solution the white king moves away from the black king and gives it a free square. In doing so, the white king unpins one of its own pieces, which creates the threat. If the black king occupies the square granted to it, mate must be given by another piece, not the one that created the original threat. In the main variations Black pins the unpinned white piece.

**№ 99. A. Kopnin, "Peugeot", 1962, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 99. А. Копнин, «Пежо», 1962, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0099.png)

Diagram № 99. **1. Kf1!** with the threat **2. e3#**: **1...Ke3 2. Rc3#** and **1...Nc3 2. Rd5#**, also **1...Nd6 2. Rc7#** and **1...Nc7 2. Rc6#**.

The **Nissl theme** is one in which White during the solution sacrifices one or another piece and then promotes a pawn to a piece of the same type.

**№ 100. A. Popandopulo, "64", 1971, Mate in 7**  
![№ 100. А. Попандопуло, «64», 1971, Мат в 7 ходов](./diagrams/diagram0100.png)

Diagram № 100. *1. Kg2? Bxe4+!* does not work. The correct play is **1. Bc5! dxc5**, the bishop is sacrificed, **2. d6 c4 3. d7 c3 4. d8B!**, and the pawn also promotes to a bishop, **4...c2 5. Bg5 c1Q 6. Bxc1 B- 7. Bb2#**.

The **Novotny theme** is the compositional theme in which White, by sacrificing a piece on the critical square, causes mutual interference of two long-range black pieces of different movement on the square of sacrifice. In the logical form of the theme there must be two thematic threats after White's move to the sacrifice square. In three- and many-movers the theme may be achieved with critical moves by the black pieces. The theme was developed in 1854, though it has been established that it already occurred in 1844 in a problem by J. Breds.

The combination of mutual interference of two differently moving black pieces without the sacrifice of a white piece is called the **Grimshaw theme**, discovered by Grimshaw in 1850.

**№ 101. C. Mansfield, "Die Schwalbe", 1956, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 101. К. Мэнсфилд, «Ди швальбе», 1956, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0101.png)

Diagram № 101. One of the first examples illustrating the choice of the correct square for realizing the Novotny theme. Tries: *1. f4? e3!*, *1. g3? Nc2!*, and *1. g4? Nxf2!* Solution: **1. f3!** with the threats **2. Qd1#** and **2. Qe3#**, which work against the defenses **1...Rxf3** and **1...Bxf3**. In the variations **1...Bf4 2. Qxe4#** and **1...Rf4 2. Bxb3#**, the Grimshaw theme appears.

If after the interference the interfering piece is decoyed away, as in the Plachutta theme, this is called the **Brunner-Novotny** or **Brunner-Plachutta** theme, discovered by E. Brunner in 1912.

**№ 102. E. Brunner, "Munchener Neue Nachrichten", 1912, Mate in 3**  
![№ 102. Э. Бруннер «Мюнхенер нойе нахрихтен», 1912 Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0102.png)

Diagram № 102. **1. N2e3! Rxe3 2. Rf3+ Rxf3 3. Rb6#** and **1...Bxe3 2. Rb6+ Bxb6 3. Rf3#**.

The **Plachutta theme** is a forced combination of mutual interference of two equally moving black pieces on the square of sacrifice, caused by the sacrifice of a white piece. In the logical form of the theme there must be two thematic threats after White's move to the sacrifice square.

**№ 103. W. Shinkman, "White Rooks", 1910, Mate in 3**  
![№ 103. В. Шинкман «Уайт рукс», 1910 Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0103.png)

Diagram № 103. **1. d5! Qxd5 2. Ra8+ Qxa8 3. Rg8#** and **1...Bxd5 2. Rg8+ Bxg8 3. Ra8#**.

The combination of mutual interference of two equally moving black pieces on one square without the sacrifice of a white piece is the **Wurzburg-Plachutta theme**, discovered by W. Shinkman's nephew O. Wurzburg in 1909.

**O. Wurzburg. "Zlata Praha", 1909.** White: **Kg8, Qb7, Re8, Nf3, Ng7 (5)** and Black: **Kf6, Rc4, Rd3, Na7, Nd8, pawns c7, g4, g5, g6 (9)** — mate in three.

The first example of a Plachutta interference without sacrifice of a white piece on the intersection square. **1. Ne5!** with the threat **2. Qb2!**; **1...Rcd4 2. Qd5! Rxd5 3. Nxg4#** and **1...Rdd4 2. Qe4! Rxe4 3. Nd7#**.

The combination of one black piece interfering with another black piece of the same movement is called the **Golzhausen theme**.

**W. Golzhausen. "Rigaer Tageblatt", 1908.** White: **Kh1, Rd7, Be1, Nf4, Nh5, pawn f3 (6)** and Black: **Ke5, Rb3, Rc5, Ba7, Bb1, Na5, pawns c3, c4, c7, f5, f7 (11)** — mate in three.

*1. Bh4?* does not work because of *1...Rb6!*, threatening *2. Bf6#*. Therefore White first forces Black with **1. Bf2!** to interfere with its own rook on b3 by **1...Rb5**, because that move eliminates the mating threat, and only now carries out the intended idea with **2. Bh4**. The black rook on b3 is interfered with, and the defense **2...Rb6** removes control of d5, after which **3. Rd5#** becomes possible.

The **aiming-point theme** is a logical combination in which one preparatory plan is based on drawing a black piece onto a square controlled both by itself and by another black piece, the aiming point; the second preparatory plan removes the control of that square by the second piece; and the main plan is the destruction by White of the black piece drawn there.

**№ 104. H. Zander, "Kolner Stadt-Anzeiger", 1960, Mate in 3**  
![№ 104. Г. Зандер, «Кельнер штадтанцайге», 1960, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0104.png)

Diagram № 104. White's attempts to place the queen immediately onto the point of mutual interference of the differently moving black pieces, *1. Qb7?* and *1. Qb4?*, are refuted respectively by *1...Nh5!* and *1...Nxe8!*, because in each pair of interferences one mating reply is lacking. Only by drawing the black pieces with **1. Ne4!** to the aiming point e4 does White obtain the possibility of carrying out one of the main plans: **1...Rxe4 2. Qb7! Rxb7 3. fxe#** or **2...Bxb7 3. Nxg7#**, and **1...Bxe4 2. Qb4! Bxb4 3. fxe#** or **2...Rxb4 3. Nd6#**.

The **extended defense theme** is a complicated supplementary defense in which the defensive aspect of Black's move is used not only to separate the mates but also to parry the threat.

**№ 105. I. Birbrager, "Trud", 1950, 2nd prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 105. И. Бирбрагер, «Труд», 1950, 2-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0105.png)

Here in № 105 the extended defense theme is combined with the repeated-threat theme, black correction. After **1. Nd6!**, White threatens **2. Qe4#**. Black includes its pieces by retreating the knights, and repeated threats appear: **1...Nd- 2. Be5#** and **1...Ne- 2. Rd5#**. While parrying these threats, the black knights can play to f4, but then mates become possible by exploiting the interference of black pieces. The exact choice of mate after each knight move is determined by the action of the included black piece: **1...Ndf4 2. Qe3#**, but not **2. Nf5#?**, and **1...Nef4 2. Nf5#**, but not **2. Qe3#?**.

The defensive motif, inclusion of black pieces, not only refutes the initial threat but also provides exact separation of the mates, which is the essence of extended defense. Additional play: **1...Ndc5 2. Qxc4#**, **1...Nec5 2. Nb5#**, and **1...Nc7 2. Nc6#**.

The **Rice theme** consists in choosing one of two systems of thematic variations with a double unpinning of the white piece pinned by the key move, and it is usually based on the mechanism of simple change of play.

**№ 106. D. Rice, British Chess Problem Society Tourney, 1957, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 106. Д. Райс, Конкурс Британского шахматного союза проблемистов, 1957, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0106.png)

Diagram № 106. The first-move capture of the pawn on d5 by the white knight, *1. Nxd5?*, with the threat *2. Qc4#*, creates thematic variations with unpinning of that knight and exact mate separation: *1...Nc5 2. Nb4#* and *1...Ne5 2. Nf4#*, but is refuted by *1...Bc3!* The solution is the queen capture **1. Qxd5!** with the threat **2. Re3#**; the queen is unpinned by the same moves: **1...Nc5 2. Qc4#** and **1...Ne5 2. Qe4#**.

The **Rudenko theme** occurs in a multi-phase problem, with at least two phases, where in one phase several mates are threatened and in other phases those mates occur in the variations. At the same time, the mutual thematic pair, a black move plus the corresponding white mate from one phase, must not recur in other phases, even if the thematic black move in one phase is not a defense to the threat in another phase. Thus, problems with changed play in the form of the Rudenko theme are characterized by a specific link between two phases: the mating moves of the thematic variations in one phase become a multiple thematic threat in the second phase.

**№ 107. V. Rudenko, "Ceskoslovensky Sach", 1956, prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 107. В. Руденко, «Чехословенски шах», 1956, приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0107.png)

The first example of multi-phase change in the form of the Rudenko theme, № 107. The initial set play with blocking, *1...Rxe5 2. Qd7#* and *1...Bxe5 2. Qc4#*, in the try *1. Nd3?* with the threat *2. Rxf6#*, changes into variations with the Grimshaw combination: *1...Re5 2. Nf4#* and *1...Be5 2. Nc5#*, refuted by *1...Rd5!*.

The solution is **1. Re1!** with two thematic threats, **2. Qd7#** and **2. Qc4#**, whose defenses lead to a new change of mates and thematic content: **1...Rxe5 2. Bf5#** and **1...Bxe5 2. Qd6#**.

It should be noted that the initial play is revealed by the emphasizing try *1. Bg8?* with the threat *2. Bf7#*, refuted by **1...Bb8!**

Thus the mates from the apparent play become threats in the solution, and against the constant black defenses the mates change in each phase. Two threats are used here as one of the means of tying together three phases.

The **Rukhlis theme**, or change of defenses, consists in the fact that the same white mating moves are preserved both in the set play and in the actual play, while only the black moves against which those mates follow are changed.

**№ 108. E. Rukhlis, Sverdlovsk Committee of Physical Culture and Sports Tourney, 1946, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 108. Е. Рухлис, Конкурс Свердловского комитета физической культуры и спорта, 1946, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0108.png)

The position in diagram № 108 gave impetus to broad and thorough development of this theme. In the initial position there is a ready network of mates when black pieces interfere on d4, a Grimshaw combination: *1...Rd4 2. Nc3#* and *1...Bd4 2. Qe4#*. There is also the try *1. Bf2?* with the threat *2. Nb6#*, bringing this mechanism into operation and refuted by *1...Re3!*

The key move **1. d4!** creates the same threat. The mating moves **2. Nc3#** and **2. Qe4#** now work against new defenses, **1...Bd3** and **1...Rd3**, again with Grimshaw interference but now on a different square, so the starting point of the strategic combination has shifted from d4 to d3. These two variations illustrate the simple form of the Rukhlis theme. But in the solution there is also a change of mates against the former black moves from the set play: **1...Rxd4 2. Nb4#** and **1...Bxd4 2. Nf6#**.

This variety of the theme, where new mates follow old black moves and old mates follow new defenses, is called the **full form of the Rukhlis theme**. Note that in the West precisely this form is called the Rukhlis theme; if the mates do not change against the former defenses, then it is simply the theme of transference of mates.

The **doubling theme** is the so-called Terton doubling: a long-range piece by a critical move clears a line for another long-range piece, the latter occupies the critical square and then moves in the direction opposite to that of the first piece, making further use of its support. This distinguishes the doubling theme from the theme of simple line clearance, such as Lloyd's, where the piece that clears the line no longer takes part in the subsequent play.

The doubling theme has three varieties.

The **Terton theme**, discovered in 1856, is one where the doubling is carried out with the stronger piece in front.

**№ 109. J. Kohtz and C. Kockelkorn, "Indian Problem", 1903, Mate in 4**  
![№ 109. И. Коц и К. Коккелькорн, «Индийская задача», 1903, Мат в 4 хода](./diagrams/diagram0109.png)

Diagram № 109. An example of Terton doubling of two white rooks. If one tries to double the rooks immediately with *1. Rff5?*, Black has an adequate defense in *1...Kb7*. Therefore the piece already standing on the doubling line must retreat along it to let the second piece pass in front; that is the Terton maneuver: **1. Rg5! Kb7 2. Rff5! f3 3. Bg7 Kc5 4. Rxc5#**.

But what is the true purpose of the move *1. Rg5?* Is it really so important for the solution in what order the rooks stand on the fifth rank? It turns out that the rook move to g5 is made only to open the diagonal for the bishop.

The **Lloyd-Terton theme** is one where the doubling is carried out when the stronger piece gives way to the weaker one. It was discovered by S. Loyd in 1856.

**№ 110. E. Zepler, L. F. Palitsyn Memorial Tourney, 1932, 3rd prize, Mate in 4**  
![№ 110. Э. Цеплер, Конкурс памяти Л. Ф. Палицына, 1932, 3-й приз, Мат в 4 хода](./diagrams/diagram0110.png)

Diagram № 110. Black threatens to stalemate after *1...Kb4+ 2. Rxb4*. White must retreat the queen, removing the attack on c2. However, after *1. Qd6* there follows *1...Ne1! 2. Rd7 Nd3 3. Qxd3*, again stalemate. The rook must be allowed to go in front: **1. Qd8! Ne1 2. Rd7 Nd3 3. Rxd3 Kc2 4. Rc3#**.

The **Brunner-Terton theme** is the name given to Terton doubling of rooks.

**№ 111. E. Brunner, "Akademische Monatshefte", 1910, Mate in 3**  
![№ 111. Э. Бруннер, «Академише монатсхефте», 1910, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0111.png)

Diagram № 111. To release Black from stalemate, White must move the rook from c4. The try *1. Rf4?* does not achieve the aim: *1...Kxc5 2. Rgg4 Kc6 3. Rc4+ Kd7!* Therefore the rook must go farther and make the Terton move **1. Rh4!**, so that later the other rook mates on c4, bringing the bishop on h3 into play on d7: **1...Kxc5 2. Rgg4 Kc6 3. Rc4#**.

The **Stocchi theme** is the three-variation choice of the mating move in the combination theme in tries.

**№ 112. O. Stocchi, Italian Problemists' Union Tourney, 1937, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 112. О. Стокки, Конкурс Итальянского союза проблемистов, 1937, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0112.png)

Diagram № 112. In the problem, by blocking the free square e6, three mates become possible, but each time two of them are parried by inclusion of a black piece: **1. Bg8!** with the threat **2. Rf5#**: **1...e6 2. Rd4#**, but not *2. Nb4#?* or *2. Ra5#?*; **1...Be6 2. Ra5#**, but not *2. Rd4#?* or *2. Nb4#?*; and **1...Re6 2. Nb4#**, but not *2. Ra5#?* or *2. Rd4#?* The supplementary defense is carried out by three black pieces, the bishop on f8, the rook on h4, and the queen on g1.

The **Umnov theme** is a complex of two or more variations in which a white piece moves to the place previously vacated by a black piece.

**№ 113. E. Umnov, VTsSPS Chess Club Tourney, 1938, 1st prize, Mate in 3**  
![№ 113. Е. Умнов, Конкурс шахматного клуба ВЦСПС, 1938, 1-й приз, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0113.png)

Diagram № 113. After **1. Bb4!**, the black knight is threatened with capture in two ways, by bishop or by pawn.

In the thematic variations obstruction and indirect self-pin of black pieces are used successively: **1...Bxd4 2. Qf6! Kxd5 3. Qe5#** or **2...Bxf6 3. Bxc5#**; and **1...Nxd4 2. Qb5! Kxd5 3. Qc8#** or **2...Nxb5 3. Bxc5#**.

The vivid, tactically rich play required considerable technical concessions: the key is weak, since it brings two passive white pieces into action and pins the black knight, and the two threats are unpleasant, especially because the content is confined to two central variations.

The **Fedorovich theme** is one in which the refutations of mating moves in the tries or false trails become, in the solution, defenses to which there follow mates not prepared in the initial position and also impossible in the tries or false trails for some reason caused by the black defense and eliminated by the key move. The author is the Sambor problemist R. Fedorovich, a pseudonym of R. F. Zalokotsky.

**R. Fedorovich. "Chervony Prapor", 1982.** White: **Ka2, Qa4, Rb6, Rf7, Bf8, Ne4, Nf4, pawn f3 (8)** and Black: **Ke5, Qc7, Rg1, Bb7, Ne1, pawns c6, d2, d7, e6 (9)** — mate in two.

To create the threat *2. Qe4#*, the knight on e4 must retreat. But where? If *1. Nd6?*, stopping the pawn d7 from playing d7-d5, there is the reply *1...c5!*, which includes the bishop on b7 on the mating square of the threat and thus eliminates the mate of the threat itself. Trying to preserve the threat, the white knight by its key move stops the other pawn, c6, whose advance refuted the first try: *1. Nc5?*, but then *1...d5!*

Note that after *1...c5* the rook on b6 is included on d6, suggesting the mate *2. Bg7#*, and after *1...d5* the square d5 is blocked, freeing the knight on f4 for the as yet impossible mate *2. Ng6#*. Then we find the solution with the same knight: **1. Ng3!**, interfering with the rook on g1, which allows the previously impossible, that is, anticipated, mates to be carried out freely against the refuting defenses **1...c5** and **1...d5**: **2. Bg6#** and **2. Ng6#**.

The **Fleck theme** has several forms. The first is that after the key move White threatens three mates at once, but by carrying out the thematic defense Black parries all the mating threats except one.

The second is a synthesis of repeated threats and supplementary defense. In the position White carries out a repeated threat, while Black's moves separate the mates exactly, which is supplementary defense.

**I. Banai. "Magyar Sakkvilag", 1941, honorable mention.** White: **Kh1, Qa4, Rd4, Rg7, Bg2, Nd3, Nh5, pawns b3, d6, e5, g4 (11)** and Black: **Ke6, Qa8, Rc7, Rf8, Bd8, Be8, Nc6, Nf7, pawns b4, e4 (10)** — mate in two.

**1. Bxe4!** with the threat **2. Bf5#**: **1...Nfd6 2. Bd5#**, but not *2. Nhf4#?*; **1...Nfxe5 2. Nc5#**, but not *2. Ndf4#?*; and **1...Ncxe5 2. Ndf4#**, but not *2. Nc5#?*.

The third is the **Fleck-Karlstrom theme**: after the key move several threats arise; the number of defenses in the position must equal the number of threats, and in play they eliminate all the threats at once, so the mating moves are different.

**A. Karlstrom. "Problemisten", 1947.** White: **Ka4, Qh6, Rg1, Rg4, Bg7, Na6, Ne7, pawns a2, b2, b3, b4 (11)** and Black: **Kd4, Qe5, Re4, Rh7, Bh8, Na8, pawns a3, a5, d3 (9)** — mate in two.

**1. Nc7!** and four threats: **2. Nb5#**, **Nc6#**, **Ne6#**, and **Nf5#**: **1...Nxc7 2. Qb6#**, **1...d2 2. Qxd2#**, **1...Rf4 2. Qxf4#**, and **1...Rxg4 2. Rxg4#**.

The **Frolkin theme** is the theme of revival in retro-analytic play of at least three promoted pieces.

Note that according to the Chess Code of the USSR, retrograde analysis belongs to the special kinds of composition, and this is understandable, for according to the same Code the basic requirement for every problem or study is the legality of the position. That is, a position is considered legal if it can be reached from the initial arrangement of the pieces by means of a proof game. The verification of a position's legality is called retrograde analysis.

**№ 114. A. Frolkin, "Probleemblad", 1981, 3rd honorable mention, How did the position arise?**  
![№ 114. А. Фролкин, «Проблеемблад», 1981, 3-й почетный отзыв, Как получилась позиция?](./diagrams/diagram0114.png)

Diagram № 114. Retro play: **1. f8Q Bc4 2. Qf5 Qf7 3. Qh1, Qf2, 4. Kh6 Bd3 5. Qc2 Bf5 6. gxf Qg2 7. f6 Qf2 8. f7 Qg2 9. f8B Qf2 10. Bg7 Qg2 11. Bd4 Qf2 12. Bc5 Qg2 13. Bb4 Qf2 14. Kg7 Qd4+ 15. Kf8 Qe5 16. h6 Qg7+ 17. hxg a5! 18. g8Q axb 19. Qf7 b3 20. Qf1 bxc 21. Qd1 cxdN 22. Kg7 Nf2 23. Kf8 Nd3 24. Kf7 Nc5 25. Be8+ Nd7 26. Rxd7+**.

In the retro play four promoted pieces are captured, White's bishop and two queens and Black's knight. This is a record for problems without an additional retro condition.

And here is the critical position of the problem, that is, the position from which the proof of its legality begins.

White: **Kg5, Qb8, Ra1, Rd6, Bc1, Bd7, Nh8, pawns a2, b2, c3, d2, e2, f7, g4, h5 (15)** and Black: **Kd8, Qe8, Rb7, Rc7, Ba7, Bd3, Na8, Nc8, pawns a6, b6, c6, d5, e6, e7, g6, h7 (16)**. White to move.

The **Hannelius theme**, named after the Finnish problemist Jan Hannelius, is close to the Dombrovskis theme, but in it defense a is met by mate B, and defense b by the mate of threat A. In this theme the mate from the threat of one try appears in the solution after the move that refutes the other try, and vice versa.

**№ 115. J. Hannelius, "Die Schwalbe", 1960, 2nd-3rd prizes, Mate in 2**  
![№ 115. Я. Ханнелиус, «Ди швальбе», 1960, 2—3-й призы, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0115.png)

Diagram № 115. The theme is shown against a background of white correction. First phase: *1. N4-?* with the threat *2. Bc3#* and the refutation *1...Rxc5!*

Second phase: *1. Nd2?* with the threat *2. Rd3#* and the refutation *1...Bxc5!* In the solution phase **1. Nd6!** with the threat **2. Nb5#**, after **1...Rxc5** there follows the mate from the threat of the second phase, **2. Rd3#**, and after **1...Bxc5** the mate from the threat of the first phase, **2. Bc3#**.

In the three-move genre, writes grandmaster V. Rudenko, the mechanism of the Hannelius theme occurred long before its formal discovery. For example, any problem on the obstruction theme with try play necessarily also satisfies the Hannelius theme provided that White's second move is regarded as an intermediate move.

The **Elbe theme** is a complex of two logical themes, Dresden and Hamburg, appearing in independent branches of one and the same variation.

This substantial and technically difficult theme is brilliantly realized in two variations in the following three-mover.

**L. Loshinsky. "Die Schwalbe", 1971, 1st-2nd prizes.** White: **Ka4, Re7, Rg4, Be2, Be5, Na6, Nd4, pawns b6, c7, d3, g3, g6 (12)** and Black: **Kd5, Re3, Rh8, Be1, pawns b4, e4, g7 (7)** — mate in three.

The tries *1. Bd1?* and *1. Rg5?* reveal Black's possibilities of preventing the completion of White's main plans, that is, the threats *2. Bb3#* and *2. Rd7#*, by corresponding captures on d3.

Therefore the move **1. Kb5!** with the threat **2. dxe+ Rxe4 3. Bc4#** induces mutual obstruction of black pieces on that square: **1...exd 2. Bd1!**, if **2...d2**, then **3. Nxb4#**, the Dresden theme; if **2...Rxe5**, then **3. Rd7#**, the Hamburg theme; **1...Rxd3 2. Rg5!**; if **2...Rxd4**, then **3. Bf4#**, the Dresden theme; if **2...e3**, then **3. Bf3#**, the Hamburg theme.

The **Ukrainian theme** is the cyclic alternation of threats and mates in the variation. This idea was developed by the Kharkiv composer S. A. Shedey in 1967.

**S. Shedey. "British Chess Magazine", 1970, 2nd prize.** White: **Kf6, Qh3, Bb7, Na3, Ne6, pawns c3, c5, c6 (8)** and Black: **Ke4, Bh1, Na1, Na5, pawns g2, h6 (6)** — mate in two.

*1. Ba6?* with the threat *2. Qd3# (A)*: *1...Kd5 2. Qf3# (B)*, but *1...Nc4!*; *1. Nd4?* with the threat *2. Qf3# (B)*: *1...Kd5 2. Qf5# (C)*, but *1...g1N!*; and **1. Nc4!** with the threat **2. Qf5# (C)**: **1...Kd5 2. Qd3# (A)**.

An important point here is the condition that Black's thematic defensive move must be the same in all phases.

**V. Melnichenko. "64", 1968, 3rd honorable mention.** White: **Kh6, Qc7, Rf7, Rh5, Bg5, Bh3, Nd6, Ne1, pawns b2, b5, e6 (11)** and Black: **Kd4, Bd3, Na4, pawns d5, e3, e4, e7, g6 (8)** — mate in two.

*1. Kxg6?* with the threat *2. Nf5# (A)*: *1...Ke5 2. Bxe3# (B)*, but *1...Nc5!*; *1. Rxe7?* with the threat *2. Bf6# (C)*: *1...Ke5 2. Nf5# (A)*, but *1...e2!*; and **1. Rf3!** with the threat **2. Bxe3# (B)**: **1...Ke5 2. Bf6# (C)**.

It should be noted that priority in the first expression of the Ukrainian theme was mistakenly attributed to V. A. Melnichenko on the grounds that he published his composition two years earlier than S. A. Shedey. However, V. A. Melnichenko himself refuted this, recounting the history of the theme's discovery in the book Masters of Chess Composition.

At the same time it must be acknowledged that V. A. Melnichenko was indeed the first to realize the theme, so to speak, in its pure form. For example, in S. A. Shedey's problem, as in many later compositions on the theme, all three mates against the thematic defense are already prepared in the initial position, and the phases merely select among them. When these mates are absent, one can speak of a theme of change in the form of choice.

This original problem idea did not go unnoticed. It was worked on extensively and fruitfully by the Leningrader V. Erokhin, the Odessan Yu. Gordian, the Sverdlovsk composer M. Gafarov, the Volgograd composer Yu. Antonov, the Tambov problemist V. Timonin, and other Soviet and foreign composers. A great number of two-movers on the Ukrainian theme were selected for the FIDE Albums.

**№ 116. Yu. Gordian, V. Melnichenko, V. Chepizhny, and S. Shedey, "British Chess Magazine", 1969, 2nd prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 116. Ю. Гордиан, В. Мельниченко, В. Чепижный и С. Шедей, «Бритиш чесс мэгезин», 1969, 2-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0116.png)

Diagram № 116. A convincing example clearly illustrating a modification of the Ukrainian theme in the cyclic form of the Zagoruiko theme. In all phases the thematic defenses *1...Kd3* and *1...e5* are preserved. In the first set play after *1. Kc6?*, the replies are respectively *2. Qg6#* and *2. Bc2#*, refuted by *1...B-*; in the second set play after *1. Rh3?*, the replies are *2. Nc5#* and *2. Qg6#*, refuted by *1...Bd4!*; and in the solution **1. Re2!**, zugzwang, the replies are **1...Bc2#** and **2. Nc5#**.

**№ 117. M. Velimirovic, Yugoslav Championship, 1977, 1st place, Mate in 3**  
![№ 117. М. Велимирович, Чемпионат Югославии, 1977, 1-е место, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0117.png)

Diagram № 117. Here the same idea is realized in a typically three-move form. The set play *1...Rxb2 2. Be6!* and *3. Bxf5#*, and *1...Qa1 2. c8N!* and *3. Ne7#*, with decoy of the black rook from f1 and the black queen from b4, is changed by the try *1. Bd6?* with the threat *2. Qxf6+*: *1...Rxb2 2. c8N!* and *3. Ne7#*, a decoy of the rook from e1, and *1...Qa1 2. Nf3!* and *3. Nh4#*, a decoy of the queen from b4, but this is refuted by *1...Nd7!*

The cycle of White's second moves is completed by the solution variations **1. Bf4!** with the threat **2. Qxf6+ gxf 3. Rg8#**: **1...Rxb2 2. Nf3!** and **3. Nh4#**, a decoy of the rook from d1, and **1...Qa1 2. Be6!** and **3. Bxf5#**, a decoy of the queen from b5.

The result is a cyclic alternation of three White replies against two persistent black moves.

The **focal theme** is the combination of eliminating the control of a black line piece over one of two squares protected by it along different lines, those squares being called focal.

**№ 118. W. Golzhausen, "Deutsches Wochenschach", 1909, Mate in 3**  
![№ 118. В. Гольцгаузен, «Дойче вохеншах», 1909, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0118.png)

Diagram № 118. In the initial position the black queen is tied to two squares, b7 and b3, called focal squares, on each of which the white knight threatens mate. White's plan is to decoy the queen away from the simultaneous defense of both squares. The immediate attempt *1. Bc4?* is refuted by *1...Qf3!*, the queen using a new nodal point to keep both dangerous squares under control. White first interferes with the third rank by **1. c4!**, and only after **1...dxc4** carries out the main plan with **2. Bc4!**

The **French-Soviet theme** is one in which each thematic try creates weaknesses for White that Black uses in the refutation.

**№ 119. J. Savournen, "Tem-64", 1971, 1st-3rd prizes, Mate in 2**  
![№ 119. Ж. Савурнен, «Тем-64», 1971, 1—3-й призы, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0119.png)

Diagram № 119. The set play is *1...Rf2 2. Bb3#* and *1...b4 2. Ra5#*. The tries are *1. Nf5?* with the threat *2. Qa7#*, refuted by *1...b4!*, and *1. Nc4?* with the same threat, refuted by *1...Rf2!* The correct move is **1. Nd5!** with the same threat: **1...Rf2 2. Ndc3#** and **1...b4 2. Nb6#**.

The **Kharkiv theme**, or the theme of legalization of refutations, was proposed by the Kharkiv problemist A. Mochalkin. Its formulation is that in one phase of a two-move problem the refutation of a try is accomplished by several possibilities simultaneously, while in another phase, try or solution, those refuting moves become thematic defenses.

**A. Mochalkin and S. Shedey. "Khliborob Ukrainy", 1986.** White: **Kh2, Qb4, Ra4, Bh7, Ne3, Ng4, pawns e5, g2, h4 (9)** and Black: **Kf4, Qd4, Re4, pawn f6 (4)** — mate in two.

This is an example illustrating the synthesis of the theme of legalization of refutations of false tries with classical themes.

After *1. Qd6?* with the threat *2. Qxf6#*, Black, while defending, pins its pieces in Nitvelt fashion: *1...Qxe5* and *1...Rxe5*, to which White replies *2. Rxe4#* and *2. Rxd4#*. However, this play is false because of *1...Qxe3!* and *1...Rxe3!* White then corrects its action, creating a battery with **1. Qd2!** and the threat **2. Qf2#**: **1...Qxe3 2. Rxe4#** and **1...Rxe3 2. Rxd4#**, again Nitvelt defenses, while the mates show a Rukhlis change of defenses.

**Black correction**, or the **repeated threat**, is a mechanism synthesizing into one connected complex four elements of a problem:

1. the threat;
2. an indifferent move of a black piece that defends against it;
3. a new mate arising from the weakness caused by that indifferent move and called the repeated threat;
4. an exact move, a correction of the indifferent move, by the same black piece, parrying not only the original threat but also the repeated threat and leading to a new weakness used by White.

**№ 120. L. Gugel, "64", 1929, 6th prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 120. Л. Гугель, «64», 1929, 6-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0120.png)

Diagram № 120. By using the mechanism of black correction, a complex set of tactical ideas is realized in the problem. After **1. Rd3!**, White threatens **2. Nc3#**. To defend against this threat it is enough for the knight on f3 to move away, and the white knight on e2 will be pinned. But at the same time the black rook on e4 also becomes pinned, and a new repeated threat arises, **2. N6f4#**. To defend against this as well, the black knight unpins its rook on f5, which, however, is accompanied by interference among other black pieces: **1...Ne5! 2. Rxd4#** and **1...Ng5! 2. Ne7#**, again with the rook on e4 pinned.

Thus Black's moves contain two defensive moments and, in addition, two weakening ones, so their tactical richness is substantially increased.

The **Swiss theme** is a logical theme whose essence lies in the fact that the replacement of defenses against the main plan is based on the replacement, as a result of a preliminary plan, of the mating move in the main plan itself.

**№ 121. V. Timonin, "Komsomolskaya Pravda", 1971, 1st prize, Mate in 3**  
![№ 121. В. Тимонин, «Комсомольская правда», 1971, 1-й приз, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0121.png)

Diagram № 121. The try **1. Qd7?** with the threat **2. Qa4** is refuted by unblocking h5: **1...R5h4!**, but not **1...R3h4?** because of **2. f6#**. The preliminary maneuver **1. Nd3!** with the threat **2. Ne5+ Kh4 3. Rf4#** gives White the possibility, in reply to **1...R3h4**, to carry out the main plan with a changed mating move: **2. Qd7!** and **3. f6#**, while against the defense **2...Rh3** White executes the initial threat, **3. Qa4#**.

Thus, compared with the try play, there is an alternation of the threat and the mating move in reply to the defense against the threat.

A second thematic complex is: **1. Nd7?** with the threat **2. Qb4#**, but **1...R3h4!**, while if **1...R5h4?**, then **2. Nf6#**. The correct play is **1. Nd3! R5h4 2. Nd7!** and **3. Nf6#**, or **2...Rh5 3. Qb4#**. This is united with the first by mutual obstruction of the black rooks.

The **Shedey theme** is one in which in a multi-phase problem the mating move of White from one phase becomes the mate in each subsequent phase, but now against different black defenses. The author is international master S. A. Shedey of Kharkiv.

**S. Shedey. "Shakhmaty", 1963, 1st-2nd prizes.** White: **Kh6, Qb7, Ra3, Bd1, Be1, Nc3, Nd2, pawns c5, d4, d6, c6, f5, g4 (13)** and Black: **Kf4, Rb8, Rf2, Bb2, Nh3, pawns c6, g2, h4, h7 (9)** — mate in two, with White's thematic move indicated.

**1...Ke3 2. Ne2#** and **1...Kg3 2. Nd5#**. *1. Qxc6?* with the threat **2. Nd5#** is met by *Rb3!*; the correct move is **1. Qg7!** with the threat **2. Qe5#**: **1...Ke3 2. Nd5#** and **1...Kg3 2. Ne2#**.

## CHESS COMPOSER'S WORKSHOP

The reader has become acquainted with the nature of chess compositions, with their formal and artistic requirements, knows some of the themes and ideas most often used by composers, and has in general terms learned the methods of solving problems and studies.

Enriched by this knowledge, a lover of composition, if there is a spark of creativity in him, should naturally try to compose a problem or study himself.

But this does not mean that as soon as he begins constructing a composition it will immediately succeed on the first attempt and come out as interesting and perfect as those he has seen and solved in this book. No recommendations can serve as a universal recipe for a great leap by the beginner.

During the course of a problemist's or study composer's life he encounters a number of astonishing, hard-to-explain phenomena in his own work: one composition is created over several years, another in a single day, and a third requires several weeks or months of development.

What factors determine such phenomena? There are many explanations. It may be a return to abandoned positions or to ideas whose difficult treatment demanded much labor.

There are also happy flashes of inspiration when an idea arises instantly in the form of a finished composition; and in most cases such positions are created whose polishing requires only slight effort and time for the final check for defects and for acceptance of the problem or study as a finished artistic work.

It is necessary to warn at once that teaching someone to compose problems or studies is not easy. Another matter is that one can lead a chess player who wishes to take up compositional creativity toward understanding the technique of composing, partially reveal to him the composer's process of thought, and so on.

Every problemist has his own method of composing. Some build sketches of intended problems in their mind; others must have at least some position before them on the board so that they can see the pieces; still others, when sitting down to compose, set up a ready-made composition, their own or someone else's, and start from it.

Some sufficiently experienced problemists, before building the scheme of a future problem, arbitrarily place the basic pieces on the board.

And here is how vividly the authors Ya. G. Vladimirov, R. M. Kofman, and E. I. Umnov in the book Grandmaster of Chess Composition presented a scene of L. I. Loshinsky composing problems.

"To an outside observer the initial stages of work on a problem must have seemed incomprehensible and even absurd. Loshinsky would take batya, as he affectionately called the black king, place him on the board, and then, running his fingers over it, estimate the possible moves of White and Black.

Sometimes the king remained alone for several minutes. Then pieces would shower onto the board like hail, moving quickly and being rearranged... then suddenly all of them, except of course batya, would be removed from the board, and everything would begin again from the start..."

International grandmaster V. F. Rudenko instantly grasps the scheme of one design or another. It is said that during preparation for the match of composers of the socialist countries, members of the Soviet team spent a long time struggling over the scheme of a two-mover on the Novotny theme.

Then Valentin Fedorovich came up, glanced at the board, thought for a moment and stepped out onto the balcony. Three or four minutes later he returned to the board and quickly set up a finished problem in which our design was fully realized, and with such interesting additional variations that the members of the team had not even suspected them."

Nevertheless, we recommend that the beginner first of all sketch on the board the subject of the future problem, the striking force of the scheme, the design as it will work in play.

For example, you intend to present battery mates with shut-off of the black queen along several lines after its critical moves. You are not yet planning the number of variations, but for their supposed maximum let us centralize the battery-queen basis: White, **Rd2, Nd3**; Black, **Kd6, Qd4**. Now, if we can make the black queen move to all six sides, the following variations may result: **1...Qa1 2. Nb2#**, **1...Qg7 2. Ne5#**, **1...Qa4 2. Nf4#**, **1...Qa4 2. Nb4#**, **1...Qa7 2. Nc5#**, and **1...Qg1 2. Nf2#**. But how can this be carried out?

Clearly a threat must be created, otherwise the queen will not obey. Most realistic is a threat from d7, say by the queen. We try to take away the necessary squares from the black king. Let us place the white queen on b7, where it both holds the position well and aims at the threat square. On g6 we pin a white knight, and we take away e6, say, with a bishop from g8.

But how are we to hold c5 without closing the path of the black queen? That square will be covered by a bishop on g1; at once we also place a black bishop on a3 so that its white counterpart does not happen to mate on c5, and on g3 a black pawn to prevent *1...Bh2+*.

It is not easy to create the threat, but if only we could remove the piece from d7... Yes, exactly, let us move the bishop from g8 to d7, since from there it still controls e6. *1. Bc8!* might solve, but there is also *1. Bf5*. We successfully replace the pawn on g3 with a black knight, but then there is a mate in one, *1. Qc6#*, and it is not clear how to get rid of that side solution. The black rook on a6 will parry the threat with *1...Ra7*. Let us shift the whole position to the right, and now even without the pawn and knight we have rid ourselves of *1. Bh2+*. We place the black rook on a6 and add a black pawn on a7.

The problem seems ready: White, **Qc7, Re2, Be7, Bgh1, Ne3, Nh6**; Black, **Ke6, Qe4, Ra6, Bb3, Nh3, pawn a7**.

**1. Bd8!** with the threat **2. Qe7#**, against which the moves **1...Qb7, 1...Qb4, 1...Qh7**, and **1...Qh4** defend, whereupon there follow **2. Nd5#**, **2. Nc4#**, **2. Nf5#**, and **2. Ng4#**, mates by the discovered battery. But **1...Qb1** and **1...Qxh1** are not defenses to the threat.

Thus we have managed to present only four variations out of six in this Meredith problem. But do not hurry to write the position into your notebook, and still less to send the problem off to a newspaper or magazine for publication. Look for defects. It is possible that a cook will be found here as well. And indeed it is so: after **1. Bxe4!** there arise at once three threats of mate, a trial: **2. Bf5#**, **2. Qd6#**, and **2. Bd5#**. To eliminate the cook let us place another black rook, for example on a1. It seems as if everything is now in order. True, the Meredith is gone, but even a so-called damned Meredith, a total of 13 pieces, without white pawns, is not bad either.

But where is the white king?

We must place him on the board, although he is entirely unnecessary for the realization of the idea. Yet since the problem must resemble a position from an actual game, one cannot do without the king.

And now a new concern appears: where to hide the king so that he interferes with no one? In the present situation there is nowhere. We shall have to add more pieces to fence him in. Let us remove the rook from a1, and instead place black pawns on a2, c3, and f2, a black knight on b1, and the white king on a1. The position becomes: White, **Ka1, Qc7, Re2, Be7, Bh1, Ne3, Nh6 (7)**; Black, **Ke6, Qe4, Ra6, Bb3, Nb1, Nh3, pawns a2, a7, c3, f2 (10)**, mate in two.

That is how much material was required to imprison His Majesty. No, the position really must be cleaned up. Moreover, by moving the white king to c1, we can remove the pawn a2 and move the bishop from b3 into its place, take away the knight on b1 and the pawn on f2, and shift the pawn from c3 to c2. Rotating the board by 180 degrees, we move the bishop from a8 to b7 and add a black pawn on b6.

**№ 122. V. Melnichenko, 1982, Mate in 2**  
![№ 122](./diagrams/diagram0122.png)

Now there are no defects, and the position already contains 15 pieces, and it is probably impossible to economize any further. See diagram № 122: the solution is **1. Be1!** with the threat **2. Qd2#**.

Even so, it is hard to believe that four thematic variations are the maximum possible. What if in a fifth variation we use the pinning of the queen?

Here is an approximate scheme: White, **Ka2, Qb2, Rg5, Nc3, Nf5 (5)**; Black, **Kc5, Qe5 (2)**. The threat *2. Qb5#* is parried by the queen moves *1...Qb8, 1...Qe8, 1...Qxc3, 1...Qe2*, and *1...Qh2*, also with pinning, after which come *2. Nd6#*, *2. Ne7#*, *2. Nd4#*, *2. Ne3#*, and *2. Ng3#*. Now all the squares of the black king must be covered, all possible defects must be eliminated, and the key move must be made: *1. Qb2!*

After a little thought we obtain the following position: White, **Ka2, Qd2, Rd8, Rg5, Bh1, Nc3, Nf5, pawns a3, a5 (9)**; Black, **Kc5, Qe5, Rc4 (3)**. The solution can be made by the queen move *1. Qd2-b2*, that is, the queen stands on d2 in the initial position. But d4 must be covered once again so that in the line *1...Qe8 2. Ne7#* there is no *1...Kd4*. This is achieved by placing a white bishop on h8. But then *1. Bxe5* also becomes possible, which is already a cook. The addition of a black bishop on f8 and a knight on a8 eliminates it; the knight, moreover, also parries the second threat, *2. Qb6#*.

The problem is finally ready, and no defects are visible. So that there should not even be a hint of the so-called defense *1...Qxh8 2. Ng7#*, it is desirable to move the bishop from h8 to f6.

**№ 123. V. Melnichenko, 1982, Mate in 2**  
![№ 123](./diagrams/diagram0123.png)

Therefore № 123 is a two-move task: five similar thematic variations are the maximum possible for the chosen scheme.

The process of composing the problem in this case was not difficult. The working scheme, as they say, was already on the board. The rest was a matter of technique, and again not a very difficult one.

You have noticed such common devices in problem construction as rotating the board, shifting pieces right and left, up and down, replacing some pieces with others, and so on. In general, the white queen in a problem is a rather aggressive piece for the key move. In this two-mover it can be replaced by a rook and, after certain rearrangements and acts of taming, so to speak, one can arrive at the following position: White, **Ka2, Qg5, Ra6, Rd2, Bf6, Nc3, Nf5, pawn a3 (8)**; Black, **Kc5, Qe5, Rh6, Bg1, Bh5, Nh1, pawns b5, c4, f7 (9)**, mate in two. Then **1. Rb2!** seems a less obvious key.

**№ 124. C. Mansfield, "Problemist", 1953, 4th prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 124](./diagrams/diagram0124.png)

№ 124 is a knight of the two-move genre. The English international master of chess composition D. Rice, having photographed the process of its birth with a hidden camera, shares his reflections.

"The composer probably began with the following skeleton of the problem: White, **Qe8, Rc8, Rd8, Bd4, Nd7 (5)**; Black, **Kd3, Bh7, Na5 (3)**, where the first move of the solution has already been made. The threat is to be 2. Qe3#, and the thematic defenses 1...Nc4 and 1...Be4 with the mates 2. Nc5# and 2. Ne5#, with complex blocking and mates by a masked battery. Of course, that is insufficient for a good problem. It would be nice if one could also realize a pair of mates with the same tactical motif, namely blocking, say by capturing the thematic bishop on d4.

This idea becomes the starting point of the composer's further work on the problem. Since the scheme includes a white queen, it must be activated. And it naturally suggests itself to use the queen for mating when the free square d4 is blocked. One of the mates is obvious: 2. Qe2#.

For easier control of squares and so on, it is best to rotate the board counterclockwise. Add a black pawn on f5 so that there is a defense 1...fxe with the mate 2. Qg5#.

From where else can the queen mate? From c7? No, then it would simply capture the queen. But by moving the queen to b5 one can play 2. Qxb8#.

Now that the scheme is becoming clearer, it is time to think about the first move. From where can the bishop go to e4, creating the second blocking variation, note that the blocking piece must be only the bishop.

So we arrive at the following position: White, **Qb5, Ra3, Ra4, Ba8, Nb4 (5)**; Black, **Kf4, Bb8, Bh1, Nd1, pawn f5 (5)**, mate in two. **1. Be4!** with the threat **2. Qxf5#** must be the key. But before that we unexpectedly discover another possible mate, **2. Qxb8#**. Fortunately this defect is easily removed by moving the white bishop to b7.

To hold g5, it is best to place the white king on h6. The knight from d1 is moved to f1, where it now interferes with the white rook on e3. The squares g3 and g4 are filled by black pawns. Incidentally, one should avoid an abundance of white pawns and not replace a black pawn by a white one; for example, a white pawn on h3 instead of a black pawn on g4 only spoils the appearance of the composition.

Against capture of the bishop by the king we immediately find four replies. If only there were a black piece acting on a4, it would have to be shut off, and then only a single knight move would mate.

It is easy to see that if the rook on a3 is moved to c3, better than b3 because the pieces are not so crowded on one line, and a black rook is added on a1, then after *1...Kxe4* only **2. Na2#** mates. This mating picture fits the design well: the masked battery becomes a direct one.

Now it is time to search for cooks and other flaws in the position. The queen attack **1. Qe2** threatens both thematic mates, **2. Nd3#** and **2. Nd5#**. The English grandmaster got rid of this defect by adding a black rook on f2 and shifting the bishop to g2 in order to eliminate *1...Rh2+*. Another cook, **1. Bc8**, is eliminated by a black knight on f8.

Only then is the problem ready.

In this form it was published in 1953 in the journal of the British Society of Chess Composers, Problemist, and later awarded the fourth prize.

Its full solution is **1. Be4!** with the threat **2. Qxf5#**: **1...Ne3 2. Nd3#**, **1...Be5 2. Nd5#**, **1...Bxe4 2. Qxb8#**, **1...fxe 2. Qg5#**, and **1...Kxe4 2. Na2#**.

Thus we have traced step by step the birth of a composition. When it is printed and one becomes acquainted with its construction and content, one naturally does not see how many different difficulties the problemist had to overcome, in a word, how much the position had to be turned around and what a variety of technical devices had to be applied.

Like any creative process, the composing of chess problems and studies bears the stamp of individuality, although this process is also subject to general laws.

To create a work of chess art, a problemist or study composer must know the theme of the future composition, choose the optimal schematic framework of the problem or study, accurately select the type of thematic pieces for maximum expression of the design, and determine the number of thematic variations. Finally, calling on his erudition and his entire technical arsenal, he must shape the design with the utmost economy of material and include in the plot of the composition thematic tries.

From my own experience and many years of practice working with beginning problemists, I have come to the conclusion that for a chess player of any qualification who wishes to take up composing problems and studies, the first steps in chess composition should begin with the construction of block problems with a waiting move, including in the content of the opus the simplest tactical ideas, decoy or blocking.

After this, as a rule, there follows the creation of a series of similar works, which will lead the future master, surely and probably unconsciously, according to the basic law of method, from simple to complex, toward composing block problems with a transition from zugzwang to threat and with a change of one mate, two mates, and so on, and most importantly with the inclusion of more complex and interesting ideas such as half-pins, interferences, or checks to the white king.

The ease of perceiving the construction of such works is explained by the fact that in the initial position of such problems White already has mates ready against every black move, and the composer simply has to find such a quiet, extra move after which the whole system comes into motion.

Here it must be taken into account that if the composer decides to complicate the design, this as a rule leads to deterioration of the form of the composition, its construction, its architecture. In other words, the creator of a work of chess art must strive for a harmonious combination of content and form in the problem or study.

For this purpose small tricks may be used, technical devices. They are divided into three groups: constructive, tactical, and thematic.

Constructive devices include such fundamental ones as rotation of the board and shifting of the position, complete or partial, as well as more particular devices such as changing or adding material and pinning pieces. In a study they also include stalemating the king, entombing a piece, constructive preparation of stalemate or mate, and so on.

Rotation of the board by 90 or 180 degrees is carried out in order to change the direction of pawn movement, thereby placing a square or piece under attack or removing it from attack, giving a pawn the possibility of promotion or depriving it of that possibility, and likewise with regard to en passant capture.

Shifting the position, complete or partial, is used to restrict or expand the sphere of action of pieces, and for pawns, to deprive them of the possibility of en passant capture or promotion.

When replacing a piece, for example a stronger piece by a weaker one, queen by rook or bishop, bishop by pawn, and so on, depending on the direction in which the queen or bishop was supposed to act, the composer achieves a certain economy of material, for example by using a pawn to attack a neighboring square on the diagonal rather than the whole diagonal.

A composer must be very cautious in adding material when creating a work. It is justified only if the addition of material considerably enriches the composition with interesting variations that emphasize the idea, making the play subtler, more dynamic, and more substantial.

An intermediate check, or a series of checks, is a case where a piece, before performing its main function in a problem or study, incidentally gives check or a series of checks.

It seems that such devices as constructive preparation of mate or stalemate, the introduction or elimination of capture, sacrifice of a piece, indirect attack, and decoy need no special explanation; they are often encountered in practical play as well.

Of the strategic devices the composer most often employs the transfer of the initiative. A material advantage is also a very popular and distinctive device.

The essence of the device of transferring thematic play lies in what international master of chess composition A. O. Herbstman writes in his book Selected Chess Studies: to make a certain idea that forms the artistic purpose of the work, constituting the basis of its content, into a means of expressing another idea, to use a certain idea as a device.

For example, such a means may be the transfer from the main play of many studies into a thematic try of the idea of pinning a piece, while the goal, the point of the play and its ideological climax, is a completely different idea, the idea of refusal of capture. Or, say, the same idea of pinning is transferred into the introduction, while in the main play the idea of correspondence of squares by pieces is carried out.

Examples of the synthesis of ideas may be the synthesis of a positional draw and stalemate, when for example there is a constructive preparation of a stalemate refuge with the addition of the necessary material for this and the movement of the white king into the area of the coming stalemate, or the synthesis of the ideas of the main play and a thematic try.

Consequently, as regards methods of composing studies, one should probably be guided here by the opinion of the outstanding Czechoslovak grandmaster R. Réti, who said:

"There are two kinds of study composers:

1. those who study interesting positions, single out among them those deserving special attention, and give them an artistic form, economical and pure in aim;
2. those composers who proceed from some concluding position, for example mate, stalemate, zugzwang, and so on, and attach an introductory play to it..."

By the way, the second method of composing studies mentioned by Réti is called the device of retro-analysis, and for its realization it is usually necessary to employ a whole complex of other devices.

Let us consider several examples of these methods of composition. The first two are taken from the magazine 64-Chess Review for 1981.

**Capablanca-Steiner. Los Angeles, 1933.** White: **Kh1, Qh5, Ra1, Rf1, Nh4, pawns a2, c2, c3, d3, e4, g2, h2 (12)**; Black: **Kg7, Qd8, Ra8, Rg8, Ne7, pawns a7, b7, c6, e5, e6, f6, h7 (12)**; White to move.

In this position the former world champion unexpectedly shattered the opponent's defense by a rook sacrifice: **1. Rxf6! Kxf6 2. Rf1+ Kf5 3. Nxf5! exf 4. Rxf5+ Ke7 5. Qf7+ Kd6 6. Rf6+ Kc5 7. Qxb7 Qb6 8. Rxc6+! Qxc6 9. Qb4#**.

**№ 125. B. Olimpiev, J. Beck Jubilee Tourney, 1981-1982, 1st commendation, Win**  
![№ 125. Б. Олимпиев, Юбилейный конкурс Й. Бека, 1981–1982, 1-й похвальный отзыв, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0125.png)

This moment from practical play is already embodied in a study, № 125. Here Black has three extra pawns, forcing White to take emergency measures.

**1. Rxc6! Kxc6 2. Rc2+ Kd6 3. Qb6+ Kd5!** After *3...Ke7* there follows *4. Rc7+ Kf8 5. Qxf6*.

**4. Qb7+! Kd6!** If *4...Kd4*, then *5. Rd2+ Kc5 6. Qc7+ Kb5 7. Rb2+*.

**5. Qc7+ Kd5.** In the event of *5...Ke6* there follows *6. Rc6+ Kf5 7. Qg7 Qg8 8. Rxf6+ Ke4 9. Qb7+ Qd5 10. f3+ Kd4 11. Qb6+ Kc3 12. Rc6+*; or *7...Rxh3 8. Rxf6+ Ke4 9. Qg2+ Kd4 10. Rd6+ Kc3 11. Qxh3+ Kb4 12. Rb6+ Kc4 13. Qb3+ Kc5 14. Qe3+ Kc4 15. Kc1 Kd5 16. Qd2+*, and the black king cannot escape the mating net.

**6. Rd2+ Ke6 7. Rd6+ Kf5 8. Qg7 Qg6 9. Rxf6+ Qxf6 10. Qg4#**.

The next two examples are taken from A. O. Herbstman's book Selected Chess Studies.

White: **Kc2, Ra6 (2)**; Black: **Ke8, Rf4, Nc1, Nc4 (4)** — draw.

Before us is the finale of a study with the idea of a positional draw on the theme of perpetual attack. The knight on c1 cannot retreat to a2, since that square is under the rook's direct attack; e2 is inaccessible to it because of the rook's indirect attack, Re6+ and Rxe2 with draw; and the squares b3 and d3 are controlled by the king.

Since the knight has nowhere to retreat, it must be defended by **Rf1**; then the king attacks the second knight, **Kc3**. It is easy to verify that the knight on c4 is in just as pitiful a condition as its fellow on c1, although it stands free: all the squares available to it, in this case the knight's full complement of eight, a3, a5, b2, b6, d2, d6, are under direct attack, while e3 and c5 are under indirect attack. It has nowhere to retreat.

Consequently Black has only one move preserving its theoretically sufficient material advantage for a win, the move defending the knight on c4, **Rf4**, to which White replies **Kc2** again, with perpetual king attack against both enemy knights. The rook's pendulum, **Rf1-f4-f1**, mimics the opposing king, **Kc2-c3-c2**.

Let us attempt, starting from this position, to build up the moves in reverse order that could have led to it. The knight on c4 stands too freely; it could hardly have been forced there. The knight on c1 is another matter: it could have arrived there from a2, escaping the attack of the white king. Place the white king on a4 and the knight on a2, and a small play results: *1. Kb3 Nc1+ 2. Kc2*, and so on.

The rook's indirect attack, **Re6**, is possible because the black king is on e8. Could one, by placing the king on the edge file of the kingside, decoy it onto that square by rook checks?

Let us place the black king on h7, planning to stop the checks when the king reaches e8. We now have to construct a decoy mechanism so that, as the black king approaches the checking rook, it neither escapes via the sixth rank nor reaches any square other than e8.

One can block the black king's access to the sixth rank by placing on a6 a black minor piece, say a bishop, which in case the king withdraws to the sixth rank will be captured with check. White, correspondingly, will have to add a minor piece to preserve the balance of forces, and try to ensure that it is not merely static material compensation for the bishop but also performs definite functions in the play.

After trying various possibilities, the composer will arrive at the following position: he places a white knight on f4, and the black rook originally occupying that square he moves to d4 so that, since the black bishop on a6 is under attack from the white rook, it constantly attacks the knight on f4. The knight, threatening a fork on e6 or a check on a6, takes from the black king the dark squares h8, g7, f8, and e7, forcing it to proceed under rook checks in a white zigzag exactly to e8. At the same time the knight on f4, like the bishop on a6, becomes bait for the rook and will be taken by it after the white rook has decoyed the king onto e8 and captured the bishop on a6. The rook was moved to a8 after the black bishop had appeared on that square.

Thus the draw: **1. Ra7+ Kg8 2. Ra8+ Kf7 3. Ra7+ Ke8 4. Rxa6 Rxf4**, and then follows what we already know, **5. Kb3 Nc1+ 6. Kc2**, and so on.

Repeating the bait device, let us place a white pawn on h7, move the black king to h6 and the white rook to h8, and we obtain a study whose introduction is lengthened by one move.

**№ 126. Z. Birnov, "Soviet Chess Study", 1955, Draw**  
![№ 126. З. Бирнов, «Советский шахматный этюд», 1955, Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0126.png)

№ 126. **1. Ra8 Kxh7 2. Ra7+ Kg8 3. Ra8+ Kf7 4. Ra7+ Ke8 5. Rxa6 Rxf4 6. Kb3 Nc1+ 7. Kc2 Rf1 8. Kc3 Rf4 9. Kc2 Rf1**, positional draw.

It is quite likely that the artistic thinking of the Volgograd master when developing the idea he found had somewhat different contours, but there can be no doubt that in this case he proceeded from the final position and by retro-analysis, that is, in reverse order, built the introductory play upon it, making use along the way of technical devices such as pursuit of a piece, **Kb3**, bait, of which there are three in the study, the bishop on a6, the knight on f4, and the pawn on h7, decoy, and indirect attack.

And now let us look into the workshop of international grandmaster G. M. Kasparyan.

The position, White: **Kg3, Re7 (2)**; Black: **Kh5, Ng5, pawns g6, h6 (4)**, is remarkable in that mutual zugzwang arises: White to move draws, Black to move wins. The grandmaster recognized it as exceptional and began its further development: he moved the rook to a6, the knight to b7, and the pawn from g6 to h7, adding a white knight on f3 and a black bishop on d7, thus obtaining an interesting introductory play:

**1. Ne5 Be8 2. Re6! Bg6 3. Nxg6 hxg 4. Rb6 Nd8 5. Rd6 Nf7 6. Rd7 Ng5 7. Re7**, with a win.

But after carrying out a light operation consisting in replacing the white knight by a white bishop and making minor rearrangements in the position, the white king to h2, the rook to d8, the black bishop to g2, and the white bishop to b1, he succeeded in lengthening the solution:

**1. Rf8! Bc6 2. Rf6! Be8 3. Kg3 Bg6 4. Bxg6+ hxg 5. Rb6 Nd8 6. Rd6 Nf7 7. Rd7**, and so on.

This play, however, did not satisfy the grandmaster, and he searched again and found: moving the rook to c4 and the black bishop to e2, he achieved the most dynamic play in the study, which at the tourney of the newspaper Uralsky Rabochy in 1946 won the 2nd and 3rd prizes. Here is its final solution.

The disconnectedness of the black pieces, despite material equality, allows White to create mounting pressure culminating in an interesting zugzwang position.

**1. Re4 Bf3**. Black's best chance. The other bishop retreats lead to a quick loss. For example, *1...Bf1 2. Kg3 Kg5 3. Re5+ Kf6 4. Rf5+*; or *1...Bd1 2. Kg3 Kg5 3. Rb4 Nd6 (d8) 4. Rd4*; or finally *1...Ba6 2. Re6 Bc4 3. Re5+ K- 4. Re4* and White wins.

**2. Rf4 Bc6!** Again great care is required from Black in order not to land at once in a hopeless position. For example, *2...Bd1 3. Kg3 Nd6 4. Rd4*; or *2...Be2 3. Kg3 Nd6 4. Rf6*; or *2...Bg4 3. Kg3 Bd7 4. Rf6! Kg5 5. Rb6 Nc5 6. Rd6 Be6 7. Rc6* with a win, and if *5...Na5*, then *6. Rd6 Bc6 7. Re6!*, and White wins.

**3. Rf6!** Tempting but weak is *3. Kg3? Nd6 4. Rd4 Nf7 5. Kf4 Ng5 6. Bf5 Be4!* with a draw.

**3...Be8**. If *3...Bd7*, then *4. Kg3 Kg5 5. Rb6*, winning a piece.

**4. Kg3 Bg6 5. Bxg6+ hxg**. The pursuit of the black bishop has ended with its exchange. Now it seems that Black has achieved some success, doubling the pawns, but White, using the unfortunate position of the black knight, creates new difficulties for Black, and the pawn on g6 will play a negative role.

**6. Rb6 Nd8 7. Rd6 Nf7**. Or *7...Nb7 8. Rd7*, and White wins.

**8. Rd7 Ng5**. Nor does *8...Ne5 9. Rd5* help, or *8...Nh8 9. Rh7*, and so on.

**9. Re7!**, and White wins, since it is Black's move.

Interesting thoughts on composing studies with a static finale were expressed in the article The Technology of the Chess Study, published in the Yugoslav magazine Problem in 1968, by another outstanding Soviet study composer, likewise international grandmaster, V. A. Korolkov.

Suppose, he writes, that we want to construct a study with the following stalemate finale: White, **Ka1 (1)**; Black, **Ba3, Nc3 (2)**. That is, in the final position White to move is stalemated. How could this have arisen? Clearly, neither the black bishop nor the black knight went to their squares voluntarily: the last move bringing them there must have been a capture.

To facilitate creation of the introductory play, let us rotate the board by 90 degrees and obtain the following position: White, **Ka8**; Black, **Nc6, Bc8**. By moving the bishop to a6 and adding a white pawn on c7, we get the concluding move c7-c8Q and Bxa6xc8, stalemate. By adding a black pawn on d7, we see that before this Black's last move must have been Nb8-c6.

Continuing to work in the same way, we can obtain the following study: **V. Korolkov, "Shakhmaty", 1929**. White: **Kb7, Ba4, pawn c6 (3)**; Black: **Kh7, Be4, Nb8, pawns d4, d7 (5)**. Draw: **1. Bc2 d3 2. Bxd3 Bxd3 3. c7 Ba6+ 4. Ka8 Nc6 5. c8Q Bxc8**, stalemate.

Thus a study has been composed satisfying the basic requirements for the construction of a work of chess art. But in working on it we had the possibility of going another way and using other possibilities for creating the introductory play.

What means to choose, what additional material to add, how to unfold the introductory play, all this depends on the composer's creative aims, taste, experience, and finally technique. He must try dozens of possibilities before he finds satisfactory play. Careful analysis is necessary, and great skill is required in order in each position to choose the retro-continuation that opens the greatest possibilities for creating the introductory play.

Of course, a composer does not always move so slowly in work on a study, searching one after another for the preceding moves of each side. No, often, and this method is the most creative, he seeks and finds a long series of moves united by one design and constituting a whole combination, sometimes very complex and deep. Rich imagination and intuition play a great role in this, yet the creative process still rests on the use of the fundamental technical devices of study composition.

For example, how does the struggle proceed in the study just considered? The purpose of the move 1. Bc2 is to unpin the pawn on c6 so as to activate it. For this White sacrifices the bishop, pinning the enemy bishop with the threat of capturing it. To transfer its bishop to a stronger position, Black in turn sacrifices a pawn by 1...d3. White's move 2. Bxd3 is the logical continuation of the combination it has begun, provoking the reply 2...Bxd3, and by playing 3. c7 White creates a double threat by promoting the pawn on b8 and c8. By means of 3...Ba6+ Black attacks the white king and at the same time brings one of the promotion squares under fire, and after 4. Ka8 the move 4...Nc6 removes the second promotion threat as well.

Finally, by playing 5. c8Q White sharply increases its material strength, forcing Black to destroy the newly born queen, which after 5...Bxc8 leads to stalemate. White's design, which consisted in liquidating its own pieces and arranging a stalemate refuge for the king, was carried out thanks to a consecutive series of blows and tactical threats.

Undoubtedly every study has its own special scheme, differing from other schemes, determined chiefly by the pawn structure. The arrangement of the pawns in studies, just as in practical play, is of great importance.

The pawn configuration determines the defensive and attacking possibilities of the position. Analysis of positions with a particular pawn arrangement can reveal the peculiarities of the position and serve as material for the rise and development of study ideas. Development of such structurally related positions can give positive results in the composer's work. What is needed here is painstaking analytical labor.

Let us give an example of the development of related positions, from the moment of discovering the primary idea, through the detection of additional subtleties, to the final shaping of the composition, as carried out by the famous Soviet classic of the pawn study M. A. Zinar.

Once, having noticed a study by A. Votava published in Deutsche Schachzeitung in 1954, White: **Kc6, pawn h2 (2)**; Black: **Ka4, pawns c7, d5 (3)**, win, and having analyzed its solution, **1. Kc5! d4 2. Kc4 d3 3. Kc3 c5 4. h4 c4 5. h5 Ka3 6. h6 d2 7. Kxd2 (c2)** with a win, M. A. Zinar became interested in the curious design of the author. In that study, in the form of a miniature, a triple refusal of capture had been achieved.

Everything would have been splendid, but Zinar noticed a great ideological resemblance to a study quartet by the founder of the pawn study, N. D. Grigoriev, from 1928: White: **Ke1, pawn h2 (2)**; Black: **Ka2, pawn c7 (2)**. Win: **1. Kd2! Kb2!**, if *1...Kb3 2. h4 c5 3. Kc1* with a win, **2. Kd3! Kb3 3. Kd4! Kb4 4. h4 b5+ 5. Ke3!**, the counter-check maneuver, **5...Kb3**, Black having failed to exploit the gain of check loses a tempo, **6. h5 c4 7. h6 c3 8. h7 c2 9. Kd2! Kb2 10. h8Q+**, and Black will not get a queen.

Consequently, in Votava's study one can also continue **2. Kxd4 Kb3 3. h4 c5+** and then follow Grigoriev: **4. Ke3! Kb3 5. h5 c4 6. h6 c3 7. h7 c2 8. Kd2! Kb2 9. h8Q+**, and so on.

If now, in order to get rid of the cook, we block the diagonal a1-h8 by adding pawns, a white one on f6 and a black one on f7, then it turns out that there is no win at all: *7...Kb2 8. h7 c3+ 9. Ke2 c2 10. h8Q c1Q*, draw.

So the square a3 must be made inaccessible. This is achieved by shifting the whole position one file to the right and adding a white pawn on a2 and its black counterpart on a5. But the study again does not solve, because the black king, shifted from a4 to b4, now manages to enter the square of the h-pawn.

This defect too can be removed by adding a white pawn on f5, but then a suspicious queen ending arises after 1. Ke7. Even moving the black pawn to a3 does not equalize the chances, because Black acquires a new concern, counterplay against the pawn on a2.

Let us block the diagonal a1-h8 in another way: place black pawns on e7 and f6, and a white pawn on f5. Now the first refusal of capture disappears, since the pawn on e5 turns out to be protected, and after *1. Kd5* one can also play *1...Kc3 2. h4 Kd3 3. h5 e4*, draw.

Removing the first move of the solution, we arrive at the position: White: **Kd5, pawns a2, f5, h2 (4)**; Black: **Kb4, pawns a5, d7, e4, e7, f6 (6)**.

The solution is **1. Kd4! e3! 2. Kd3! e2 3. Kxe2 Kc3 4. h4!**, after *4. Kd1?* the black king reaches the pawn square, **4...Kc2 5. Ke3!**, that is, now as in Grigoriev's study, **5...Kc3 6. Ke4! Kc4 7. h5 d5+ 8. Kf3! d4 9. h6 d3 10. h7 d2 11. Ke2 Kc3 12. Kd1**, with a win.

The result is Votava's design without its beginning plus Grigoriev's design without its end. Yes, but the price of connecting two truncated ideas was high, five technical pawns. Would it not be better to replace the blocking of the diagonal by lengthening the route of the h-pawn?

In Zinar's study, published in Radyanska Ukraina in 1986, White: **Kd5, pawns a2, a4, b5, g2, h3 (6)**; Black: **Kb4, pawns a5, b6, d7, e4, h6 (6)**, the route of the rook pawn is lengthened. But this cost a pretty penny: there are no longer five but seven technical pawns on the board, two to lengthen the route, and the pawn a4 to eliminate counterplay against the a-pawn.

If you ask why not move the pawn to a7 and do without additional material, you will find the answer in the notes to Black's second move in the solution. It is this: a new technical variation appears. Moreover, the pawns b5 and b6 ensure a win on the fourth move, and the first refusal of capture again had to be abandoned. Although the pawn on e5 would then no longer be protected, if you put it there and the king on d6, the study becomes unsound: *1. Kd5* and after *1...d6! 2. g4 Kc3 3. h4 Kd3 4. g5 hxg 5. hxg e4*, draw.

Correct is **1. Kd4! e3 2. Kd3! e2**.

The play ends in mate after *2...d5 3. g4 d4 4. h4 Kc5 5. g5 hxg 6. h5! g4 7. h6 e2 8. Kxe2 g3 9. h7 d3+ 10. Kxd3 g2 11. h8Q g1Q 12. Qe5+ Kb4 13. Qc3+ Kxa4 14. Qb3#* or *4...Ka3 5. g5 hxg 6. hxg Kb2 7. g6 e2 8. Kxe2 Kc2 9. g7 d3+ 10. Ke3 d2 11. g8Q d1Q 12. Qb3+*, with a win.

**3. Kxe2 Kc3 4. g4.**

One can also play 4. h4, another plus.

**4...Kc2.**

Or *4...Kd4 5. Kf3! Ke5 6. h4*, winning thanks to the remote passed pawn.

**5. Ke3! Kc3 6. Ke4! Kc4 7. h4 d5+ 8. Kf3! Kc3 9. g5 hxg 10. h5! d4 11. h6 d3 12. h7 d2 13. h8Q+**, and White wins.

This is already better, though not quite as in Grigoriev's study, but the decoy of the king into check and the addition of two technical pawns to eliminate unwanted lines fully justify the study composer's work on the design.

Let us continue our examination of simple examples of working over study positions, taken from the creative laboratory of the classic of world chess composition, our compatriot L. I. Kubbel. This article was first published in the collection Problems and Studies nos. 4 and 5 for 1928-1930, but it has not lost its relevance to this day.

For example, we want to build a study with the following stalemate finale: White, **Kd1 (1)**; Black, **Kb3, Qe3 (2)**.

Let us imagine that one move before this position the black queen stood on h3, g3, or c3, and White had a rook somewhere on the e-file, for example on e8. White reduced the play to the indicated stalemate by **Re3+ Qxe3**, stalemate.

Let us now try to deepen this stalemate combination without adding new pieces, in other words let us attempt to devise some sort of introduction using the existing material.

It is not hard to see that with the existing pieces one cannot induce the black queen to move to h3, g3, or c3, nor the black king to b3. If, for example, one places the black king on b2, then after **Re2+** it is under no obligation to go to b3, but may retire, say, to a1. Nor will shifting the entire position to the left, sometimes a very good expedient, help, because then too the black king will go to a1.

Consequently, one must proceed by other means. And here two technical devices come to our aid at once, rotating the board and pawn promotion. Then we obtain the following position: White, **Ka5, Rh4 (2)**; Black, **Kc7, pawn c2 (2)**.
