Here we have the possibility, by moving the white rook from h4 to d6, d8, or b6, b8, of adding one white move, namely **Rd4** or **Rb4**, after which on **c1Q** there follows **Rc1+** with the stalemate we have in mind.

As a result of these manipulations, an important tempo has been gained for lengthening the solution, because Black does not yet have a queen at the start but must create one from the pawn.

**Pawn promotion** is a very valuable and specifically study-like device.

As we have already said, in the position just considered the white rook can be moved to b6, b8, or d6, d8.

In cases where there is a choice among several possibilities, it is necessary to examine the positions carefully in order to select the one that is more convenient for further deepening of the combination.

In the present case the hanging position of the white rook tells against moving it to d6, d8, or b8, whereas the move to b6, where it is protected by the king, gives the possibility of lengthening the play by one more move. This is achieved by moving the rook to d6, better a6, and the black king to b7. Then a little study with a three-move solution already results.

White: **Ka5, Rd6 (2)**; Black: **Kb7, pawn c2 (2)** — draw: *1. Rb6+*, but not *1. Rd7? Kc8!* with a win for Black, *1...Kc7 2. Rb4 c1Q 3. Rc4+ Qxc4*, stalemate.

There is, however, another path to deepen this study if we take as our starting point the position of the black king on c7 and the white rook on b6. Its idea lies in switching to rook side checks. Let us try to realize it by moving the white rook to b7 and the black king to d6.

The solution is *1. Rb6+*, and if now at once *1...Kc7*, then *2. Rb4* and so on. But if *1...Kd5*, then *2. Rb5+ Kd4 3. Rb4+ Kd3 4. Rb3+* with perpetual check, because after *4...Kd2* there follows *5. Rb2*. The trouble, however, is that Black can answer *1...Kd7! 2. Rb7+ Kc8!* and wins.

Here we have an opportunity to use the technical device mentioned at the beginning, shifting the position, but not completely, only partially.

The study is corrected by moving the kings and the rook one square upward, that is, the white king to a6, the black king to d7, and the white rook to b8. At the same time it becomes clear that this new position admits further deepening by moving the white rook to b7 and the black king to d8. We then get the following position: White, **Ka6, Rb7 (2)**; Black, **Kd8, pawn c2 (2)**; draw: *1. Rb8+ Kd7*, if *1...Kc7* then *2. Rb7+ Kc6 3. Rb8!*, *2. Rb7+ Kc8 3. Rb5* and so on, or *2...Kd6 3. Rb6+ Kd5 4. Rb5+* and so on.

Comparing this position with the previous one, we must acknowledge significant progress in handling the chosen theme: the main stalemate variation now comprises four moves instead of three, and in addition side play with perpetual check has been added.

At the same time one must say that, with the given material, it seems impossible to achieve more, but the addition of a single white pawn is enough to pour new content into the study and deepen it considerably.

**№ 127. L. Kubbel, 1928, Draw**  
![№ 127. Л. Куббель, 1928, Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0127.png)

In the study, № 127, the black king can on neither the first nor the second move go to the c-file, c7 or c8, because of the reply **Rb4**, so it sets off on a journey along the d-file.

**1. Rb8+ Kd7 2. Rb7+ Kd6 3. Rb6+ Kd5 4. Rb5+ Kd4 5. Rb4+ Kxd3 6. Rb3+ Kd4 7. Rb4+ Kd5 8. Rb5+ Kd6 9. Rb6+ Kd7 10. Rb7+ Kc8 11. Rb5! c1Q 12. Rc5+ Qxc5**, stalemate.

Having destroyed White's support, the pawn on d3, the black king returns by the same route in order to escape the checks of the importunate rook on c8 or c7. But then it turns out that this maneuver, obviously the only one if Black wishes to avoid perpetual check, has also benefited White: rid of its own pawn, it has obtained the possibility of carrying out the stalemate combination with a rook sacrifice. The modest pawn on d3 has done great work: it helped to turn an elementary stalemate combination into a study with a twelve-move solution.

Now let us acquaint ourselves with the second approach to composing studies: we take as our basis not the final position but some idea, the expression of which is the goal of composing the study regardless of the final position.

Let us try to compose a study on the theme of winning a black piece by a discovered, battery, check. First of all it is necessary to establish the main dramatis personae. On White's side let us take the weakest battery, bishop plus pawn, whose sacrifice should win the strongest black piece, the queen. The queen must be forced to move onto the line of fire; this is most easily achieved by attacking it. Let us appoint the white rook as the attacking piece. Having thus distributed the roles, we can pass to building the scheme of the chosen idea.

Here is one possible scheme: White, **Kh1, Rg2, Bd5, pawn c4 (4)**; Black, **Kb6, Qa7, pawn c7 (3)**. White plays *1. Ra2!* and after *1...Qxa2 2. c5+*; if instead **Qb8**, then **2. Ra8** or **Rb2+**. This dual is easily removed, but that need not occupy us at present.

The scheme of the study is ready. Ahead lies another, no less important part of the work: one has to devise the introductory play. At the same time the place of the white king and the form of material advantage necessary for White to win in the thematic variation are also determined.

Let us deepen the given position. Replacing the black queen on a7 is awkward, but can we not move the black king to b7, say the white bishop to e6, and give check on d5? On closer examination it turns out that this is quite possible: if **1...Kc8**, then **2. Bc6 Kd8 3. Re2** and **4. Re8#**; if **1...Kb8**, then **2. Rg8**; after **1...Ka8** there follows **2. Ra2+**; and after **1...c6**, **2. Rg7+**. Thus the first step has been made, and quite successfully at that, since a whole series of side lines has arisen.

But what should be done next? After some thought, we discover a new and interesting possibility: by placing a black pawn on d6, putting the white king on d3, and shifting the bishop to about f5, we can, after 1. Be4+, induce the move d6-d5, transferring the bishop onto the diagonal we need, since 1...Kb6 cannot be played because of mate, 2. Rb2+ Kc5 3. Rb5#, or loss of the queen, 2...Kb5 or Ka5 3. Ra2+.

At this point we can finish the creative work; all that remains is technical finishing, which requires no effort: by adding a white pawn on c3, we shield the white king from checks by the black queen and secure White's win in the main line.

**№ 128. L. Kubbel, "64", 1927, Commendation, Win**  
![№ 128. Л. Куббель, «64», 1927, похвальный отзыв, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0128.png)

The final study position, № 128: **1. Bf3+ d5! 2. Bxd5+ Kb6 3. Ra2! Qxa2 4. c5+** and **5. Bxa2**, with a win.

Thus we see that, after choosing an idea, we must first mentally outline the pieces suitable for developing it and then construct the corresponding scheme. After that, the same analytical work begins, studying the hidden possibilities in the position, just as when working from a pre-established final position.

Unlike the previous one, however, this scheme must in no case be treated as final and unchangeable; on the contrary, if necessary, it should be modified in whatever way is most expedient for creating interesting play. At the same time, it is not recommended to limit oneself to only one scheme, but to construct several schemes and choose from them the most flexible one for deepening the intended idea.

Continuing our discussion, let us draw some conclusions. Among the many ways and methods of developing technical mastery in composing chess works, the most common are the following:

1. Reconstructing a position from its solution.
2. Eliminating defects in a composition.
3. Thematic competitions.
4. Competitions for improving compositions, that is, their reconstruction.

Recently, a method of improving technical mastery in constructing problems and studies has been gaining popularity: using combinational moments from practical games. In Finland, in 1981, an international competition was even held in which participants were invited to embody or develop combinational motifs inspired by practical play.

In our view, among the named methods of improving personal composing technique, the most productive for a beginning problemist or study composer is the second one: eliminating defects in a composition. This is justified because, first, the chess player sees an already finished position clearly; second, he knows its defect, and therefore all that remains is, by means of imagination and already acquired skills, to create a full-fledged work of chess art.

We offer you a kind of practical workshop for the chess composer. In its first part, incorrect, defective compositions are given, from all the main composition types; in the second, corrected versions are provided. After independently eliminating one defect or another, the beginning study composer or problemist can compare his position with the one given in the book.

### Assignment No. 1. Eliminate the Cook

**№ 129. F. Davidenko, 1980, Mate in 2**  
![№ 129. Ф. Давиденко 1980 Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0129.png)

Diagram № 129. **1. Qb5!** - zugzwang: **1...Nb- 2. Qxb1#** and **1...Nc2! 2. Rb4#**, **1...Nf- 2. Qf5#** and **1...Ne4! 2. Rf2#**, **1...Bxd2 2. Re1#**. Cook: *1. Kf3*.

**№ 130. W. Shinkman, 1885, Mate in 4**  
![№ 130. В. Шинкман 1885 Мат в 4 хода](./diagrams/diagram0130.png)

Diagram № 130. **1. Rh7!** (freeing the seventh rank) **1...d2 2. Ra8!** (freeing the a-file) **2...d3 3. Qa7!!** and **4. Qg7#**. Cook: *1. Re7! d2 2. Re5! d3 3. Rxb4! Kd4 (or Kxb4) 4. Qxb2 (or Qa5)#*.

**I. Kulis and M. Marandyuk, 1982**. White: **Kb1, Qb8, Rf1, Rg5, Be6, Bg1, Ne5, Nf4, pawn g3 (9)** and Black: **Ke4, Qe7, Ba8, Bc3, Nf6, pawns c5, c7, d3, d7 (9)** - mate in two. **1...c6 2. Bf5#**, try **1. Qh8? Nh7 2. Bf5# - 1...Qh7!**, try **1. Qb5? c4 2. Bf5#**, but **1...Qd6!**; correct is **1. Qb2! Bd2 2. Bf5#**. Fourfold change of defenses with an ambush of the white queen. Cook: *1. Bh3*.

**№ 131. V. Kopaev, 1982, Mate in 3**  
![№ 131. В. Копаев 1982 Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0131.png)

№ 131. **1. Be5!** with the threat **2. Qe8+ Bd7 3. Qxd7#**: **1...Qg6+ 2. Rxg6+ hxg6 3. Rc3#** or **2...Nf6+ 3. Rfxf6#**; **1...Nf6+ 2. Rxf6 - Rxf6 3. Rc2#**; **1...Qf5 2. Rc3+ Nxc3 3. Rg6#**; and **1...Qe5 2. Rc2+ Qc3 3. Rfxc3#** - cyclic alternation of four white moves. Cook: *1. Rc2+*.

**A. Spirin, 1981**. White: **Ke2, Qg6, Rb4, Bf8, Nb1, Nb6, pawns b7, c4, e4, g5 (10)** and Black: **Kd4, Ba3, pawns b2, b3, c5, d5, d7, e5, e6 (9)** - mate in three. The author's idea is very original. *1. b8Q? Bxb4 2. Bd6*, but *1...Bb? 2. Qd6* is stalemate; and *1. b8N? Bxd7*, but *1...Bxb4*. Correct is **1. b8B! cxb8 2. Cbd6!** and **3. Bc5#**, and **1...Bxb4 2. Bfd6!** and **3. Bxe5#**. However, the problem can also be solved by other moves, for example *1. Rxb3, 1. Bd6, 1. Qf7, 1. Qf6*, and so on.

**№ 132. M. Kuznetsov, 1981, Mate in 4**  
![№ 132. М. Кузнецов, 1981, Мат в 4 хода](./diagrams/diagram0132.png)

№ 132. **1. Bc7!** with the threat **2. Bb3**: **1...Qg8 2. Rd5+ Ke6 3. Re5+ Kf7 4. Be8#**, **1...Rxe2 2. Rc6+ Kd4 3. Rc4+ Ke3 4. Bb6#**, and **...Kf4 4. Rd4#** - three variations with distant blocking of squares g8, e2, and g5. However, *1. Bb3* also solves.

**A. Kuzovkov, 1982**. White: **Kh2, Qf6, Bf2, Bg6, Nc5, Nd1, pawns f5, g3, h4 (9)** and Black: **Kg4, Qa7, Ra3, Re8, Ba1, Bc2, Nb5, Nd4, pawns c6, c7, e2, e4, f3, g5, h6 (15)** - mate in four. **1. Nd7!** with the threat **2. Qxg5+ hxg5 3. Nf6#**: **1...Ne6 2. Bd4! Qxd4 3. Ne6+** and **2...Bxd4 3. Ne3+**; **1...Nb3 2. Qd4! Qxd4 3. Nf6+** and **2...Bxd4 3. Ne3+**. Cook: *1. Qf7!*

**S. Sukharev, 1982**. White: **Ke2, Be3, Bg4, Nf6, pawns b4, e4, g2, h6 (8)** and Black: **Kh8, Ba6, pawns a7, b5, b6, b7, e5, f7, g6, h7 (10)** - mate in eight. **1. Ke1! g5 2. Bd1! g4 3. Bc1! g3 4. Ba4 bxa4 5. b5**, and then the bishop route on moves 6-8: Ba3-f8-g7. Cook: *1. Bf2 g5 2. Bh5 g4 3. Bh4 g3 4. Nd7* and so on.

**M. Liburkin, "64", 1935**. White: **Kf5, Rc7, Rh7, Bg5, pawns b5, e4 (6)** and Black: **Kg1, Qh1, Bh2, pawns b6, b7, e5, f2, f3, f6, g2, g3 (11)** - win. One of the first studies in which White answers Black's promotion to bishop or knight with a corresponding underpromotion to knight and bishop.

**1. Rc1+ f1N**.

Or *1...f1Q 2. Be3+* and *3. Rhc7*.

**2. Be3+ f2 3. Bc5 bxc5 4. b6 c4 5. Rh8 c3 6. Ra8 c2 7. Ra6 bxa6 8. b7 a5 9. b8B!**

*9. b8Q?* is bad - see White's sixteenth move.

**9...a4 10. Ba7 a3 11. Be3 a2 12. Ke6! f5 13. Kxe5 fxe4 14. Kxe4 a1Q 15. Rxa1 c1Q 16. Bxc1.**

Had White played *9. b8Q?*, Black would be stalemated here.

**16...Nd2 17. Bxd2+- f1Q 18. Be3#.**

If *1...f1B*, then the play is the same up to move 9, and then *9. b8N! a4 10. Na6 a3 11. Nc5 a2 12. Nb3 a1Q 13. Rxa1 c1Q 14. Nxc1 Ba6 15. Ne2#*.

Unfortunately, the study has a cook: *1. Rc1+ (N) 2. Be3+ f2 3. Bf4 exf4 4. Rhc7 f3 5. R7c6 bxc6 6. bxc6 b5 7. c7 b4 8. c8N b3 9. Rb1 b2 10. e5 fxe5 11. Nd6 e4 12. Nb5 e3 13. Nd4 e2 14. Nxf3#*. In addition, in the second variation 9. b8B is also possible. (Note by R. Kofman.)

**V. Karpov, 1980**. White: **Kc1, Qg8, Rb5, Rd1, Be3, Bf5, Na7, Nh5, pawns b3, c5, d4, f6 (12)** and Black: **Kd5, Be8, Bg3, Nc4, Ne6, pawns a3, d7 (7)** - mate in two.

The try *1. Bf4?* with the threat *2. c6#*: **1...Ne5 2. dxe#**, **1...Nd6 2. cxd#**, and **1...Bf7 2. Qa8#**, is refuted by *1...Nd2!*. The solution is **1. f7!** with the threat **2. Nf6#**: **1...Nxd4 2. fxe#**, **1...Nc5 2. fxeN#**, and **1...Be5 2. Qg2#**.

Cook: *1. Nf4*.

**E. Spirin, 1980**. White: **Ka8, Qg4, Rc8, Ng1, Ng5, pawns c2, d2, f4 (8)** and Black: **Kd4, Re1, Rh3, Ba2, Bh8, Nf2, pawns d5, e3, h7 (9)** - mate in two. The false play *1. Qf5?* with threats *2. Ne6#* and *2. c3#* is refuted by *1...exd!*; *1. Qf3?* with the threat *2. Ne6#*: *1...exd 2. c3#* is met by *1...Rh6!*; and *1. Qe2?* with the threat *2. c3#*: *1...exd 2. Ne6#* is met by *1...Rc1!*. The solution is **1. Qd7!** with the threat **2. Qa7#**: **1...Nd3 2. c3#**, **1...Ne4 2. Ne6#**, **1...Rh6 2. N1f3#**, and **1...Rb1 2. Ne2#**.

Cook: *1. Qxh3*.

**N. Ivanov and Ya. Rssomakho, 1980**. White: **Kf2, Qd8, Re3, Rh5, Be3, Bd5, Nf4, Ng2, pawns c4, e2, g4 (11)** and Black: **Kc5, Qd4, Rf5, Rf6, Bc5, Bh7, pawns c7, e4, e6, f7 (10)** - mate in three. **1. Bc6!** with the threat **2. Bxd4+ Bxd4 3. Qxc7#**: **1...Rh6 2. Kf1!** threatening **3. Re4#**, **2...Rfxh5 3. Nd3#**; and **1...Bd6 2. Kg1!** threatening **3. Nd3#**, **2...Qxc3 3. Rxe4#**; also **1...Qxc3 2. Qxf4+ Kd4 3. Rxe4#**, or **2...Bd6 3. Nd3#**.

Cook: *1. Kg1*.

**№ 133. V. Andreev, 1982, Mate in 2**  
![№ 133. В. Андреев, 1982, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0133.png)

Diagram № 133. Initially there is *1...Kd4 2. Rd2#* and *1...Bxa3 2. Qf1#*; after *1. Qg6? Kxd4 2. Qd6#* and *1...Bxa3 2. Qa6#*, but *1...Re3!* refutes it. Correct is **1. Qg8!** with the threat **2. Qc4#**: **1...Kxd4 2. Qd8#** and **1...Bxa3 2. Qxb3#**.

Cook: *1. Rd7*.

**A. Lobusov, 1980**. White: **Kh8, Qd2, Rg4, Be8, Bg7, Nb7, Ne4, pawn a4 (8)** and Black: **Kd5, Qa3, Rc8, Rd6, Bd3, Bd8, pawns c5, c6, e7, h6 (10)** - mate in three. **1. Qf4!** with the threat **2. Qxd6+ exd6 3. Bf7#**: **1...c4 2. Bf7+ e6 3. Qf5#**, **1...Bc7 2. Qf5+ e5 3. Nf6#**, and **1...Rc7 2. Nf6+ exf6 3. Qxd6#**.

Cook: *1. Qh2*.

**Yu. Marker, 1980**. White: **Kg3, Qc1, Re6, Rf5, Bb1, Bh8, Ne7, Ng7, pawns a4, b4, c4, g5 (12)** and Black: **Kd4, Qf6, Re5, Bh1, Ne8, Nh3, pawns b3, c6, g4 (9)** - mate in three. **1. Nc8!** with the threat **2. Qd2+**: **1...Rb5! 2. Re4+! Bxe4 3. Ne6#** and **1...Re2 2. Rd5+! Bxd5 3. Nf5#**. Also **1...Qxg5 2. Rd6+! Nxd6 3. Ne6#**, and **1...Qd8 2. Rf4+! Nxf4 3. Nf5#**.

Cook: *1. R5xf6*.

**№ 134. A. Sevastyanov, 1980, Mate in 3**  
![№ 134. А. Севастьянов, 1980, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0134.png)

Diagram № 134. **1. Ng3!** - zugzwang: **1...Kxg3 2. O-O! Kxh3 3. Rf3#** and **1...Ke3 2. Nf5+ Kf3 3. O-O#**, also **1...Kg2 2. Be2 Kxg3 3. Rg4#**.

Cook: *1. Rg1*.

**№ 135. A. Poletaev, 1982, Mate in 3**  
![№ 135. А. Полетаев, 1982, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0135.png)

Diagram № 135. **1. Qc1! Kb4 2. Rg5 Kb3 3. Rb5#**, **1...Kd4 2. Rg5 Kd3 3. Rd5#**, **1...Kb6 2. Qa3!**, **1...Kd6 2. Qe3!** and **3. Rg6#**.

Cook: *1. Qf8*.

**M. Marandyuk, 1980**. White: **Kd2, Rc1, Rd7, Ba3, Nc4, Nc5, pawns b5, c7, e3, e7, f2 (11)** and Black: **Kd5, Ra6, Re8, Nd6, pawns a4, d3, e4, f3, h6 (9)** - mate in four.

**1. Kc3!** - zugzwang! **1...Rb6 2. Rd1!** with the threat **3. Rxd3+ exd3 4. e4#**; **2...Rxg7 3. c8N!** and **4. Nxb6 (or Nxe7)#**; and **1...Re6 2. Rh1!** with the threat **3. Rh5#** - **2...Rd8 3. c8Q!** and **4. Qxc6 (or Qxg8)#**.

Cook: *1. Rd8*, and there is no defense against **2. c8Q**, for example **1...Rxd8 2. exd8Q** or **2. cxd8Q** and **3. c8Q** or **3. e8Q**.

**№ 136. V. Nikitin, 1980, Mate in 6**  
![№ 136. В. Никитин, 1980, Мат в 6 ходов](./diagrams/diagram0136.png)

№ 136. **1. Qf5! Kg2 2. Qb1! Kxh3 3. Qf5+ Kg2 4. Qf3+ Kg1 5. Qh3** (there is no pawn on h3) **5...Kxh1 6. Qf1#**.

Cook: *1. Qe4 Kf1 2. Qxe3 Ke1 3. Qd3! Kf1 4. Qd1+ Kg2 5. e3* (or e4) *Kxh3 6. Qf3#*.

**S. Sukharev, 1980**. White: **Kc6, Ra3, Rd1, Bb3, Bb4, pawns a2, c2, d4, f6 (9)** and Black: **Kb1, Ba1, Nc1, pawns b2, b5, c3, c7, d2, d6, f7 (10)** - mate in nine.

The author's idea: **1. Ra5! d5 2. Bf8! b4 3. a3 bxa3 4. Bh6! a2 5. Bxd2 cxd2 6. c4 dxc4 7. Ba4 c3 8. Rc5** (the point of 1. Ra5!) **8...c2 9. Bxc2#**.

Cook: *1. Ra8 d5 2. Bxc3 b4 3. Re8* (or g8) *bxc3 4. Re6* (or g6) *and 5. f7, 6. f8Q, 7. a4, 8. Qa3, and 9. Qa2#*.

**№ 137. N. Chebanov, 1980, Mate in 7**  
![№ 137. Н. Чебанов, 1980, Мат в 7 ходов](./diagrams/diagram0137.png)

№ 137. **1. Nf7! Kxg4 2. Nh6+ Kh4 3. Nf5+ Kg4 4. Ne3+ Kh4 5. Kf3! Kxh3 6. Ng2 h4 7. Nf4#**, and **1...hxg5 2. Nf4 g3 3. Kf3 g2 4. Ke4! Kg4 5. f3+ Kg3 6. Ke3 Kh4 7. Bf2#**.

Cook: *1. f4 hxg5 2. Ng5 Kh5 3. Bc5! Kh6 4. Bf8 Kh5 5. Ke4 Kh4 6. Nf6 g3 7. Kf3 (or Kg6)#*.

**A. Begdzhanov, 1982.** White: **Ka1, Qh8, Ne2, Nf6, pawns a2, a4, a5, b3 (5)** and Black: **Ke5, pawns a2, a4, a5, b3** - mate in five. **1. Nc3!** zugzwang: **1...Kd6 2. Qc8 Ke5 3. Nce4 Kf4 4. Qc5! Kf3 5. Qf2#**; **1...Kf4 2. Qh3! Ke5 3. Ncd5 Kd6 4. Qe3! Kc6 5. Qb6#**; **1...Ke6 2. Nce4! Kc5 3. Qc8! Kf4 4. Qc5 Kf3 5. Qf2#** or **2...Kf3 3. Qh2! Ke6 4. Qh5! Ke7 5. Qe8#**; **1...Kf5 2. Ncd5 Ke6 3. Qh3! Kd6 4. Qe3! Kc6 5. Qb6#** or **2...Kc6 3. Qb8! Kf5 4. Qe8! Kg5 5. Qh5#**; also **1...Kd4 2. Nfd5+ Kc5 3. Qe5! Kc4 4. Nb4! axb4 5. Qd5#**, or **2...Kc3 3. Qh4+ Kc5 4. Qf4! Kc6 5. Qc7#**, or **3...Kd3 4. Qg4! Kd2 5. Qd1#**.

Cook: *1. Ng3!*

**Yu. Gordian, 1982.** White: **Kg2, Rc3, Re6, Bd2, Nd8, Ne3, pawns e2, f3, h4 (9)** and Black: **Kf4, Qb1, Ra4, Bd1, Be5, Ng8, pawns c6, d5, f6, f7, g6 (11)** - mate in four. **1. Rc5!** with the threat **2. Nxd5 Kf5 3. Rxf6+!** and **4. Ne7#**: **1...Ra7! 2. Nc2+! Kf5 3. e4+! dxe4 4. Nd6#**, and **1...Qb7 2. Nc4+! Kf5 3. e4+! dxe4 4. Nd6#**.

Cook: *1. Be1*.

**№ 138. L. Topko, 1982, Draw**  
![№ 138. Л. Топко, 1982, Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0138.png)

№ 138. **1. Qg6! Nf5+-! 2. Kg1 Rg7 3. Rf6+! Kg8 4. Qxg7+ Nxg7 5. Bf7+! Kh8 6. Bg6! Qg8!** (otherwise *7. Rf8#*) **7. Bf7! Qh7!** (otherwise *8. Rb6#*) **8. Bg6**, with a positional draw.

However, a cook was found: *1. Qb4!* - counter-battery.

**№ 139. А. Лобусов, 1980, Мат в 2 хода**  
![№ 139. А. Лобусов, 1980, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0139.png)

№ 139. *1. Qd8?* with threats *2. Rc5#, 2. Rb5#,* and *2. Ra5#*: *1...Rf4 2. Rd4#*, *1...Rf3 2. Rd3#*, and *1...Rf2 2. Rd2#*, but *1...Rd7!* refutes. Correct is **1. f4!** with threats **2. Rd4#, 2. Rd3#,** and **2. Rd2#**: **1...Rc7 2. Rc5#**, **1...Rb7 2. Rb5#**, and **1...Ra7 2. Ra5#**.

Cook: *1. Nd7*.

**V. Sitnikov, 1982**. White: **Ke1, Qf8, Rd3, Rf1, Ba7, Bh7, Ne7, Nf2 (8)** and Black: **Ke5, Qa6, Rg6, Rh6, Ba2, Nb7, Nb8, pawns b5, c3, e6, h4 (11)** - mate in two. Initially: *1...Qd6 2. Re3#* and *1...Nd6 2. Bd4#*. In the try *1. Nd5?* with threat *2. Qf4#*: *1...Bxd5 2. Bd4#* and *1...exd5 2. Re3#* - but *1...Qa4!* refutes. Solution: **1. Ne4!** with threat **2. Qf4#**: **1...Kxe4 2. Re3#** and **1...Rf6 2. Bd4#**.

Cook: *1. Rd4*.

**V. Gladkikh, 1979**. White: **Kh3, Qe5, Rf6, Ba4, Bd4, Nd3, Ng4, pawns c2, c7, g2 (10)** and Black: **Kc4, Qe1, Rf2, Rg7, Bc8, Bh2, Ne6, Nf5, pawns b3, d5, e3, f3, f4, g5, h6 (15)** - mate in three. **1. Bb2!** with threat **2. Qd4!** and **3. Nge5#**: **1...Ne- 2. Rxf5**, **1...Nf- 2. Rxe6**, **1...Ned4! 2. Qe6!**, and **1...Nfd4! 2. Qf5!**.

Cook: *1. Bxb3+*.

**№ 140. V. Kapusta, 1982, Mate in 3**  
![№ 140. В. Капуста, 1982, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0140.png)

№ 140. **1. Nc8!** - zugzwang: **1...Be5 2. Qb7! Rd5 3. Qh7#**, **1...Bf4 2. Qh1 Rf3 3. Qh7#**, and **1...d5 2. Qe1! Bxe1 3. Nd6#**, also **1...N- 2. Qb4**.

Cook: *1. Rxg4* and *1. Qf1*.

**A. Lobusov, 1982**. White: **Kf1, Qg4, Rd3, Rh5, Bh6, Ng2, Nh4, pawn e2 (8)** and Black: **Ke4, Ra6, Rf6, Bb7, Be1, Ng1, Nh8, pawns a5, b2, c3, d4, f4, h2 (13)** - mate in three.

**1. Bg7!** with threat **2. Qf5+! Rxf5 3. Rxd4#**: **1...Bc8 2. Rxd4+! Kxd4 3. Qxf4#**, **1...Bf2 2. Qxf4+! Rxf4 3. Re5#**, and **1...Rd6 2. Re5+! Kxe5 3. Qf5#**.

Cook: *1. Rxd4+* and *1. Nf3*.

**F. Davidenko, 1981**. White: **Ka2, Qg2, Ra5, Rd6, Bb5, Bf4, Nd5, Nf7, pawn e7 (9)** and Black: **Kf5, Qa7, Ra8, Rh5, Bh2, Nh3, pawns a6, d2, e4, g5, g7, h4 (12)** - mate in three.

Set play: *1...Bxf4 2. Bd7* and *3. Ne3#*, or *1...Nxf4 2. Ne3+* and *3. Bd7#*. Solution: **1. Ra4!** with threat **2. Qxe4+ Kg4 3. Ne5#**: **1...Bxf4 2. Ne3+ and 3. Bd7#**, and **1...Nxf4 2. Bd7+ and 3. Ne3#**.

Cook: *1. Be2*.

**№ 141. M. Matous, 1980, Draw**  
![№ 141. М. Матоуш, 1980, Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0141.png)

№ 141. **1. c7 Bf5.**

If *1...Rc2*, then *2. Rc6! Bxc6 3. c8Q Bg5 4. Kd4 Bxd8 5. Qg4+ Rg2 6. Qd1+* and so on.

**2. Rh5! Rd3+!**

After *2...Rc2* there follows *3. Rxf5 Bb4 4. Ne6 a3 5. Nd4! Rc3+ 6. Ke2 a2 7. Nf3+ Kg2 8. Rg5+ Kh3 9. Ng1+*, with perpetual check.

**3. Ke2 Rxd8 4. cxd8R!!**

Very unexpected!

**4...Bg4 5. Ke1!**,

and then the branching:

I. **5...Bb4 6. Rd2 Bxg5**. If *6...Bxd2+ 7. Kxd2 Bxh5 8. Kc1*, and the corner a1 is of the wrong color. Or II. **5...Bxd8** (after *5...Bxh5* there follows *6. Rg8+ Kh2 7. Rh8+*) **6. Rg5+! Bxg5**, with two stalemates.

Cook: *2. Rg6+ Rg2* (or *2...Bxg6 3. c8Q Bg5+ 4. Kf3 Rf2+ 5. Kg3 Ba4 6. Qc5*) *3. Ne6! Bxe6 4. Rxe6 Bc5 5. Kd3 Rg8 6. Rg6+!* and so on.

**№ 142. N. Rezvov, 1980, Win**  
![№ 142. Н. Резвов, 1980, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0142.png)

№ 142. **1. Rb8 Qa7!**

If *1...b1Q*, then *2. Bb6+ Kxg7 3. Bd4#*.

**2. Rc8 Qg1+**.

Against *2...Qd7* strong is *3. Bf6+! Qxc8 4. Nf5+ Kg8 5. Ne7+ Kg8 6. Nxc8 f2 7. Kh6! f1Q 8. Bg7+ Kg8 9. Ne7#*.

**3. Kf6 Qd4+ 4. Kf7! Qxg7 5. Ke6!**

Impressive: *5...Qxg6 6. Bf6+ Kh7 7. Rh8#*.

**5...Qg8+ 6. Ke5 Qe8+!**

Counter-sacrifice!

**7. Bxe8 b1Q 8. Bg6! Qa1+ 9. Kf5 Kg7**.

After *9...Qb1* there follows the familiar *10. Ke6!*

**10. Rc7+ Kg8**.

Also weak is *10...Kf8* because of *11. Be7+ Kg8 12. Bh7+! Kxh7 13. Bf6+* and *14. Bxa1*.

**11. Bh7+ Kf8 12. Be7+ Ke8 13. Bg6#**.

However, *1. Re3* also wins, for example: *1...Qa4* (or *1...Qa6 2. Bf6!*) *2. Nf5 b1Q 3. Bf6+ Kg8 4. Nh6+ Kf8 5. Bxb1 f2 6. Rc3 Qf4+ 7. Kxf4 f1Q+ 8. Kg5 Qg1+ 9. Ng4 Qxb1 10. Rc8+ Kf7 11. Ne5+ Ke6 12. Rc6#*, again with a mating finale.

### Assignment No. 2. Eliminate Duals

**№ 143. I. Kavnatsky, F. Kapustin, and L. Lyubashevsky, 1981, Mate in 3**  
![№ 143. И. Кавнатский, Ф. Капустин, и Л. Любашевский, 1981, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0143.png)

№ 143. **1. Ke1!** with threat **2. Ne2+**: **1...Be6 2. Qd1!** with no *2...Ne6* and no *2...Rd6*, then **3. Qd4#**; **1...Ne6 2. Qe2!** with no *2...Be6* and no *2...Rc6*, then **3. Qc4#**; **1...Re6 2. Qxf5!** with no *2...Ne6* and no *2...Bxf5*, then **3. Qc5#**. Each time with obstruction and interference.

Dual: after *1. Ke1 Be6* both *2. Qe2* and *2. Qd1* are possible.

**Yu. Gashev, 1980**. White: **Ke8, Qe3, Bd2, Bh7, Nc6, Nd6, pawn c3 (7)** and Black: **Kd1, Rf1, Bh8, pawns a2, b2, f7, g2, h2 (8)** - mate in seven. **1. Bd3!** with threat **2. Qe2#**: **1...g1N! 2. Ne4!** with threat *3. Nf2+*; **2...h1N! 3. Be1** with threat *4. Qd2#*; **3...b1N! 4. Nb4** with new threat *5. Bc2#*; **4...a1N! 5. Bc2+ Nxc2 6. Qd3+ Kc1 7. Qxc2#**, or **6...Kc1 7. Nxc2#**.

Dual: *2. Bxf1 a1Q 3. Bd3! Qa8+ 4. Kd7!*

**B. Gelpernas, 1980**. White: **Kg8, Qb4, Bd5 (3)** and Black: **Ka1, Nf2, pawns a4, b5, d6, d7, f6, g3, h2 (9)** - mate in twenty-one.

**1. Qd2! Kb1 2. Ba2+ Ka1 3. Bf7! Kb1!** (the continuation 3...d5 shortens the solution) **4. Bg6+ f5!** (very bad is *4...Ka1?* because of *5. Qc1 Ka2 6. Bf7+ d5 7. Bxd5#*) **5. Bxf5+ Ka1 6. Qb4!** - first pendulum swing... **6...Ka2 7. Bb1+ Ka1 8. Bg6! Ka2 9. Bf7+ d5** (here too *9...Ka1 10. Qa3+ Kb1 11. Bg6+ Ne4 12. Bxe4#* is weak) **10. Bd5+ Ka1 11. Qd2!** - second... **11...Kb1 12. Ba2+ Ka1 13. Bf7! Kb1 14. Bg6+ Nd3! 15. Bxd3+ Ka1 16. Qb4!** - and third... - **16...Ka2 17. Bb1+ Ka1 18. Be4 Ka2 19. Bd5+ Ka1 20. Qa3+ Kb1 21. Be4#**.

There is a dual: *15. Qxd3+ Kb2 16. Qd2+ Ka3 17. Bf7 d5 18. Bxd5 b4 19. Qa2#*.

**S. Makhno, 1979**. White: **Kc7, Ba5, Ba8, Nd6, Nf8, pawns d2, e3, g4, g5 (9)** and Black: **Ke5, Bc1, Bc2, Na2, Nh8, pawns c4, d5, g7 (8)** - mate in nine.

**1. Bb6!** with threat **2. Bd4#**: **1...Bb2 2. d4+ cxd3 e.p. 3. Nc4+ Ke4 4. Nd2+** (using the blocking of d3) **4...Ke5 5. Bc5! Nf7! 6. Ng6+ Ke6 7. Nf4+** (using the blocking of f7) **7...Ke5 8. Bf8** (dual: *8. Nf3+ Ke4 9. Bxd5#*) and **9. Bxg7#**.

Also, after *2. Kd7!* mate is delivered in eight moves: *2...Ba4 3. Ke7 Bb5 4. Nd7! Bxd7 5. d4+! cxd3 6. Nc4+ Ke4 7. Nd2+* and *8. Bc7#*.

**№ 144. V. Novikov, 1981, Win**  
![№ 144. В. Новиков, 1981, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0144.png)

№ 144. **1. c5!** (but not *1. b7? f3 2. Nc6 Bg3*) **1...Bxc5** (but not *1...axb? 2. c6*) **2. b7 f3 3. Na6 f2 4. Nxc5+ Ka5!** (on *4...Ka3* mate is reached one move earlier: *5. b8Q f1Q 6. Qb3#*) **5. Nb3+ Ka6!** (otherwise *6. Nd2*) **6. Kc6! f1Q 7. b8N#**.

Unfortunately, there is an unpleasant dual: *3. Nd7* and so on.

**V. Udartsev, 1982**. White: **Kc1, Qc5, Rd8, Bf2, Nc7, Ng3, pawns b3, d4, e3, f4 (10)** and Black: **Kd3, Re4, Ba5, Nc4, Nd6, pawns b5, c2, c3, e7, f3, f5 (11)** - mate in three. **1. Na6!** with threat **2. Qxc4+**: **1...Rxe3 2. Qxf5+ Kxd4 3. Qe4#** or **2...Nxf5 3. Nc5#**. In the line **1...Nxe3 2. Qxb5+ Kxd4 3. Qc4#** there is a dual: after *2...Kxd4* both *3. Qc4#* and *3. Qd5#* are possible. If *2...Nxb5*, then *3. Nc5#*.

**V. Udartsev, 1982**. White: **Kb6, Qf3, Rb5, Rc6, Ba3, Na7, Nf7, pawns d2, d3, e2, c4 (11)** and Black: **Kd4, Qg1, Rb1, Rh6, Bf8, Bh7, Nh3 (7)** - mate in two. False play: **1. Ka6?** with threat **2. Rd5#**: **1...Rh5 2. Rc4#** and **1...Rd6 2. Bc5#**, refuted by **1...Qg5!**. Also **1. Kb7?** with threat **2. Rc4#**: **1...Rc1 2. Rd5#** and **1...Qc1 2. Qe3#**, but **1...Rb4!**. And **1. Rb2?** with threat **2. Nb5#**: **1...Rh5 2. Rc4#** and **1...Rxb2+ 2. Bxb2#**, but again **1...Qg5!**. The solution is **1. Rf6!** with threat **2. Nc6#**: **1...Rc1 2. Rd5#** and **1...Qc1 2. Qe3#**.

However, the try *1. Ka6?* is refuted by a dual: the intended *1...Qg5!* and the unforeseen *1...Nf4!*.

**№ 145. M. Liburkin, "Trud", 1950, Win**  
![№ 145. М. Либуркин «Труд», 1950 Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0145.png)

№ 145. **1. Bf8!** with threat **2. Bg7#**: **1...Rg2+ 2. Kh6 Rg1 3. Ba3 Rg2 4. Bb4 Rg3 5. Bc5 Rg4 6. Bd6 Rg5 7. Be7**, and White wins.

False trail: *1. Bc5? Rd2 2. Bb4 Rc2* and *3. Rc6+* with draw. Duals: *3. Bc5 Rd1 4. Ba3 Rd2 5. Bb4* etc., or *4. Bc5 Rg4 5. Bd6!*

**M. Liburkin, "1234 Modern Endgame Studies", 1938**. White: **Ka2, Rd1, pawns a6, c6, d4, d7, e6, f3, g6, h3 (10)** and Black: **Kh1, Ng1, pawns a7, c5, c7, d2, e7, g2, g7, h2 (10)** - win. A study with five promotions to knight.

**1. d8N! cxd8.**

Black plays for stalemate, since after *1...c4* there follows *2. Nf7* (or b7), then *3. Ng5* (or c5), and *4. Ne4!*

**2. Nf7 d3 3. Nh6 gxh6 4. g7 h5 5. g8N! h4 6. Nf6 exf6 7. e7 f5 8. e8N! f4 9. Nd6 cxd6 10. c7 d5 11. c8N! d4 12. Kb6 axb6 13. a7 b5 14. a8N!**

White's fifth pawn has promoted to a knight! The concluding phase is also noteworthy: now three black pawns promote to queens.

**14...b4 15. Kb6 b3+ 16. Ka3 b2 17. Nc4 (or Na4) b1Q 18. Rxb1 d1Q 19. Rxd1 d2 20. Nb2.**

Or *20. Nxd2 d3 21. Ne4* and *22. Nf2#*.

**20...d3 21. Ra1 d1Q 22. Nxd1 Ne2 23. Nf2#**.

However, deviation from the author's conception is possible - duals: *4. f4* (instead of *4. g7*), *4...h5 5. f5 h4 6. f6 exf6 7. e7 f5 8. e8N f4 9. Nf6 f3 10. Ng4* and *11. Nxf2#*; or *3. Nd6* instead of *3. Nh6* with analogous lines.

**№ 146. M. Liburkin, "64", 1930, Win**  
![№ 146. М. Либуркин «64», 1930 Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0146.png)

№ 146. At first glance the position does not promise much struggle, since the pawn appears to be held by the rook.

**1. d7 Rd1 2. Nf5+.**

A tricky check! It turns out the king has only two retreat squares, since after *2...Kg4* there follows *3. Ne3+*. Both main variations begin here:

**2...Kh5 3. Nf6+.**

Again Black has two ways to continue:

**3...Kg5 4. Nd6! Rxd6 5. Ne4+ and 6. Nxd6**, or

**3...Kg6 4. Nd5 Rxd5 5. Ne7+ and 6. Nxd5.**

Second variation: **2...Kh3 3. Ng5+ Kh2 4. Nd4! Rxd4 5. Nf3+** and **6. Nxd4**.

A remarkable authorial find! Unfortunately, the last variation is spoiled by a transposition of moves: *4. Nf3+* is possible first, and only then *5. Nd4*, i.e. a dual. (Notes to this and the previous study by international master and honored RSFSR trainer R. M. Kofman.)

### Assignment No. 3. Eliminate Unsoundness

**№ 147. L. Silaev, 1982, Win**  
![№ 147. Л. Силаев, 1982, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0147.png)

№ 147. **1. Bd4+!**

A striking introduction! There is no defense in *1...Kxd4 2. Qe5+ Kd3 3. Nc5+ Kc4* (if *3...Kd2*, then *4. Qb2 Ke1 5. Qc3 Kd1 6. Qb3*, or *3...Kc2 4. Qe2 Kc3 5. Qd3+ Kb4 6. Qd4+*) *4. Nxa4 Qxa4 5. Qxf4* and *6. Qxa4*.

**1...Rxd4 2. Qc5+ Kd3.**

*2...Rc4?* is bad because of *3. Qxc4+! Kxc4 4. Nb6+*.

**3. Ne5 Ke4!**

Forcing f2-f3.

**4. f3 Ke3 5. Qc3+ Ke2 6. Qxd4 Qg8+ 7. Ng4.**

Immediately!

**7...Kxf3.**

White's knight perishes, but...

**8. Kf1!!!**

Also possible is *8. Qf2+!*

**8...Qxg4.**

If *8...Kxg4*, then *9. Qg1+*. Against *8...Qg5* there follows *9. Ne5 Kg3 10. Qg1+* and *11. Qh2#*; or *8...Qe6 9. Ne5 Kg3 10. Qf2 Kh3 11. Qg2 Kh4 12. Nf3* and *13. Qg5#*.

**9. Qd3#.**

Beautiful, but after *3...Ke2!* and *4. Qxd4 Qg8+ 5. Kh2 Qh7+ 6. Kg2 f3 7. Kg3 Qg7+ 8. Kf4 Qh8+ 9. Kg5 Qg7+ 10. Kf5 Qf8+ 11. Kg6 Qg8+ 12. Kf6 Qf8+ 13. Ke6 Qe8+ 14. Kd6 Qd8+ 15. Nd7*, draw is reached by the quiet *15...Kf1!*, so the study is unsound.

**№ 148. G. Nadareishvili, 1982, Draw**  
![№ 148. Г. Надарейшвили, 1982, Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0148.png)

№ 148. **1. d7 Ne5+ 2. Kg3! Nxd7 3. Bd5+ K- 4. Kf2 Bd3 5. Ke3 Bf5 6. Kf4 Bh3 7. Kg3 Bh1 8. Kf2 Bh3 9. Kg3** - positional draw.

But after *5...Kc5!* White cannot save the game, so the study has no solution.

**№ 149. M. Liburkin, 1938, Win**  
![№ 149. М. Либуркин, 1938, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0149.png)

№ 149. **1. f7+ Kxf7 2. gxf7+**.

Now, depending on the king move, play branches into three continuations:

I. **2...Kxh7 3. f8N+!**,

II. **2...Kh8 3. f8R+!** (*3. f8Q+?* leads after *3...Kxh7 4. Bxh5 Re1+ 5. Kf2 Rh1+ 6. Kxf1* to stalemate),

III. **2...Kg7 3. f8B+! Kxh7 4. Bxh5**, and White wins.

However, in the first variation after *3...Kg7 4. Bxh5 Rxg2 (or Re5, Re7)!* the study is unsound.

**G. Amiryan, 1982**. White: **Kf5, Qe2, Nh3, pawns b3, c2, d4, e6 (7)** and Black: **Kg3, Qh1, Rc6, Ba3, Nd7, pawns c3, f7, g2 (8)** - win.

**1. Qe3+ Kh2 2. Ng1** - **3. Nf3+ Kg3 4. Ne5+ Kh2 5. Ng4#**; **2...Rc5 3. dxc5 Bxc5 4. Nf3+ Kg3 5. Nd4+! Kh2 6. Qf4+ Kg1 7. Ne2#**; **2...Bc1 3. Kg4 f5+ 4. Kh4 Ne5 5. dxe5 Rc4+ 6. bxc4 Bxe3 7. Nf3#**; and **2...Qxg1 3. Qh6+ Kg3 4. Qf4+ Kh3 5. Qg4+ Kh2 6. Qh4#**.

However, after *1...Kh4! 2. Ng1 fxe6+* the study is unsound.

**E. Enkin, 1981**. White: **Kg3, Re8, Bd7, Nc1, pawns d3, e2, f3, g6, h5 (9)** and Black: **Kf6, Qh6, Ba8, Bg7, Ne1, pawns b6, c6, d4, e3, g5 (10)** - mate in sixteen.

**1. Re6+ Kf5 2. Rxe3+ Kf6 3. Re6+ Kf5 4. e4+! dxe4 5. Rxe3+ Kf6 6. Re6+ Kf5 7. Rxe1+ Kf6 8. Re6+ Kf5.**

After eliminating the knight, one must attract the bishop to d4 and remove the pawn on c6.

**9. d4! Bxd4 10. Rxc6+ Ke5 11. Nd3+ Kd5 12. Nb4+ Ke5 13. Re6+ Kf5**. And finally, the main plan: **14. Rxb6 Ke5 15. Nd3+ Kd5 16. Be6#** - a correct mate. But after *9...Be5+!* the problem is unsound.

**V. Shanshin, 1982**. White: **Kg3, Qg4, Rc1, Rd8, Be7, Ne2, Ne8, pawns c7, f2 (9)** and Black: **Ke5, Ra7, Rh5, Bb6, Bb7, Nh7, Nh8, pawns b4, d3, d4, f7, h3 (12)** - mate in two. *1. Nf4?* with threat *2. Nxd3#*, but *1...Ba6!*; and according to the author *1. Nd6!?*, but due to *1...f5!* the problem is unsound.

**V. Sychev, 1979**. White: **Ka2, Qe5, Bb7, Bc3, Na3, Ng4, pawn b2 (7)** and Black: **Kd3, Qh2, Rd6, Rh5, Bd7, Bg5, Nf5, Ng3, pawns c4, c7, d2, f2, h6 (13)** - mate in three. **1. Bf3!** with threat **2. Qe3+** and **3. Ne5#**. **1...Be3!** (after *1...Bb5* there follows *2. Qf4!*) **2. Qb5! Bxb5 3. Ne5#**; and **1...Ne3!** (if *1...Nf7*, then *2. Qc5!*) **2. Qd4+! Rxd4 3. Ne5#**.

It is unsound because of *1...Nd4*.

**№ 150. A. Chumakov, 1979, Mate in 4**  
![№ 150. А. Чумаков, 1979, Мат в 4 хода](./diagrams/diagram0150.png)

№ 150. **1. Kf4 f6 2. Qa7+ Ba6 3. Bd1! Kb5 4. Qc5#** and **1...Ka6 2. Bc8+ Ka5 3. Qa7+ Ba6 4. Qxa6#**.

But after *1...Ka4!* the problem is unsound.

**№ 151. S. Zakharov and L. Mitrofanov, 1979, Win**  
![№ 151. С. Захаров и Л. Митрофанов, 1979, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0151.png)

№ 151. **1. d6 Bc6(?) 2. d7! Bxd7 3. Bd5+ Kg1 4. Kc2! h2 5. a7 a2 6. Kb2 a3+!**

Before beginning to chase bishop with bishop, Black maximally strengthens the position.

**7. Ka1! Be6! 8. Ba8!!**

Suddenly, zugzwang!

**8...Bc4! 9. Be4!!**

Roman idea: Black's bishop must be lured to d3.

**9...Bd3 10. Bh1! Kxh1 11. a8Q+ Kg1 12. Qg8+**, and White wins: the queen approaches and, with check, captures the badly placed bishop.

However, there is no win after the subtle *1...Kf1 2. Bd5 a2! 3. Bxa2 Bc6 4. d7 h2!*, or *2. Kc2 Bc6 3. d7 h2! 4. Bc4 Kf2 5. d8Q h1Q*.

**№ 152. V. Yurzinov, 1981, Win**  
![№ 152. В. Юрзинов, 1981, Выигрыш](./diagrams/diagram0152.png)

№ 152. **1. Re4! Rxe4 2. b7 Re1 3. Kb2! Re2+ 4. Kc3 Re3+ 5. Kd2! Rd3+! 6. Kxd3 Ne5+ 7. Kd4!? Nc6+ 8. Kc5! Nb8 9. Kd5 Na6 10. a4** (tempo gained) **10...Nb4+ 11. Kc5 Na6+ 12. Kb6 Nb8 13. a5 Kxe6 14. a6 Kd6 15. a7**, with a win.

But first of all, *7. Ke4! Nc6 8. Kd5 Nb4+ 9. Kc5 Na6+ 10. Kb6 Nb8 11. Ka7* etc. also wins; second, *7. Kd4?* leads to a draw because of *7...Kxe6*; third, no clear win is seen after *4...Rxa2 5. b8Q Nf6 6. Qb7+ Kxe6 7. Qb3+ Nd5 8. Kd4 Ra5* and so on.

### Assignment No. 4. Reconstruct Positions from the Solutions

**№ 153. C. Mansfield, British Chess Federation Tourney, 1974, 1st prize, Mate in 2**  
![№ 153. К. Мэнсфилд, Конкурс Британской шахматной федерации, 1974, 1-й приз, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0153.png)

№ 153. The unexpected key **1. Be7!** creates the threat of a mate by the indirect battery, **2. Bf6#**. On the check to the white king **1...Kxe6+**, a black direct battery, the same white pieces form a direct battery: **2. Bg5#**. On **1...Kd4+**, the combined white battery plays: **2. Bf5#**. One bishop forms a direct battery with one rook and an indirect battery with the other. On captures on b7, further beautiful battery mates follow with shut-off of black pieces: **1...Qxb7 2. Bd3#** and **1...Bxb7 2. Bc2#**.

№ 154. In the problem under consideration, the move *1. Be6?!* immediately creates the battery threat *2. Kc3#*, and the thematic defenses are: **1...Bxc4+ 2. Kxc4#**, **1...Nxe4 2. Kxe4#**, **1...Nb4+ 2. Qxb4#**, and **1...Qxe5 2. f8Q#**. However, because of the single but sufficient black reply *1...Nf3!*, this white move is only a thematic try. By playing **1. Kc3!** first, White changes both the threat to **2. Be6#** and the mating replies to all four thematic black defenses, a so-called change of play: **1...Bxc4 2. Bxc4#**, **1...Nxe4+ 2. Bxe4#**, **1...Nb4 2. Bc5#**, and **1...Qxe5+ 2. fxe5#**. In addition, Black can defend by **1...Bxc6**, and then two mates are possible at once: **2. Bxc6#** and **2. Rxc6#**, i.e. a dual. Can it be eliminated?

№ 155. The straightforward tries *1. Qb6?* with short threat *2. Qg1#* and *1. Qd5?* with *2. Qh1#* are both parried in one way only, by checking the white king: *1...Rc6+!*, so they are thematic tries. By *1. Qe6?* White can create the threat *2. Qe1#*, using the constructive doubling mechanism, formed by two line-moving pieces on one line, where the mating piece is supported by the rear piece. This try too is refuted by black rook move to c6, made possible by disruption of the pinning mechanism.

Clearly, if in this doubling mechanism the front unit were the white rook and the supporting one the queen, then the defense *1...Rc6* would no longer prevent mate on c1. Therefore, by moving the rook through e6 by any of three seemingly equivalent moves, *1. Re3*, *1. Re4*, and *1. Re5*, White creates the threat *2. Qe6!* and *3. Re1#*.

Only **1. Re5!** reaches the goal, with thematic lines underlying the author's conception: **1...Bc4 2. Qb6!** and **3. Qg1#**, **1...Nc6 2. Qd5!** and **3. Qh1#**. In these lines the white queen twice unpins the black rook, exploiting Black's inability to check the white king. The other moves, *1. Re3?* and *1. Re4?*, refuted respectively by *1...Bc4!* and *1...Nc6!*, together with the try *1. Qe6?*, are inseparable from the full content and serve as thematic tries.

№ 156. After **1. Rf5!** with threat **2. Qc7+ Rc6 3. Qxc6#**, the defense **1...Re5** creates a frontal half-pin of Black's rook and knight: **2. Qf4! Re4 3. Qc7#** or **2...Nxf4 3. Bxe7#**. When the black king retreats to a free square, these same pieces become diagonally half-pinned: **1...Kc4 2. Qh1! Re5 3. Qc1#**, **2...Nc3 3. Qc6#**, or **2...Nb4 3. Qc4#**.

The most serious defect is duals in the second thematic line against other black knight moves: *2...Ne3 3. Qc6#* and *3. Qe4#*; *2...Nf4 3. Qc6#*, *3. Qe4#*, and *3. Qc1#*.

Also unpleasant is the dual on move two after the obvious defense *1...Rc6+ 2. Rxd5+* and *2. Qh1*, as well as the key move activating the otherwise sidelined white queen while simultaneously pinning the active black knight, especially in view of the eye-catching initial capture *1...Nxd4*.

Can these weaknesses of the problem be eliminated?

№ 157. Although the mating move is the same in all three thematic lines, the resulting mate positions are considered echo-mates, since the arrangement of pieces changes accordingly each time.

**1. Bg7!** with threat **2. Qe4+**: **1...Bd3 2. Ne8+**, a second threat, **2...Ke6 3. Nc7+ Kd6 4. Qf8#**; **1...Kd4 2. Nxd7+ Kc4 3. Nb6+ Kc5 4. Qf8#**; and **1...d5 2. Ne4+ Ke6 3. Nc5+ Kd6 4. Qf8#**.

The impression is somewhat reduced by the white pawn on a5, participating in only one line. Especially unpleasant is that after the black king goes to free square e6, besides the correct-mate continuation **2. Qe4+ Kf7 3. Qe8+ Kxg7 4. Nh5#**, there is also a dual: *3. Bh8!* with inevitable *4. Qe8#*.

Can these defects be removed?

№ 158. The main plan *1. Qc2* and *2. Qc6#* cannot be carried out immediately due to obvious obstacles, black rook or bishop moves to c3. White must force both black pieces to cross critical square c3, achieved by two consecutive preparatory plans: **1. Qb1! Bb2 2. Qh7! Rb3**.

Now Black's former defenses no longer refute the main plan,

**3. Qc2!**, since the critical character of the previous moves can be exploited: **3...Rc3 4. Qf5#** and **3...Bc3 4. Qxg2#**, with a Grimshaw combination on critical square c3.

Our comments to positions № 153-158 are taken from the book *Pursuit of a Theme* by international grandmaster V. F. Rudenko, 1983. For the next study we offer notes by another Soviet international grandmaster, G. M. Kasparyan.

№ 159. All White's hopes of salvation are connected with the pawn. **1. c7 Nb5!** If now *2. Rc6*, then *2...Rc8* and *3...Nxc7* with a win.

**2. Re3!** It turns out that only by sacrificing the pawn can White hope to save the game.

**2...Nxc7+ 3. Kc6 Na6! 4. Kb5! Nc7+ 5. Kc6 Ba5.** Black has successfully regrouped forces, but still...

**6. Rh3+!** The move *6. Re5? Rg6+ 7. Kb7 Bb6* would lose.

**6...Kg7 7. Rh5! Ra8 8. Kb7.** The white king feels excellent among black pieces.

**8...Kf6.** On *8...Ra6* White replies *9. Rc5*.

**9. Rc5 Ne6!** Black begins to show activity.

**10. Rh5!** If *10. Rd5?*, then *10...Bd8* and Black wins.

**10...Kg6 11. Re5! Nc7.** Forced.

**12. Rc5 Rb8+! 13. Ka7.** If *13. Kxb8?*, then *13...Na6+* with a win.

**13...Ra8+ 14. Kb7 Ne6 15. Re5 Rb8+! 16. Ka6!** On *16. Ka7?* there follows *16...Bb6+*.

**16...Ra8+ 17. Kb7 Nc7 18. Rc5**, and draw.

№ 160. The knight must be tucked away in the corner to avoid trouble.

**1. Na1 Kf6 2. Be8 Bd6+ 3. Ke3 Bxh2 4. Kd2 Nb2 5. Kc3 Ba4.** If *5...Be5+*, then *6. d4!*

**6. Bh5 Nd1+ 7. Kb4 Nh2 8. Kc3 Kg5.** The king drives the bishop from its convenient square.

**9. Bf3!** The bishop finds a suitable square.

**9...Kf4 10. Bh5 Kg5 11. Bf3 Bd1 12. Bc6! Na4+ 13. Kd2 Nb2 14. Kc3 Ba4 15. Bf3**, and draw.

Another symmetrical continuation is also possible: *8...Bd1 9. Be8 Ke7 10. Bc6! Kd6 11. Be8 Ke7 12. Be6 Na4 13. Kd2* and so on, also with a draw.

№ 161. After **1. Kd2** the knight can no longer jump to c3 attacking the rook because of *2. Rd3*, threatening to take the knight with the rook and then the pawn. After *2...Bf4+ 3. Ke1*, the bishop cannot check on g3. But after *1...Nc1*, the rook cannot reach the knight from c5: square a3 is free, and after two bishop checks Black queens the pawn. Immediate *1...Bf4+*, exploiting the fact that the pawn is protected by a fork, also fails, because after *2. Kxe2 Nc3+ 3. Kf3 (or Kd3) Nxd5 4. Ke4!* White still wins one of Black's pieces and saves the game. As one of the moments that make the composition irrefutable, this episode is excellent.

After **1...Nc1** the threat of two bishop checks followed by promotion to queen cannot be met by *2. Rd4* because of a new fork on b3, nor by *2. Rf5*, to which *2...Bg3* follows and there is no *3. Rf1*, nor of course by *2. Rd3* under attack by the knight, nor finally by *2. Rg5* because of *2...Bf4+*. Each of these refutations by itself is ordinary, but the fact that all of them are present together creates an impression of sorcery. So many defensive possibilities, and each is parried.

But even that is not all. **2. Rb5+ Ka2.** Here too one still cannot defend against the terrible two-check threat by *2. Rb4*, because of *3...a1Q+ 4. Kxe1*, and the fork strikes again. What tenacious black play. In what a dense net of traps White must maneuver.

White must play **3. Ra5+**, but now the king no longer lets the rook reach a4. **3...Kb3!** Curiously, however, after *3...Kb2* White is saved only by *4. Ra4!*, not by *4. Rf5 Nb3+!* (*4...Ba3 5. Rf2!*) *5. Kxe2* and again a fork; and if *5. Ke1*, then *5...Nd4!* (but not *5...Bg3+ 6. Kxe2 Nd4+ 7. Kf3 Nxf5 8. Kg4!*, and for the second time on different squares, after losing the rook, White's king attacks two black pieces and wins one of them; this alone would suffice for a study) *6. Rg5*, and for the sixth time the fatal fork catches White.

So only **4. Ra4**. Now after *4...Bc7*, not *5. Re4* immediately, which is bad because of *5...Ba5*, but first *5. Rb4+ Ka3*, and when the knight is undefended, *6. Re4* with draw.

**4. Rf5**. This is now possible, since square b3 is inaccessible to the knight. At the same time, because of Rf3+, square g3 has become inaccessible to the bishop. What astonishing, constantly changing, yet never broken interdependence.

**4...Bd6 (or Bc7) 5. Rf3+**, and if **5...Kb2**, then **6. Rf2 Bb4 (or Ba5)+ 7. Ke3**.

As a result of Black's fifth move and White's reply, the pawn becomes pinned. True, Black can now win the exchange and remain with knight and pawn, leaving White apparently nothing but resignation. But after **7...Bc5 (or Bb6)** there follows **8. Kd2!!**, and after **8...Bxf2** White is stalemated.

What an unexpected saving resource White finds at the very end of the struggle, after successfully avoiding all the dangers that lay in wait at every step.

But even this is not all.

If Black had played **5...Ka4** (*5...Kb4* closes the bishop's checking diagonal, and *6. Rf2* ends the fight with a draw), then **6. Re3** would follow. With the black king on b2, the move *6. Re3* would be impossible because of *6...Bb4 (or Ba5)+*, and the rook would have to be given up for nothing.

Now, however, after **6...Bf4**, White escapes the pin by sacrificing the rook a second time, **7. Ke1!! Bxe3**, and for the second time saves the game by pure economical stalemate.

### Did You Correct Them Properly?

For diagram № 129: add a black pawn on g3.

For diagram № 130: add a white pawn on d5.

For the two-mover by I. Kulis and M. Marandyuk: add a black pawn on g4.

For diagram № 131: move the bishop from b2 to f6.

For A. Spirin's problem. Reworking: White - **Kg7, Rb3, Bb1, Bf8, Nb4, Nb6, pawns b7, c4, d2, e4 (10)** and Black - **Kd4, Ba3, Be8, pawns b2, c5, d7, e5, e6 (8)**; mate in three - the solution remains the same.

For diagram № 132: move the white king from c2 to b3 and the white bishop from b8 to c7. The theme is distant blocking: by means of a battery mechanism, "ultra-distant" blocking of squares g8 and e2 is shown. **1. Kc2!** with threat **2. Bb3. 1...Qg8 2. Rd5+ Ke6 3. Re5+ Kf7 4. Be8#; 1...Rxe2 2. Rc6+ Kd4 3. Rc4+ Ke3 4. Bb6#; and 1...Nxg5 2. Nc4+ Kxe4 3. Ng3+ Kf4 4. Rd4#**.

For A. Kuzovok's four-mover: move the bishop from c2 to g8, and add a black pawn on c2.

For S. Sukharev's problem. Reworking: White - **Kd1, Be3, Bg4, Nf6, pawns b4, c5, g3, h2, h3, h6 (10)** and Black - **Kh8, Ba6, pawns a7, b5, b6, b7, e6, f7, g6, h7 (10)**; mate in eight - **1. Ke1!** and so on. But *1. cxb5* also exists.

For M. Liburkin's study. Reworking: White - **Kf5, Rc8, Rh7, Bh6, pawns b5, e4 (6)** and Black - **Kg1, Qh1, Rh2, Bh3, pawns b6, b7, e5, f2, f3, g2, g3, g4 (12)**; win - **1. Rc1+ f1N** or *1...f1B* **2. Be3+ f3 3. Bc5 bxc5 4. b6 c4 5. Rh8 c3 6. Ra8 c2 7. Ra6 bxa6 8. b7 a5 9. b8B!** or **9. b8N!**, and White wins. Yet again there is *1. Rd7! f1N 2. Rd1!* Young friend, dare to fix it.

For V. Karpov's two-mover: move the white king to c3, and instead of bishop g3 add a black pawn on g5.

For E. Spirin's two-mover: add a black pawn on h2.

For diagram № 133: move the white king from a8 to h6.

For the collective three-mover by N. Ivanov and Ya. Rossomakho: replace white pawn c4 with a black pawn on c6. Now 1. Bxc6 solves.

For A. Lobusov's problem: add a black pawn on g3.

For Yu. Marker's three-mover: add a black pawn on b7; then there is 1...Nxg7.

№ 134. Remove bishop d3, move the knight from h3 to c3, and add a white pawn on h3: the line changes to *1...Kg2 2. Ke2 Kxg3 3. Rg4#*.

№ 135. Move the rook from g3 to g8.

M. Marandyuk corrects his four-mover by moving the white king from d2 to c3 and the rook from c1 to a1; instead of bishop a3, adding white pawns a3 and b4; and removing black pawn a4, assuming only 1. a4 solves and so on. But there is also **1. Rd1!** with threat **2. Rxd3+ exd3 3. e4#**: **1...Rxe7 2. c8Q Ra6 (or Rb6) 3. Qb7+** or **3. Qg8+**. Here too, try your hand at correction.

№ 136. Move the white queen from f3 to f8.

For S. Sukharev's nine-mover: remove black pawn c7, move white pawn f6 to g5, and black pawn f7 to g6. Both the thematic conception and the play are preserved.

№ 137. Raise the entire position by one rank: **1. Nf8!**

For A. Begdzhanov's five-mover: move knight e2 to b5.

For Yu. Gordian's four-mover: add a black knight on h1.

№ 138. Move the white queen to g6; start the solution with Black's move 1...Nf5+!, then continue as by the author.

№ 140. Reworking: White - **Kf8, Qb1, Rf6, Rg5, Bg1, Nc8 (6)** and Black - **Ke4, Rd3, Bg3, Na2, pawns d6, e6, g4 (7)**; mate in three - **1. Ba7!** and so on.

For S. Makhno's problem. Reworking: White - **Kc7, Bb8, Be6, Nd6, Nf8, pawns d2, e3, g5 (8)** and Black - **Ke5, Bc1, Be8, Nf2, pawns c4, f5, g4, h2 (9)**; mate in nine - now **1. Ba7** solves, then as by the author.

№ 144. Move the white king from d5 to d7.

For V. Udartsev's three-mover: remove white pawn f4.

For V. Udartsev's problem. Reworking: White - **Kf7, Qc3, Re7, Rf6, Bc5, Bc8, Nd2, Ng8, pawns b5, d4 (10)** and Black - **Kd5, Qa2, Ra7, Rf1, Bg1, Bh3, Nc1, pawns g2, g3 (9)**; mate in two - **1. Rf3!**

№ 145. Reworking: White - **Kf6, Ba3, Bh7 (3)** and Black - **Kh8, Rc8, pawns c6, h5 (4)**; win - **1. Kg6 Rg8+** (on *1...Rb8*, then *1. Be7*) **2. Kh6 Rg2 3. Bb4** (but not *3. Bc5 Rd2 4. Bb4 Rd4 5. Bc3 c5*) **3...Rg3 4. Bc5 Rg4 5. Bd6 Rg5 6. Be7 Rf5 7. Bxf5**.

For M. Liburkin's study. Reworking: White - **Kd5, Ra1, Bh7, pawns a6, c6, d7, c6, g6 (8)** and Black - **Kh2, Qh1, Rg2, Bg1, pawns a7, c7, e7, f2, f6, g3, g7, h3 (12)**; win - **1. Rf1 f5 2. d8N! f4 3. Nf7 f3 4. Nh6 gxh6 5. g7 h5 6. g8N! h4 7. Nf6 exf6 8. e7 f5 9. e8N! f4 10. Nd6 cxd6 11. Ke6 d5 12. c7 d4 13. c8N! d3 14. Nb6 d2 15. Bc2 axb6 16. a7 b5 17. a8N!**

№ 146. Reworking: White - **Ka7, Ne7, Nh7, pawn d7 (4)** and Black - **Kh4, Rd1, Na3 (3)**; win - **1. Nf5+ Kh5 2. Nf6+ Kg6 3. Nd5 Nb5 4. Kb6! Rxd5 5. Ne7+**, or 2...Kg5 3. Nd6 Nb5+ 4. Kb8! Rxd6 5. Ne4+.

In case of *4...Nd4*, White wins with *5. Nfe4+! Kg6 6. Nc3 Nc6+ 7. Kc7 Rxd6 8. Kxd6 Nd8 9. Kc7 Nf7 10. Ne4 Kg7 11. Nd6 Kg7 12. Kc8 Ne6 13. Nf5+* and *14. Nd4*. If *5...Kf4*, then *6. Ke7!*

Second variation: **1...Kh3 2. Ng5+ Kh2 3. Nd4** (now *3. Nf3+? Kh1! 4. N3(5)d4 Nb5+ 5. Kb6 Nxd4 6. d8Q Rb1+ 7. Ka6 Ra1+ 8. Kb7 Rb1+ 9. Kc8 Rb8+! 10. Kxb8 Nc6+* is impossible) **3...Nb5+ 4. Kb6! Rxd4 5. Nf3+**. If *4...Nxd4*, then *5. d8Q Rb1+ 6. Kc5 Rc1+ 7. Kd5*.

№ 147. Reworking: White - **Kg1, Qe1, Ne4, pawn f3 (4)** and Black - **Ke5, Qa8, Rd6 (3)**; win - **1. Qc3+ Rd4 2. Qc7+ Ke6 3. Qb6+ Ke5 4. Qf6+ Kd5 5. Qd6+ Kc4 6. Qc5+ Kd3 7. Nf2! Ke3 8. Qc3+ Ke2 9. Qxd4 Qg8+ 10. Ng4! Kxf3 11. Kf1! Qxg4 12. Qd3#**.

№ 148. Reworking: White - **Kf4, Bc5, pawn c6 (3)** and Black - **Kh6, Bc3, Na5, Nf6 (4)**; draw - **1. c7 Nd5+ 2. Ke4 Nxc7 3. Kd3 Be5 4. Ke4 Bc3 5. Kd3 Nd5 6. Be3+! K- Bc2** or **5...Be6 6. Ke4 Nc4 7. Be3+! K- 8. Bf4.**

№ 149. Move the white king to f2, the black rook to f1, and instead of pawn g2 place a white bishop on g3; win - **1. f7 Nxf7 2. gxf7+ Kh8 3. f8R!** (but not *3. f8Q+? Kxh7 4. Bxh5 Rf1+ 5. Kxf1* - stalemate) **3...Kg7 4. c8B+! Kxh7 5. Bxh5** or **2...Kxh7 3. f8N+! Kg7 4. Bxh5 Rh1 5. Ne6+ Kf6 6. Nf4**.

For G. Amiryan's study. Reworking: White - **Kf5, Qe3, Nh3, pawns c2, d4 (5)** and Black - **Kh2, Qh1, Ra2, Rc7, Ba3, pawns c3, f7, g2, h7 (9)**; win - now **1. Ng1** and so on.

For E. Enkin's many-mover: move the bishop from g7 to c3, and add a black pawn on g7.

For V. Shanshin's problem. Reworking: White - **Kh2, Qf7, Rc4, Bd8, Nf5, Nh5, pawns d2, d5 (8)** and Black - **Ke5, Qa4, Bb1, Bf8, Nc7, Ng2, pawns b4, d6, e3, f4, h4 (11)**; mate in two. Initially: *1...Be4 2. d4#* and *1...Bxf5 2. Bf6#*. The try *1. Nd4?* with threat *2. Nf3#*: *1...Ke4 2. Qf5#*, *1...Be4 2. Bf6#*, and *1...Qd1 2. Nc6#*, is refuted by *1...Nf5!* The solution is **1. Nf6!** with threat **2. Ng4#**: **1...Kxf5 2. Qh5#**, **1...Bxf5 2. d4#**, and **1...Qd1 2. Nd7#**.

For V. Sychev's three-mover: instead of bishop d7, add black pawns on a6 and g6.

№ 150. Move the queen from g1 to f2.

№ 151. Remove the first move; start the solution with 1. d7 and so on.

№ 153. **C. Mansfield (British Chess Federation, 1974, 1st prize):** White - **Kh5, Qe8, Re1, Rh4, Bc5, Be4, Nb7, Ng8, pawn e6 (9)** and Black - **Kc5, Qb2, Rc6, Rd5, Ba6, Nh8, pawns c3, f3, h6 (9)**; mate in two - **1. Be7!** with threat **2. Bf6#**.

**№ 154. F. Salazar, 1st FIDE Team Tourney, 1975, 2nd place, Mate in 2**  
![№ 154. Ф. Салазар, I командный турнир ФИДЕ, 1975, 2-е место, Мат в 2 хода](./diagrams/diagram0154.png)

№ 154. **F. Salazar (1st FIDE Team Tourney, 1975, 2nd place):** White - **Kd3, Qd2, Rc4, Re8, Bd5, Be3, Ne5, pawns c6, e2, e4, f4, f5, f7 (13)** and Black - **Kd6, Qg7, Bb5, Na6, Ng5, pawns b3, c7 (7)**; mate in two - **1. Kc3!** with threat **2. Be6#**.

The problem is easily corrected by removing white pawn e2 and adding black pawns b7 and f3; then the thematic try is refuted by *1...Bxc6!*

**№ 155. F. Chlubna, "Die Schwalbe", 1978, 1st prize, Mate in 3**  
![№ 155. Ф. Хлубна, «Ди швальбе», 1978, 1-й приз, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0155.png)

№ 155. **F. Chlubna ("Die Schwalbe", 1978, 1st prize):** White - **Kh6, Qb3, Re8, Rh2, Bb1, pawns d2, f4, g3 (8)** and Black - **Kd1, Rc2, Ba6, Na7, pawns b2, b7, d3 (7)**; mate in three - the tries *1. Qb6?* with threat *2. Qf1#* and *1. Qd5?* with threat *2. Qh1#* are both refuted by *1...Rc6+!*; and *1. Re3?* by *1...Bc4!* and *1. Re4?* by *1...Nc6!*

The solution is **1. Re6!** with threat **2. Qe6!** and **3. Re1#**.

The scheme of this problem is formed by three white pieces - king h6, queen b3, rook e8 - and four black pieces: king d1, rook c2, bishop a5, knight a7. The essential feature of the scheme is the pinning mechanism of the black rook in the initial position and the possibility of that rook giving check to the white king if the white queen leaves the line of the pin.

Among the thematically necessary pieces in the mating pictures are the white rook h2 controlling d2 and the black pawn d3, which blocks the line of action of the black bishop from f1.

The remaining pieces are technical: white pawn d2 limits black rook movement along the second rank and prevents the defense 2...d2; white bishop b1 controls c2 if the unpinned black rook departs; white pawns f4 and g3 prevent their own queen from attacking the first rank from those files; black pawn b7 eliminates unsoundness in black bishop defenses from b7 and c8; black pawn b2 blocks a square when the black king goes to e1 in positions with the white queen on e6 and d5.

**№ 156. A. Gulyaev, Moscow-Leningrad Match, 1933, 1st place, Mate in 3**  
![№ 156. А. Гуляев, Матч Москва — Ленинград, 1933, 1-е место, Мат в 3 хода](./diagrams/diagram0156.png)

№ 156. **A. Gulyaev (Moscow-Leningrad Match, 1933, 1st place):** White - **Ka3, Qh2, Rf4, Bd8, Bg8, Na7, Nb5, pawns f2, g5 (9)** and Black - **Kc5, Qh8, Rc6, Nd5, pawns a5, b6, d3, e7, f6, h4 (10)**; mate in three - **1. Rf5!** with threat **2. Qc7+**. It is hardly possible to eliminate the duals in this problem by simple means; they are largely inherent in the chosen scheme.

**№ 157. I. Kesl, "Zlata Praha", 1892, Mate in 4**  
![№ 157. И. Кесл, «Злата Прага», 1892, Мат в 4 хода](./diagrams/diagram0157.png)

№ 157. I. Kesl ("Zlata Praha", 1892): White - **Kb8, Qf3, Bf8, Nf6, pawn a5 (5)** and Black - **Kc5, Bb5, Nc2, Nh6, pawns c6, d7, h7 (7)**; mate in four - **1. Bg7!** with threat **2. Qe4+**. The indicated defects can be removed as follows.

V. Rudenko (*Pursuit of a Theme*, 1983): White - **Kb7, Qf3, Bf8, Nf6 (4)** and Black - **Kc5, Ra8, Rb1, Bc1, Na7, Nb8, pawns b5, d7, h6, h7 (10)**; mate in four - **1. Bg7!** with threat **2. Qc4+**. It seems the addition of two black pieces fully justifies elimination of the listed defects.

**№ 158. G. Bintzer, "Die Schwalbe", 1930, 1st prize, Mate in 4**  
![№ 158. Г. Бипцер, «Ди швальбе», 1930, 1-й приз, Мат в 4 хода](./diagrams/diagram0158.png)

№ 158. G. Bintzer ("Die Schwalbe", 1930, 1st prize): White - **Kg8, Qg6, Re8, Na5, Ne6, pawn f4 (6)** and Black - **Kd5, Rh3, Bd4, pawns d6, f6, g2, g4, h4 (8)**; mate in four - **1. Qb1! Bb2 2. Qh7! Rb3 3. Qc2! Rc3 4. Qf5#** or **3...Bc3 4. Qxg2#**.

**№ 159. Yu. Bazlov, Armenian SSR Chess Federation Tourney dedicated to the 50th anniversary of the October Revolution, 1967, 1st prize, Draw**  
![№ 159. Ю. Базлов, Конкурс шахматной федерации Армянской ССР, посвященный 50-летию Октябрьской революции, 1967, 1-й приз, Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0159.png)

№ 159. Yu. Bazlov (Armenian SSR Chess Federation Tourney dedicated to the 50th anniversary of the October Revolution, 1967, 1st prize): White - **Kd5, Re6, pawn c6 (3)** and Black - **Kh8, Rg8, Bc3, Na3 (4)**; draw - **1. c7 Nb5! 2. Re3! Nxc7+ 3. Kc6 Na6! 4. Kb5! Nc7+ 5. Kc6 Ba5 6. Rh3+! Kg7 7. Rh5! Ra8 8. Kb7 Kf6 9. Rc5 Ne6! 10. Rh5 Kg6 11. Re5 Nc7 12. Rc5 Rb8+! 13. Ka7 Ra8+ 14. Kb7 Ne6 15. Re5 Rb8+! 16. Ka6! Ra8+ 17. Kb7 Nc7 18. Rc5**, draw.

**№ 160. N. Kondratyuk and V. Yakovenko, VI USSR Team Chess Composition Championship, 1969, 1st place, Draw**  
![№ 160. Н. Кондратюк и В. Яковенко, VI командное первенство СССР по шахматной композиции, 1969, 1-е место, Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0160.png)

№ 160. N. Kondratyuk and V. Yakovenko (VI USSR Team Chess Composition Championship, 1969, 1st place): White - **Kf4, Bg6, Nb3, Nh2, pawn d3 (5)** and Black - **Ke7, Bd1, Bf8, Na4, pawn c7 (5)**; draw - **1. Na1 Kf6 2. Be8 Bd6+ 3. Ke3 Bxh2 4. Kd2 Nb2 5. Kc3 Ba4 6. Bh5 Nd1+ 7. Kb4 Nb2 8. Kc3 Kg5 9. Bf3! Kf4 10. Bh5 Kg5 11. Bf3 Bd1 12. Bc6! Na4+ 13. Kd2 Nb2 14. Kc3 Ba4 15. Bf3**, draw; or **8...Bd1 9. Be8 Ke7 10. Bc6! Kd6 11. Be8 Ke7 12. Bc6 Na4+ 13. Kd2** etc., also draw.

**№ 161. G. Matison, "Shakhmatny Listok", 1927, 1st-2nd prizes, Draw**  
![№ 161. Г. Матисон, «Шахматный листок», 1927, 1—2 призы, Ничья](./diagrams/diagram0161.png)

№ 161. G. Matison ("Shakhmatny Listok", 1927, 1st-2nd prizes): White - **Kd3, Rd5 (2)** and Black - **Kb1, Bh2, Na2, pawn e2 (4)**; draw.

Here is the "through-line" of the study: **1. Kd2 Nc1 2. Rb5+ Ka2 3. Ra5+ Kb3! 4. Rf5! Bd6 (or Bc7) 5. Rf3+ Kb2 6. Rf2 Bb4 (or Ba5)+ 7. Ke3 Bc5 (or Bb6) 8. Kd2!! Bxf2**. White is stalemated. But on move five Black could play not Kb2 but **5...Ka4**. Then **6. Re3 Bf4 7. Ke1!! Bxe3** would follow, and White is stalemated again.

Penetrating into all the corners and hidden recesses of this study, where the author must look, is a whole school of compositional mastery. We hope that whoever does not mind following this path with us will not regret the time spent. Moreover, the nature of these comments is determined by the fact that they are addressed simultaneously to both solvers and composers.

So, let us take a closer look: how the irrefutability of this two-stalemate finale was secured in a six-piece miniature; by what skillful arrangement of so few pieces, without the slightest additional material, all possible refutations of the study and side-solutions were eliminated. In a study built simply and economically, the unsoundness of many attempts to bypass the author's solution is often motivated by equally "study-like" means, by indirect threats. The interrelation of this small group of "actors" must be substantiated from many sides and checked in every possible way. Any of their "free" moves on an open board - and there are many of them - somehow turns out powerless to break this interdependence; and here every detail, even the smallest, is significant, because without it the whole structure immediately collapses.

Thus, the fork on c3 after *1. Kxe2* is in itself a discovery for constructing the initial position; but the fact that after *1. Rb5+ Kc1* the fork is preserved and pawn e2 still cannot be captured is already a double discovery that creates a linkage; and the fact that after *2. Rc5+* (yet another checking attempt from the fork) *2...Kd1* pawn e2 still cannot be captured, together with the indicated forks, binds the pieces into a single complex as tightly as maximum economy of means requires.

---

Young friends! So you have finished reading the book. Well then, who among you does not want to conquer the Olymp of chess composition? To reach its heights, you need to fulfill the following qualification requirements, set by the Unified All-Union Sports Classification for 1985-1988:

### USSR Grandmaster of Chess Composition

1. take 1st place twice in one section of two consecutive individual USSR championships;
2. take 1st place three times in any number of sections across three consecutive USSR championships;
3. obtain the title of International Grandmaster of Chess Composition and take 1st place once in one section of an individual USSR championship.

### USSR Master of Sport in Chess Composition

1. take places 1st-5th in an individual USSR championship,
2. take 6th place twice in an individual USSR championship,
3. take 1st place in an all-union candidates tournament for Master of Sport,
4. obtain the title of International Master of Chess Composition.

### Candidate for Master of Sport in Chess Composition

1. take places 6th-8th in an individual USSR championship,
2. take places 1st-3rd in an individual championship of a union republic or of the cities of Moscow and Leningrad, finishing ahead of one USSR Master of Sport or three Candidates for Master of Sport - with participation of at least 10 composers of no lower than first category.

### First Category in Chess Composition

1. qualify for participation in the final of an individual USSR championship,
2. take places 1st-4th in an individual championship of a union republic or of the cities of Moscow and Leningrad.

### Second Category in Chess Composition

1. win a prize place in a composing competition for problems and studies,
2. take places 4th-8th on one board in a USSR team championship,
3. take places 1st-3rd on one board in a team championship of a union republic or of the cities of Moscow and Leningrad.

### Third Category in Chess Composition

1. earn a distinction place in a composition competition or another composition event.

### Conditions for Meeting Qualification Requirements

1. When awarding titles and the rank of Candidate for Master in chess composition, creative results based on 50 works (for grandmasters), 25 works (for masters of sport), and 15 works (for candidates for master of sport) must be taken into account.
2. The principle of sequential awarding of categories is observed: III, II, I category, Candidate for Master of Sport, and Master of Sport. An applicant for the title "USSR Grandmaster" must hold the title of International Master of Chess Composition.
3. The titles "USSR Grandmaster of Chess Composition" and "USSR Master of Sport in Chess Composition" are awarded by the Committee for Physical Culture and Sport under the USSR Council of Ministers upon submission by the USSR Chess Federation.
4. The rank "Candidate for Master of Sport in Chess Composition" is awarded by the Committee for Physical Culture and Sport under the USSR Council of Ministers upon submission by the chess federations of the union republics and the cities of Moscow and Leningrad.
5. The first category is awarded by the sports committees of the union republics and of the cities of Moscow and Leningrad upon submission by republican commissions and the composition commissions of Moscow and Leningrad.
6. The second and third categories are awarded by chess-composition commissions of the union republics and of the cities of Moscow and Leningrad.

### Solving Problems and Studies

In competitions for solving problems and studies, as well as positions (with at least ten tasks), the following are awarded:

1. first category - to the three prize-winners of an over-the-board all-union event,
2. second category - for solving all tasks in all-union and republican over-the-board and correspondence competitions,
3. third category - for solving all tasks in any competitions, or sixty percent of tasks in all-union and republican over-the-board and correspondence competitions.
